Chapter 42
Over the Stars

Chapter One

One
	The wolves gathered together in the Sentinel.  The Leviathans, unhinged of a ruler to serve, have agreed to tolerate their presence but not actively help.  Having lost the youngest member almost a year prior, The Order is looking for recruits.  Not new werewolves, but strong hands and swift minds to help aid them in overthrowing the resisting governors.  The Leviathans promise to extend arms if the governors are suppressed, so the conquest is still in its diapers.
	Patricia Germaine sat across from them with Partheus by her side.  She funded most of the events along with a few other good benefactors.  Why she volunteered to be a patron was unknown to the five gathered around the door, but they appreciated it all the same, Partheus in particular.
	Outside the gargantuan structure that once housed a line of kings, an impressive line formed to join their cause.  Most were former Chrissenians and had a moral debt to repay, some would recall the tragedy Patricia had been included in, and the rest were variables.  The Order processed them, asking questions anywhere between what the volunteer's favorite pastime was and if they'd be willing to die for their endeavors.  This was one of many recruitment days.
	After Hodge sent away a pair of novian girls that were too young to serve, a young novian man with hair of neon white and height nearing that of a dwarf stepped up.  Salina interviewed him.  And, despite his impressive size for his race, his nerves were visibly shaken.  It was clear that he wanted to be admitted and that he feared rejection.  He claimed to be a mage in training and cast a fireball, hardly anything more than a spark, at the floor to prove it.  It was better than most could do.  His name was Xavier Pontius.
Two
	Xavier was sent to sit in the yard on the other end of the castle with the rest of those admitted and wait until the recruitment was over to be briefed and sent away until needed.  The people around him made small talk about how brutal the last winter had been and how mild this one was turning out to be.  He'd heard all of that before from a thousand other strangers, but then he caught something important. 
	An elf and a draken stood by the refreshment table on the outside of the canopy tent.  Their heads just high enough that their crests weren't struck by the slanted weak sunlight of this early winter.  The elf turned to the draken after finishing a cup of something and said, "Did you hear about the Dark Forest."
	The draken shook his head, and the green scales of his forehead glimmered blue for a moment.
	"I guess that after the Chrissenians left, things got strange there.  Real strange."
	"How's that?" Asked the draken.
	"People that go in never come back out.  It used to be perfectly safe to travel, just scary as hell.  But now there's something dangerous in there."
	"Or maybe it's so nice that no one wants to leave."
	"Maybe.  But I would think a few of them would come out to tell all of us how great it is.  It doesn't sit right with me."
	"I thought the woods were actually consumed with friendly magicks."
	The elf shrugged. "Not anymore."
	The draken glanced at Xavier and then beyond him.  The novian turned away.  He had forgotten that he was staring and did what he could to make himself look as though his gaze had only been passing over the two and not honed on them.  So occupied was he by this that he was the last to notice Partheus had come to make the day's announcements.
	"Thank you all for coming." Partheus said absently.  He carried a stack of papers with print on their front sides.  On the rears and between lines of text was a gray haze, scrap paper. "Please step forward and get your . . . we'll call it employee papers."
	And they all did.  Some even seemed to be eager, but the rest were nonchalant or touched with nervousness.  Partheus made no reaction to any of the recruits' attitudes, but he had memorized their names and thanked them individually as they went up.  He told them they were free to go home after that, and the waiting area emptied quickly.  He would turn in the direction of the old Leviathan barrack whenever he had the chance.  That was where The Order was and Miss Germaine, and Xavier found himself pondering why Partheus was so anxious to get back to them.
	Then it was Xavier's turn.  The thick stack of copier paper in Sallow's hands had deflated down to a loose bunch of sheets.  Partheus looked down at Xavier, who came only to the elf's thighs, not in the way one looks down at a child (which many did to novians) but in a way that said Xavier was just as big as anyone else.  Xavier reached up to get his copy, but Partheus held the sheets away.
	"Not for you." Said Partheus Sallow, former mayor of Chrissenia, in his most political voice. "I need to speak with you after the rest have come up."
	So Xavier stood to the side while the remaining recruits got their briefing.  They looked at him the way classmates look at another who has been called to the principal's office.  The red sun hung halfway over the horizon, and the golden crystal plating of the Sentinel gleamed in a way that would have been beautiful if it weren't so annoying.  As the night's chill began to sink into Xavier's skin, Partheus finished with the last and rolled up the remaining few papers.
	"Ok, follow me." He began toward the barrack, and Xavier followed wordlessly.
Three
	The barrack had a different feel to it now than it had before, solemn and important.  Maybe that had to do with it being dusk, or perhaps the lack of people made it so.  Either way, Xavier suspected that he was brought here for something important or something insignificant, nothing in between.
	To Xavier's left was the one he recognized as Salina talking to Miss Germaine, an exceedingly wealthy heir to the Germaine fortune.  Though he admired Salina's appearance, he dared not look long.  There was a certain danger in staring at a woman who could pop your skull off your neck with her thumb.  To his right was Arthur, a red headed man with a bizarre accent, and his wife Marianna.  Hodge, their leader, stood in front of the wall where Partheus and Patricia had been sitting earlier.  Nelrene, Hodge's wife, was nowhere to be seen.  His arms were folded over his chest, and though he was not smiling he had an air of happiness around him, of satisfaction.  They looked to Xavier like novians but were as tall as elves.  They were in their human forms.
	"Xavier," Hodge let out and unfolded his arms as if they had been holding in his words. "I hear good things about you." Partheus kept moving as Xavier stopped.  He opened a trashcan and sighed as he dropped the extra briefings into it.  Then he walked to Salina and Patricia and became lost in whatever conversation they were having.
	Xavier looked up at the man. "I haven't done anything yet."
	"I saw that flame earlier today.  That's a great start.  I assume you haven't been practicing magic for very long."
	"Just a month, sir."
	"You shouldn't even be able to spell 'flame' yet."
	"Thank you."
	"And your head doesn't swell at every compliment." Hodge looked over his shoulder as if he were checking a clock. "Keep working on that, got it?  We don't have access to the Leviathans' wizards, but they would be a great help.  You will be a great help." He reached into a pocket and pulled out a white laminated card. "This is a train pass, good for the next year." He handed it to Xavier. "I want you to go to Harkem in a week and meet me there.  Next Solday at dusk I'll be taking you and a group of other promising mages to a sort of training ground.  Bring a few pairs of clothes.  We'll probably be there a couple days."
	"You're a wizard?" Xavier asked as he carefully slipped the card into a pocket of his own.  The thing felt heavier than it really was as if the weight of its meaning was affected by gravity.
	"That's a detail." Hodge said.  There was a pause and he seemed to study the new recruit for a few moments.  Then he waved a hand and yawned for the sake of ceremony. "Well, you're free to go home.  Nothing more for today." As Xavier passed the threshold of the door, Hodge repeated. "Next Solday, the sixteenth, at dusk.  Harkem, remember that."
	Xavier went home.

Four
	Hodge turned to Arthur, and they shared a knowing glance.  Then Hodge said, "There was something off about him.  The way he smelled.  No, the way he didn't smell."
	"He was cloaking it." Arthur replied in his still very much alive British accent. "He's young, maybe twenty.  But novians age well, so I guess he could be thirty."
	"What do you think?"
	"I think he learned magic just for that.  I remember being a boy.  I reeked.  If magic had been available to me, I'd have cloaked my stench too."
	Hodge nodded and rolled his shoulders, not quite a full-blown shrug.
	"You remember how big male deodorant was when we left." Arthur waited for another nod. "It's the same here."
	"He's getting himself into deep waters because of it."
	"But, it isn't as if we're not training him."


Chapter Two

One
	Xavier unlocked the door to his apartment.  The clicking of the tumblers always sounded unsettling to him, as if he could hear the echo of them bounce off an intruder that had beaten him home.
The door swung open, and behold, there were no invaders waiting for him.  What a surprise that was.  He stepped in and closed the door shut behind him.  He left the lights off, and fumbled to find the switch.  He banged a knee into the coat hanger that he never used and flipped the switch as he wretched over to grab the throbbing joint.
He looked at the room and a half that was his home, a barren place he neglected to fill with little trinkets and the like that made such spaces homes.  Or perhaps that was the job of family.  He had neither, but this would have to be a home even so.  He set his keys on the clean coffee table and then sat on the stainless couch.  He beheld the blank wall before him, where once a television sat that a former tenant chose to bring with.
He sat up and walked to the fridge, opening it without thought.  A bottle of nearly spoiled milk sat on one tray.  Below it was a leftover sandwich from the day before.  He grabbed both of these items, thereby emptying the underworked cooling unit, and sat back down on the couch.  He took the foil off the sandwich and looked at the soggy bread and lettuce for a moment before biting in.  There had been a tomato slice the day before, but whatever flavor in it there had been departed overnight.  The ham kept its taste, the horseradish more so.  The milk was on the verge of smelling, and Xavier could detect the way the milk was trying to separate.  But for now it wouldn't make him sick, and he could finish it before it would.
Downstairs someone was yelling at someone named Fran.  It almost seemed to come from the coffee table.
As he finished the leftover sandwich, a fog of exhaustion settled over him.  It took away his appetite more than the vanishing food did, and it made Fran sound annoying.  She should apologize for whatever, or maybe the other should.  Either way, Xavier became too tired to leave the couch and tell them so.  He rolled onto his side and stared at the backrest.  It was red at first with a dark stain.  Then it was maroon with a stain, and a few seconds after that it was all black.
Two
In his dream he was wandering through an opaque wood that could only be the Dark Forest.  He couldn't see anything but somehow managed to avoid the trees.  Behind him he heard rustling, snapping twigs and heavy steps.  He was being followed by something that didn't care about stealth.  He could hear the breathing of many burdened creatures, and the trees seemed to sigh as he passed.  And in the farthest reaches of his hearing, he thought he heard whispering.
He awoke staring at the table.  Sunlight from a window fell on him and with it realization.  He scurried over the couch and looked down over the other side.  There was his alarm clock, the faithfully evil device, never set.  And he was late, not by much just yet, but soon he would be.
Xavier ran to the closet bathroom, pulling his clothes off on the way as quickly as he could.  He turned the water on in the shower and jumped in without waiting for it to warm.  The icy water met his skin, and he began yelping and gasping for breath.  He haphazardly used his shampoo and soap at the same time in hopes that he could perform both cleaning rituals at once.
He stepped out of the shower, and the cool air that struck him felt heated compared to where he'd just come from.  He'd left one towel in the room and was thankful for it.  He dried and used it as a kilt while he ran into the main room and sorted through his pile of clean clothes.  The process took only a few moments, but to him felt like another hour had passed.  On with the pants, on with the shirt, and on with the socks.
He rushed out of the room, locked the door and took a few leaping bounds before spinning around and running back.  Now was not the time to be forgetting to brush one's teeth.  He ran back in and cleaned his ivories.  Out the door again, he locked up and flew downstairs.
It would have to be cold that day.  The mild temperatures and sunshine decided to depart on the morning Xavier forgot a jacket, had no time for it.  The air stiffened his face and burned his lungs.  A cool sensation wrapped itself around his legs, and his teeth began to chatter while he ran.  His moist hair froze over and slicked itself.  It hadn't been this cold since last winter, and no one wanted a repeat of that, Xavier included.
Three
He trotted into Saint Braun and jogged down a hall.  Erica, the head nurse, raised her thick eyebrows and giggled at him as he ran past her station.  She shuffled some papers as another nurse, who seemed unaware of his scrambling, approached her with a clipboard and used it to whisper something to Erica with lips concealed.
Xavier pushed open the door of the janitor's room.  An hour late was bad, but he supposed it could be worse, and he could always work a bit faster to compensate.
Sam Petron, an elf and fellow janitor, was sitting on a metal chair in the corner of the room and sipping on a cup of the awful coffee he made every morning.  Under his dark mustache which brushed the edges of his mug with every drink, was a grin.  He appeared on the verge of spitting out the latest haul of joe.  Next to him was a counter with his coffee pot, a microwave, and a set of cabinet drawers above and a sink below.
He set the cup on the counter and looked up at Xavier.  His grin remained, and cooling coffee hung in his cookie duster.  He stood up, turned back to the cup and finished it off.  He dropped it into the sink and headed for the door.  As his hand turned the flat handle, he turned to Xavier who was now attaching his name tag. "To be young." He said in a voice that was faint and far away, as if it was trying to reach into the past and take his youth back. "Those were the days." He left the room, still talking. "Staying up late.  Never mattered.  Having all the (too faint to hear) on my arms.  It . . ." And the rest, if he was still going on, was too muffled for Xavier to hear.
There was a note on the tag folder addressed to Xavier.  It was bad enough that he was late, but extra work would make this a busy day.
Xavier,
We had an incident this morning when a few kids got ahold of a gurney and rode it around the 3rd floor.  They knocked over some vases by the janitor's closet, spilled whatever food they had by Dr. Walst's office, and chucked up whatever food they already ate in front of the cleric station before we were able to nab them. : ) -Erica
P.S. We need you to clean that up.  Sorry : (
That all sounded about right.  He grabbed a mop bucket from a closet down the hall and went to an elevator.  He pressed the up switch and watched the little electric sign go from 5D to 4D and wondered why in such a well-to-do city there were no service elevators for the maintenance crew.  It stopped at 3D and again at 2D.  Both of these were longer than the previous stops; people were boarding and dropping off.  And then it just stayed at 2 for what seemed like minutes.  Someone was holding up the elevator, probably shooting the breeze and jamming their arm into the space between the doors.
Then the steel box made its descent, the red text changing from 2D to 1D.  There was an affirming beep just before the door slid open and released a greasy ogre in a business suit.  The man's skin was teal, which was a sick color for ogres.  He wore an enormous sweat stained three piece which was all brought together by an orange tie with a green stain below the knot.  He ambled out of the car, reeking of death and surely headed for the E.R.  But instead he clumsily trudged out a door and into the light of day.
Xavier stepped into the elevator and pressed 3.
Four
If Erica was good at anything, it was exaggerating.  The garbage strewn mess he expected was nothing more than a few wrappers and soda cans.  He cleaned these up and looked for the elusive vomit he'd read so much about.  Not finding any, he laid his mop against the wall by the cleric station and let the bucket slip from his hands and drift to a stop.
He walked into Walst's office.  There was a little waiting room outside that couldn't possibly be blander in which an aged novian woman sat with her purse tucked into her lap as if it might contain her heart.  Xavier walked up to the sign in counter and stood on his toes; the girl behind it was shuffling some papers.
"Hello, Carnith." He said even though she did not know him well.  He waited for a reply.
Her deep socketed elven eyes looked at him levelly, and she smiled and leaned forward. "How can I help you," There was a pause.  It was brief, but Xavier knew it was because she'd forgotten his name, or at least it slipped by for a moment. "Xavier?"
"I don't know if you were here for it or not, but I guess some boys caused a ruckus earlier." He dropped from his toes and popped his ankles.  For a moment, Carnith was out of sight and all that could be seen was the white counter side and the junk stuck to its underside.  He raised up again, unaware that he was smiling. "I came to clean it up, but my 'briefing' made it sound a lot worse.  Did someone already start on it?  And if one of them lost their breakfast, I should probably find it before we break health codes."
"I don't think anyone beat you to it." Her hands slipped back and she began paper clipping little bundles of documents together.  Walst burst through the door next to her and asked for a 'Dara Henner.' The novian woman looked dazedly at the doctor and then went to him and was lost behind the door.  Carnith went on, "Doctor Walst took care of the vomit himself.  The kid used a trash can, and he went out, tied the bag, and told one of them to throw it in the bin out back when Security took them away.  You might have to put a new bag in, but otherwise it's done."
Xavier nodded, and his feet decided that that made him feel heavier.  He grunted and concealed it by clearing his throat. "Thanks Carnith.  And tell Walst I thank him as well."
"Don't worry, and I'll be sure to."
He doubted she would actually do that, but that wasn't his responsibility.  Xavier went back into the hall.  His calves wanted to cramp, but thankfully the tightening sensation passed.  He checked the garbage cans, and sure enough one of them was empty.  He made sure no one tossed their coffee cups in it or gum.  Seeing it at least look clean, he took a bag from a holster on the side of his mop bucket, unfolded it and aired it out.  On his toes again he stretched the bag over the metal cylinder's rim.
He looked left and right and couldn't see any more messes.  He rubbed the space between his eyebrows and walked toward the window.  The glass was thick but almost as transparent as a vacuum.  Outside he could see the ogre from before hurling in a bush on the other side of the street, and a woman next to him seemed to be coaxing him to the hospital.  There was a school a few blocks beyond them, and Xavier could see a few of the children outside for recess.  And beyond that was a sparse stretch of suburb and then wilderness.
He heard footsteps behind a corner and picked up his mop.  Best not to look lazy.  He made for the elevator, as it was his turn today to wipe the basement floor.  It was almost always deserted, so he enjoyed the time it gave him to think unimpeded.  The steps closed in and changed from echo to direct.  Xavier turned to throw a smile at whomever might be wandering the hall that morning.  When he saw her, his hands coated themselves in a thin layer of sweat, his legs locked up, and his ears suddenly heard the pounding of his heart, a deliberate and conking thing.
Five
Salina stared at him as if she had been watching him through the wall, and he supposed she might have.  Her clothes were much too large for her and helped accentuate her beauty about as much as a car accident helps tune up an engine.  But her face was exquisite, and it locked Xavier in place.  He prepared to hand back his train pass, remembered that he left it at home, and then scrambled through his inner working to find a way to say that best.
She stood next to him and leaned down.  Xavier could see the outline of her chest and pulled his eyes up as quickly as he could; he had to remember the skull popping.  His heart began to beat in arrhythmic patterns.
Then a flood of soothing senses washed over him, and Xavier could no longer understand why he was getting so worked up.  His smile broadened through great effort, and he gave her a recognizing nod.  That wasn't the right one, he would later think.  This wasn't seeing an acquaintance pass you on the street.  There was business here.
Maybe she saw his discomfort, because she straightened her back after his nod.  Though she herself didn't appear very pleased with looking so far down on someone. "Pontius was it?" She crossed her arms.
He said yes in his mind, but his lips didn't move.  He bit whatever nerve was holding his tongue with a quick series of "man up" phrases that passed through him. "Yes." He said out loud. "My first name is Xavier.  Whichever you want to use.  Not really fond of my last name." His eyes darted to the right like a convict's.  He didn't want to look this shaken.
"How long have you been practicing magic?  I don't remember what Hodge said."
The change in subject brought his eyes back to hers. "A month.  Closer to six weeks."
"Most people take a while before they can really begin to use it.  You were already able to conjure up fire." Sunlight bounced from her green eyes, and they shone with curiosity.
He pulled his hands up like a man looking at the bloodstains he's given himself, inspected them, and let them drop to his sides. "A spark is hardly a fire."
"And a snowflake is hardly the Arctic." She saw his head cock to the side. "Or a harsh winter, if you're more familiar with that.  And I imagine you have to be.  But it's a part of it, the first block." She squatted down in front of him, resting her arms on her knees and observing his face. "You must have had, or do have, a wonderful teacher." Her eyes narrowed until there was nothing but green and a speck of black in the center. "Which is odd considering that you need to be in the military or have an expensive license to learn magic legally."
Xavier's left hand found his sternum while the other went up. "I've been mostly self-taught." His hands returned to his sides. "And there's no law or license that says you can't self-teach magic." He felt her suspicion and wished it away.  Xavier didn't want Salina to distrust him.  He liked her, and he wanted her to at the very least consider him a friend, but this conversation was beginning to feel most unfriendly. "I . . . I guess I'm just picking it up fast, then.  I don't know what else to say."
The elevator door opened and he stepped in.  He pressed the plastic circle with a B in the center and watched the letter light up red.  He shrugged at Salina in the hall, and then she too entered the car.  His heart sank when she did.  Xavier supposed there were men that would kill to be alone with her, but as it was she was the last person he wanted to be alone with.

Six
They began to descend, and the screen above the floor panel changed from 3D to 2D and so on.  At both the second and first floor, Xavier hoped the door would open and she'd get off.  But it kept on smoothly rolling to the basement.  Salina was silent and faced forward, but he could feel her staring with her thoughts.  He wondered if a hospital was safe enough for him.
The door opened.  Xavier held the door subconsciously as Salina followed behind.  The lights were off, and flicking them on revealed the dust and grime the other janitors had left.  He sighed, Dante clearly hadn't cleaned the place the week before.  More work for him, and more time to spend with the werewolf.
He found the maintenance closet and pulled out all the tools he would need.  Salina propped herself against a pillar and crossed her arms.  Her eyes drilled into his back, and her silence was eerie.  Xavier went on with the cleaning as if she were nothing more than a cardboard cutout.  Hopefully she wouldn't decide to plaster the wall with his bones.  He didn't think she was unhappy exactly, so maybe it wouldn't come to that.
Seven
He began on a storage room that had been filled with metal folding chairs since time immemorial.  The room was dusty and covered in webs, not cleaned since his last turn a month ago.  He smelled something pungent, stale beer.  Someone had fun in this room but didn't bother to clean it up.
Xavier turned the light switch on, but the room stayed unlit.  A chair fell with a harsh clang, the sound of scuffling feet, and the hair on his neck stood on end.  To him he felt like he was in a bad horror film.  The room he shouldn't enter was right there, but like all those paid actor morons he stepped inside.  At best, it was a rat.  At worst, at least Salina was there.
His eyes began adjusting, and he saw the outlines of all the chairs and what wasn't a chair.  Curled up facing the wall was a figure draped in a blanket sewn together from old jackets.  It began breathing heavily as Xavier approached.  Long dark hair matted with mud fell in straggles down its back.
"Hey!" Xavier said, wishing he'd just called Security instead. "This room is restricted to employees." And he knew the man, or woman, whatever it was knew that.  Had to say it anyway, because that's just what you're supposed to do. "No parties and no free boarding."  The slumped breathing pile didn't budge, so Xavier followed the footnotes of any B horror film character and went to grab the shoulder.  Now the thing revealed itself to be an elf, though with its odor and general lack of hygiene, it might have been better described as an overgrown rat.  It was on Xavier in a flash and began bludgeoning him with an empty bottle, all the while screaming "I ain't going!" even though it came out "Ahh in geen!"
There was a painful thump on his head, and Xavier's vision departed.  He could feel his mind and eyes struggling to cling onto sight, but it was like trying to fight gravity.  He simply fell into darkness.

Chapter Three

One
He woke up in a bed.  Something that felt like gauze was wrapped around his head, but thankfully they hadn't stripped him down and thrown a ridiculous gown on.  A plastic brace was on his nose and made him breathe with a whine, so he switched over to mouth.
"I don't understand men's pride." Salina said to no one. "Why you can't just ask for a woman's help when you need it is beyond me, and I guess it always will be."
"You got him then?" It hurt his nose to talk, but he ignored it.
"You're a half pint, and you're no grand wizard yet.  Were you planning on handling that with your brute strength?"
He sighed and was surprised that his nose didn't object. "We both know I wasn't thinking at all.  That's how people get into trouble." He tried to tap his temple, but feeling the wrap, he rubbed it instead.
"I'm sorry for calling you a half pint.  Now that I think of it, that's kind of racist.  Still though, if I hadn't been there."
"Most of my people are quarter pints.  Half pint is accurate.  And thanks.  I think if we took a tally though, I'd still be the one owing you."
The subject seemed to bore her, and she moved on. "Since you're just relaxing, let's talk."
He objected on the inside but was in no position to refuse her. "You have the floor, just tell me when to step in." He consented.  And from the corner of his eye, he thought he saw a smile.
Two
They talked about The Order and where things were headed with them.  He asked her about her home, and she his.  She went into almost no details about her home other than that it was another world, one filled with people like her and the other werewolves.  He described his hometown of Bellshire, and by the time he was done explaining that alone, Salina knew more about him than all his coworkers.
Nurses came in and out, checking his cognitive function and asking about the attack.  Time moved on, and he became transfixed.  After some time, Erica walked in with hands nearly glued to her face in horror.  She told Xavier that she had him signed out and that he could take the rest of the week off.  He only asked how the elf had managed to stay in the basement so long, and that, she did not answer.
He was released, and Salina went her own way.  He watched her walk an opposite direction once they got to pavement.  She walked up to something long and black which was embedded between two cement slabs.  Pulling it out with ease, Xavier realized it was a sword.
As he walked home, he thought about her visit, almost preeminently reminisced over it.  Was she looking after him?  He doubted that very much.  More than likely it had something to do with him being one of the mages in training.  It only made sense that she would go around and scrutinize them.  Learning magic was no common task.  Keeping an eye on them couldn't hurt.  In his case it was saving.	
Three
Xavier sat on his couch and stared unhappily at the bare table before him.  His stomach hollowed out and took to scolding him about it.  He turned and looked at the fridge, no food there.  He walked back to the door.  He didn't feel like going out, and his head began throbbing.  He took one look at the coat hanger, an urge to smash it welling up, and left.
The alarm didn't go off that next morning, but he was glad for that this time around.  The bare table greeted him a good morning.  He felt eyes and isolation smother him and in an instant was convinced he was a part of a lab experiment.  Then the wakeful nonsense thoughts vanished, and he stretched and rubbed the pixie dust out of his eyes.
If he had the rest of the week to get down to Harkem, he was going to do so moseying.  He gathered up some of his clean clothes and stowed them into a backpack.  He didn't own a suitcase or carry-on, and they'd be too bulky for him anyway.
Four
Traffic was light on Main Street, and he only got his feet stepped on twice as he walked to the station.  Behind him was the golden bastion, but it was of no concern today.  The train pass in his back pocket felt weighty like an extra wallet.
He jumped in line at the station.  He wanted to board a train for Redora, an old residence of Cecil Fauden, the only werewolf he was aware of who had the unique experience of dying.  As he waited for those ahead to get themselves situated on the locomotive, a wind from the north started up and pushed against the bag strapped to his back.  It flowed over the pack and whirled around his legs.  Above it pushed the hair at the base of his skull and pushed icicles into his spine.  Winter was coming.
The train slowed when it came to the Bishop tunnel.  A year beforehand there had nearly been a derailment when some of the mutants in the tunnel were mowed down by a passenger train.  Since then, the Leviathans had come and exterminated the vile things.  But news reports claimed that the soldiers cleared out far less than expected and that the grotesque things may have migrated.
The others slid blinds over their windows to hide their faces from the mutants, even though they'd been cleared.  It was almost tradition among riders to do this, and so they went on doing so every time the tunnel came up.  Xavier kept his up, trying to fight the darkness to catch a glimpse at anything he could, but light from the cab kept his eyes from dilating.
A stewardess walked down the aisle with a drink tray.  The bottom legs of the steel server came out wider than the top tray, and seeing the brown surface of a coffee shake, Xavier understood why.  The woman was an ogre.  She was in the two hundred to two hundred and fifty pound range, so she was rather petit.  Still though, her burly arms struggled to fit into the sleeves of her uniform, and her slacks seemed to be filled with rigid watermelons.  A male ogre two seat in front of Xavier grabbed her ass as she passed him.  Xavier watched with amusement as she blushed, gasped, and then slapped the cheek seeker with a zealous hand.  The man said something about it only being a compliment, but even he couldn't believe that completely and shut up after.  Xavier lowered his head and turned toward the window, concealing his grin and holding in a chuckle as she walked by.
Light flooded in from the windows, and the cab's artificials blinked out.  A tug pulled everyone toward the back, but it was only slight and the iron horse fastened its pace.
He could see foothills pop up under the actual mountains.  A lightly forested area and a trailed knoll stood out among the rest of the less outstanding area.  Xavier wasn't quite sure, but he thought he spotted two little specks on the hillside that might have been people.  He knew it was a bit early to be going to the point, so he decided they must be having a picnic.  Or someone is getting murdered, or those are only tree stumps.  You haven't a clue which.  And thus sayeth the all-knowing and ever critical subconscious.
Five
Compared to Fraushein, Redora might have been deserted.  The streets weren't paved with red brick but with dirt compacted by years of walking on.  Nothing was made of crystal or reflective glass, or even steel.  Wood and brick composed all the buildings, and it was as if the town lived a hundred years in the past.  The sidewalks were made wooden planks, and more than a few of these had a visit from termites.  There were vacant buildings with boarded up doors and cobwebs in the windows.  The people meandered about without much concern or rush, and in that regard the place was divinely tranquil.
Xavier found a store shortly after walking into the town proper.  His stomach was ready for round two from the previous night and demanded he go in and buy something to tide him over.  He pushed the double door open as a bell chimed over his head.
Inside was a wild assortment of products.  To his right along the wall was a line of cooling units with everything from soda and water to milk, cheese, and pepperoni.  Beyond that was a glass sneeze guard and what looked like a deli/bakery.  At the end of every aisle was a plastic barrel painted to look wooden, a few empty, a few full of red or blue plastic golf clubs or flying discs.  There were snack lanes and cleaning ones beyond all that, but Xavier didn't bother to venture that far.
He walked into a checkout lane and picked out a bag of beef jerky from the lane's shelf.  Below the jerky was a sign that read "AS SEEN ON TV!" with an array of hoaxes scattered underneath that would never perform like they did on their commercials.  He put the bag on the counter, and a red haired novian girl rang it up and bagged it.
Her blazing eyebrows furrowed at him, and she asked, "Are you visiting?"
Xavier nodded.
She reached under the counter and withdrew a figurine of an elf heighted novian with the sword Salina carried on his back. "Did you know Cecil Fauden used to work at this very store?" Xavier had a feeling that that was supposed to come out in a more exciting tone than it did. "In fact, he was the assistant manager!" Some effort into that one. "It makes a great souvenir for only seven troths."
"No thanks." He pulled the leather trifold free of his back pocket. "The jerky will do for now."
She tossed the trinket unceremoniously under the counter and the two of them finished the transaction.  Xavier looked behind and noticed that no line was going to form for some time.  He opened the jerky, took a bite and looked at the cashier. "So I take it he's well known here?"
She looked over him, perhaps hoping that line would begin. "Mmhmm." She coughed. "Excuse me." She began swiveling back and forth on her stool. "A hometown boy.  I mean, he was only here for a year or two I guess.  I wasn't old enough to work back then, so I wasn't up on rumors.  But I guess he sort of just showed up one day.  Cornelius said he came from the grasslands.  Beyond that it's just desert.  Everyone liked him.  And then one day he left on a vacation, and . . . well," She shrugged, "was gone just as abruptly as he came." The girl smiled and laughed through her nose. "It was kind of comical though, because Cornelius- oh I should mention that he's the owner."
"I got that."
"Apparently there was a big shipment of potato chips.  And without Cecil around, he was in a panic.  No one knew where he went off to until the news reports talked about what happened at the Capitol."  Someone with a cart came up behind Xavier, and he nodded to the girl and left.
Six
When he stepped on the sidewalk, Xavier gazed northward toward Bishop.  On the other side of that mountain was cold air, but here winter still seemed so very far away.  Another bite of jerky, and he kicked up the dusty earth with one sneakered foot.  A puff of dust rose in a wedge out from under him before drifting south in whatever little current there was.  Winter had not forgotten Redora.
By the time he found a motel, his bag of jerky had shrunken to an airy plastic bag with an oxygen eater tucked inside.  He ditched it by the front door of "The Rest Stop" in a garbage can that he felt obligated to change.
The place had twenty four rooms, or at least it had twenty four windows to make him think that.  The outside was painted white, chipped in spots to reveal wood, and smelled of pine.  Above the front door was a wooden sign with metal letters embedded within which made up the name.  The sign faced forward, and the iron of it was foaming red with rust.  Most of the shingles were still in place, but here and there were disappearances in the rain protectors.  The gutters broke away from roof on the right side and sank to the ground out of its own weight.  Xavier stepped inside to the sound of another chiming bell.
A dwarven man with hair as fiery red as a novian's sat behind a plywood counter on a metal stool with a skeletal back support.  He was stroking his thick beard and staring happily at the wall opposite of him where a large mirror hung.  Behind him was a wall of corkboard with little pins stuck into it and numbered keys hanging from these.  Beside this was a tin sign which read "We won't refuse service to anyone unless (insert crackpot reason here)."
Xavier took a few steps toward the counter, and the floorboards creaked.  The dwarf turned to Xavier. "Need room and board?  If so, you've come to the right place.  Wrong place if ya want entertained." His voice was like that of a thirteen year old boy's who has just begun his long and awkward journey to being a man; it was unfitting.
"Just for the night." He replied after processing the hairy child in front of him.
Seven
When he had his things settled in room nine, his stomach had forgotten the beef jerky.  Tension built up between his temples, as a hunger headache began to coalesce.  Xavier was exhausted from the traveling, but he left the motel and found a diner called Steaks For Goodness Sakes.
He stepped into the coffee stained soul of a brand of restaurant that was dying out.  In Redora, it belonged just as much as a spine belongs in a back.  Red leather booths were lined along the windowed front wall and similar style barstools all the way up the long counter.  The tables were checkered with black and white diamonds.  Behind the counter was a glassless window that saw into the kitchen.  A turn wheel with order slips hung down from the opening, but no one was in to be waited on except for a shepherd canid sitting at the far end of the counter listlessly eating the last of his fries.  An ogress behind the bar with a cleaning rag on her shoulder had her elbows resting on the countertop with head nestled in palms.  She was looking at the canid and having some sort of lighthearted conversation.  As she spoke, her haggard arms shifted and made her seem like an ancient raisin with grey skin.
Xavier ambled up onto a stool with only a slight struggle.  The steel under him moaned to do its job, and the ogress turned to him and seamlessly paused her chat with the other patron.  There was a chest pocket on her shirt from which she withdrew a pad and pen. "What can I getchya, hun?" Her voice was as tired as her body.
"A cheeseburger and . . . a water."
"Anything ta go with that?"
It had been some time since Xavier had gone out and gotten a burger instead of scrounging on bland sandwiches.  He knew what a place like this served, but it took him a moment even so to consider. "Some onion rings.  And if it isn't too much trouble, throw some bacon on that burger."
She smiled, causing her face to cave in and fold wrinkles over more wrinkles. "That sounds more like it.  Joey loves makin' them burgers sizzle with pig hide." She clicked the pen's font and clipped it to her shirt pocket. "Anything else?"
Xavier bit his lip to make it appear as though he might want more, even though he wouldn't fathom it. "No, that should be it." The ogress replaced her notepad, tearing off the page with Xavier's order on it and stamping it down on the ledge of the kitchen window.  An ogre that might have been her brother stepped forth from behind a pantry, took one careful look at the slip and walked away.
Xavier turned his head and made a sweep of the diner.  As his line of sight passed over the canid, their eyes met and the man turned away.  He and the ogress went back to small talk, while Xavier's eyes rested on the dusty street beyond the windows.
The canid stood up and laid his payment on the counter.  He'd been sitting on a grey hooded jacket and slung it over his shoulder.  He nodded to the ogress and walked out the door.  The metal of it scraped against the cement floor for an instant as it closed shut behind him.  Xavier heard the wind while the door was open and knew it had picked up.
"So I haven't seen you around town before.  Traveling, or am I just going senile?" The sound of her voice seemed far off, like she was talking to him from the general store.
He looked up at her and let the examining of the backs of his hand go for another time. "Oh I'm," High pitched and strained, he cleared his throat again, "I'm going to Harkem."
"Well, that's nice.  Do you have family down there?"
He wanted to say he was seeing friends, but he thought it would sound fake. "Yes." He said uncomfortably and nodded.  He dug through his mind to find a subject to change to and found one he was fairly certain would grab her attention. "So I heard Cecil Fauden used to live here.  Did he ever eat here?  The store seems to be using him as a tourist attraction."
"Eh," Her throat rattled.  She had been a smoker at some point in her life, still perhaps. "Because he did work there, they can squeeze that out." She took the rag from her shoulder and laid it under the counter somewhere. "Saw him pass by a few times, but no, he never came in here.  I don't think he ever ate, which makes sense I suppose considering what he was."
Xavier mulled this for a few seconds.  Then replied, "I would never know the difference.  You guys could cash in on it.  They tell good stories about him, and it won't be long before they make a movie out of it."
Her jaw shifted, and it sounded as if she were grinding bits of gravel between her gums. "Dahzir was an oppressor.  So I can see why he's a tale." She peered out the window into the street.  A group of adolescent elves were walking up the road toward the store. "Tourists will believe anything if it's sold to them right.  They're lookin' to escape as it is, and adding a few stints to a story flies by them.  But ya can't fool the hometown fans, which are our reliable bread and butter.  Fickle tourists can't keep us runnin' in the winter."
He looked back down at his hands.  Something about her honesty made him feel guilty looking at her face. "Well, I'm sure the town as a whole gets to benefit anyway." Hands folded over one another. "But-
Xavier stopped himself as the door opened.  Two dwarves and their child came in.  The tot was clinging to her mother who was staring impatiently at the father.
The ogress looked at the consternated man by the door. "You guys can seat yerselves wherever.  I'll be right there." A plate was set in the window with Xavier's food.  She spun around, shedding thirty years off her age, and back again with the plate of food.  She put it in front of the white haired novian, one shade away from a whisper said "Enjoy" and walked around the counter to the family that had found a booth.
He ate his food and left a tip.  The ogress hovered around the family the entire time he ate and calmly kept the three from unraveling from whatever stress they seemed to be bearing.  A peacekeeper if he'd ever seen one.  The world needed more of them, and she needed tipped for those onion rings.
Eight
Xavier understood why the canid had been carrying a jacket.  It had been warm enough to get by with a T-shirt when he came in, but as the northerly wind passed over his face his breath escaped in a thick mist.  It would've been nice to at least have a natural coat of fur and not weigh as much as a few bowling balls.  The cold was bitter and sinking into his skin fast.  He felt the chilled surface of his jeans press against his legs while the base of his spine seemed to fill with ice water.  His teeth chattered together for a moment, and he forced his jaws shut so that he wouldn't chip a tooth.
He looked up at the mountains to the north.  Over Bishop hung a front of dark clouds, and in the west the sun was settling into bed.  As if acknowledging that it had been seen, the cloud wall lurched over the mountain and sent a rushing gale down the street.  He saw one or two other pedestrians zip up their coats and continue on unimpeded.  Xavier's fingers began tingling.  He leaned forward a bit; his bowels were laden with frost somehow.  It suddenly occurred to him that he might die of hypothermia just by walking from the diner back to his room.  The thought of it was unappealing and ironically made him feel warmer.
Nine
His hand tremored like it was filled with firecrackers.  The number tag that hung from the key bounced around with fury and slapped the sides of his trembling hand.  It went into the keyhole, and the rattling stopped.  As Xavier opened the door and felt the slightly warmer and calmer air hit his hand and forearm, he turned and saw snowflakes coming down.
He closed the door behind him, hoping deep inside that he could lock the cold outside.  He walked to the wall beside the bed.  There were radiator pipes with chipping paint.  They were warm, so he put his hands on them.  When those were warm, he leaned against it and looked at the stained bed sheets.  Something crawled against the wall by the door and slipped underneath.  Good, let winter deal with you and not me.  He slumped down.  The pipes weren't getting any hotter, and he supposed it would cost something to do so anyway.  And the bed didn't look all that inviting either, so this would do.  So long as they kept the heat running through the night.
Ten
After he fell asleep Xavier visited the Dark Forest.  He was standing in a clearing where sunshine could pierce the thick dark.  How deep into the wood he was, he hadn't the faintest idea.  He knew that it was that particular forest, knew it the way a person knows when they're being watched.
The light of the sun made a circle around him in that clearing.  He looked into the woods beyond, but the light was unable to even shine upon the bark of the first trees.  Then he heard the last thing he wanted to hear, unhappy life.  He saw the eyes of creatures watching him from within the darkness.  The sun did not reach them.  It was as if their eyes made their own light.  As he stood there, the eyes grew in number and in shape.  He was surrounded by the very worst kind of monsters, the unseen ones.  The sun steadily went on setting, the light shrinking and eyes drawing closer.
He could not stay there, and so he ran into the darkness.  Somehow he avoided the trees which could not be seen.  Something slimy grabbed for his leg but could not find purchase.  It must have had teeth or claws, because he felt his heated blood run down his ankle.  Branches snapped behind him as growling things tried to keep pace.  He dared not look back into their eyes for fear that they might lock onto his soul.
He saw a pale light ahead of him.  It was dusty like the moons' and covered a great bit of the expanse ahead.  A hope welled up within him that he might have reached the end of the forest, but it sank when he also considered that night might have come.  The creatures would keep following him if that was so.
His heart stopped for a moment of shock, and he clutched his chest before it began beating again.  The light came from another opening, and it was indeed nighttime.  But the forest went on, and in the open expanse was a great stone fortress.  He ran to its open gates and hoped the mutants would not follow him in, that perhaps the faint light of Orion and Artemis would be enough to keep them at bay.  To his great pleasure, he could no longer hear them chasing.
Xavier leaned against a wall and began catching his breath in big, burning gulps of air.  The wall he leaned on was cold, but the air against his skin was without temperature.  He felt the remnant memories of those that had built this place and who had once lived there, and maybe it was those memories that kept the demons away.  He turned his back to the wall now that his breath had been captured.  It was time to let his thoughts catch up.
Then he heard a great roar like that of a lion and a grizzly bear in unison.  He looked into the town square where a stone statue of a man whose arms had fallen off stood and watched the vacant place.  Standing on the statue's head was Cecil Fauden transformed into his werewolf shape.  The blue crystal greaves he'd once worn and were now on display in a museum wrapped around his legs.  His torso was mostly exposed and what wasn't had a covering of a torn hooded jacket.  He was just as the people of Redora said he had been when he first came to their town.  On his back was a thing he hadn't acquired until much later, the Baneblade sword.  Xavier couldn't see the intricate patterns of the sword in the dark, but he remembered it.  The werewolf's fur was absolute black in the dark.  The blue of his fur though was as brilliant as fire.  His eyes reflected only a deep blue that to Xavier looked like specks at the end of a horizon.  Claws like knives hung at the end of Cecil's hands, darker than the night.
The werewolf let loose another terrible roar, and Xavier could hear the mutants outside the wall retreat into the forest.  Cecil gazed down from his perch into Xavier's eyes.  The novian suddenly felt an anchor of terror attach to his ribs, and for no reason that he could think of, he became maddeningly horrified of the werewolf.

Chapter Four

One
The side of the bed greeted Xavier with an off-color splotch that might have been left by a greasy hand.  The radiator was still on, but it hadn't burned a hole in his back.  He pulled his phone from the right pocket of his jeans.  It told him in not so many words and more numbers that it was time to go.  No calls, no texts, good.  He left it on silent and grabbed his things.
"How'd you get that scrape on your forehead?" Asked the man child, breaking the clean escape Xavier would have liked.
"Hit over the head with a bottle.  Could've been a lot worse." Xavier tried handing the room key to the dwarf, who merely stared at the red streak just center of Xavier's left temple.  He put the key on the counter instead. "There you go." As he walked out the door, the dwarf asked more questions.
The streets were damp like ice water.  The snow didn't lay on the ground from the night before, but a thick padding of frost took its place.  Xavier could see his breath again, and the mist of it clung to his face like a cold veil.  His feet crunched the frost under him and made him sound heavier than he really was.  Everyone he passed wore a coat or a jacket, and he began envying their warmth.  If he had a better grasp of magic, he could heat the air around him with it.  Unfortunately, such advanced spells as that were still a mystery to him.  While he walked back to the station, he pondered the idea of warmth and heat.  He needed the practice and was pleased to see a spark or two shoot from one of his fingertips every few steps.  It wasn't much, but it was getting better.
Two
He looked out the window by his seat and watched the mountains and foothills fade away into long green flats and amber expanses.  He watched these shrink down into sparse shrubs and barren dirt.  The wasteland appeared to go on forever, but he knew that somewhere beyond the horizon was an ocean.  He thought the land here would be astonished to know something like that.
The stewardess from the other day walked down the hall again, unimpeded by wandering hands.  She handed glasses to a couple behind Xavier.  They made a remark about all the desert making one thirsty.  The stewardess feigned amusement, but it was a poor impression.  The wife giggled at the husband's remark, but this too felt fake.  Xavier had to wonder what he had going for him if his humor was so dry.  Looking at the stretches of nothing on the other side of the glass divide, he realized what he had just thought was on level with the man's joke.
The train slowed to a halt, the expanse the novian gazed upon stopping with it.  The platform was on the other side of the locomotive, and if a conductor wasn't saying so, he could've been fooled to believe that they were still leagues from any sort of settlement.
Three
If Redora had been a ghost town, then Harkem was just one step up from a family ranch.  The street was thick with sand and dust, no sidewalks.  There were a total of a dozen buildings, all wood and all suffering from dry rot.  The streets were empty, save for one other, an elf who had gotten off the train with Xavier.  The elf seemed to know the area and went straight into a saloon in the middle of the only street.  And there he was left alone.  
He still had two days before Solday.  He knew he needed to be in town by dusk then, but he couldn't remember where Hodge had told him to go to specifically.  He walked to the saloon.  Maybe someone in there could point him in the right direction.
Swinging double doors that he could nearly walk under separated him from the interior.  He pushed them open and stepped in.  The first thing he thought was that the owner had decided to varnish the entire place with whiskey.  The second was that everyone had their eyes on him, which would only be a grand total of five people.  The second was true, although their attention to him quickly died.  The elf's held on a bit longer, but he returned to talking with a scruffy cowpoke draken.  The color of the day (year probably even) was tan.  Brown if you were feeling a bit wild, as one draken with muddied jeans appeared to be.  They were all lizard folk, except for the elf and Xavier of course.  Their scales were dark yellow.  One had patches of black around her neck and another had some orange at the crest of his forehead.  But they all looked similar, and Xavier was certain that the town was indeed family owned.
He was about to walk up to the bar, was halfway there in fact, when two more came through the door.  Soon the place would be at max capacity.  He saw all the heads turn to the new couple, smiling and sending nods.  Xavier, compelled by everyone's recognition, turned as well.
There was Patricia and Partheus standing with their backs to the sunlight beaming in from the doors.  Their attire was like that of the townsfolk, simple though they weren't yet covered in a fine layer or two of dust.  He noted to himself that he'd never seen a wolf canid dress so plainly and didn't think he'd ever see it again.  Partheus nodded back to the patrons, and then he focused on Xavier as if he had been following the novian.  He nodded again.
They walked past him and to the counter.  As Partheus engaged the man behind the bar, Xavier felt as if he were fading out of existence.  An attention shift can do that to a person.  But he was in the right place, so that reassured him.  He shuffled his feet around, unsure what to do with himself, when Patricia and Partheus turned around and made for the door.  They stopped next to him and Patricia motioned to him with her head.  Xavier followed, feeling for the first time in a long while that he was the child following Mother and Father.  He didn't like it.
Four
"You got here early.  Maybe you can help us set up." Partheus said as he checked the street for traffic.  There was none, of course.
"I don't see why not." Replied the novian.
"Don't you work at Saint Braun's?" Patricia now.  Her voice was soft and strong, and her fur hid her age.  Xavier wasn't sure if he was being questioned by a young heiress or by an old queen. "I know that they're always busy, being where they are.  Surprised you could get weekdays off."
Xavier was beside the elf now.  Following behind made him feel shorter.  He looked up to both of them and pointed at the healing red laceration. "I got attacked on company property by an unhappy . . ." He wanted to say moron, ". . . customer.  They gave me the rest of the week off, an overreaction really."  And then he wanted to ask why Salina hadn't told them, but he kept his trap shut.
At the end of the lane they stopped by a rundown shack that was far enough from any of the main buildings that it appeared to be its own entity.  Partheus went to the door and walked in.  Patricia stayed outside with Xavier.  "The last time I visited Braun, I was a patient."
"Anything serious?"
"I was at an anti-execution rally in front of the Sentinel.  Lots of things were going on, and we weren't the only rally group there.  It was a little over a year ago, so Dahzir was still running the whole mess." She bent her knees a moment as if they might lock up if she didn't.  Her gray tail swished in reflex to this. "He came out and sent a lightning bolt through the crowd.  I was stunned along with two others.  Festen and Desten Ambrose, elf brothers.  They were in my rally group.  Everyone else was either dead or had just enough in them to get away.  I imagine a few were fine and just ran with tails between their legs." Her hand went to her mouth as if a bee had stung her on the nose. "I'm sorry for the language."
He shrugged.  He honestly didn't understand what she was referring to.
She went on, "And I guess Cecil was just passing through.  I know now that he was there for The Order, but we were still lucky to have him be there when he was.  Dahzir ordered the guards to dispose of the bodies; I heard him say it.  Even if they were still alive!"
"I can understand why you would be protesting."
"Thank you!" A young heiress was the voice now. "He came up, picked all three of us up off the ground and started walking away.  I was just conscious enough to tell him where to go.  He didn't seem to know the capitol very well.  When he set us down in the hospital, I was able to get a good look at him.  And I'll tell you, I thought the electricity was making me hallucinate."  
"There he was, the largest novian I'd ever seen.  Although, I guess he wasn't actually a novian.  And on top of that, if that wasn't just a miracle from the rabbit hat, I see that he had been carrying two other people.  I might have believed it if he had been an ogre, but I started to assume he was either an angel, or again that my mind was fried." She squinted, perhaps trying to get a better view of her memory.  "So when they asked me about who brought the brothers and myself away from the incident, I told them that I couldn't remember.  For all I know, that whole thing might have been a fabrication.  Though, I kind of doubt it.  Especially since the head nurse wouldn't stop going on about the tall stranger who brought us in.  She seemed to believe there was something between he and I."
"That's Erica.  She's an excitable one, that's for sure." He mused and then felt guilt for not commenting on the more important part of what he'd just heard. "That sounds like quite the ordeal.  Good thing he was there when he was."
Five
Partheus stepped out of the shack, arms loaded with wooden boards.  One of them fell as he walked into the open space between the desert and the town. "You can help, if you'd like." Almost condescending but then, "It's nothing official.  No top secret cargo in there.  Arthur should be doing this and not an old man like me." He dropped the rest of the wood into a little pile. "But he's off scouting."
Xavier's bowels twisted, and he was sure he would cripple over.  But the pain left as it had come, and he could only hope it would not return again until he retired for the day, maybe something he ate.  He nodded at the elf and followed him into the little shed.
Inside against either wall were separate stacks of wooden boards and steel poles.  The floor was dirt, and the walls ran down into it.  Fist sized holes in the ceiling let the furious sun puncture the darkness in spots.  Partheus grabbed another armload of boards, and Xavier grabbed what he could, which was almost as much as the elf.
They made trips until the contents of the shed were depleted.  Patricia stood away and made swipes at the screen of her phone.  She squeezed the side of the device and deposited it into a pocket. "Grandor will be on his way over in a few minutes, and Hodge said he should be here tonight."
Partheus looked at the large mound of seemingly useless material.  His blonde hair was blinding in the desert sun. "Should I meet him at the station?  Not like he needs an escort."
"No," She replied, "he said the trains are too slow and that he'll be footing it."
Six
A cloud of dust appeared before the pile of wood and metal.  It spiraled up in a funnel and dissipated above in the shape of a galaxy.  Where it had been stood the elf from the tavern, but he seemed to be a few shades brighter, glowing even.
"It won't be a palace." Said the elf to Partheus.  His hands clasped behind his head and turned blue.  A mist began seeping out from between his fingers. "But I can work with this." The hands unbound, and he cast them forward.  The mist flooded from his hands and wrapped around the pile.  It became so dense that the materials were hidden in azure fog.  He turned to the side and mumbled dimensions to himself.  Then he raised his hands, and the mist rose with them.  It spread out and took the shape.  A blue pavilion of cold steam stood with its side to the desert beyond.  He dropped his hands, and the sheet of blue fell from it, revealing a structure.
"Thank you, Grandor." Patricia remarked.
Partheus. "You made that look too easy.  But my back would disagree."
"How do you think they built the Sentinel before that crystal shell of its hardened up?" Asked the mage. "It wasn't me, but the process was the same."
Partheus looked at Xavier and shrugged. "It's always educational to be around a mage."
Grandor greeted Xavier with a bow and approached. "I thought I met the whole crew.  Guess not.  I am Grandor Aurathaine, pleased to meet you." A hand was extended.
Xavier took Grandor's hand and shook it. "I'm Xavier Pontius.  I'm just here early."
"Really?" He quipped. "I can feel your aura brimming over.  You must be a magnificent wizard to have such power.  You humble me."
"Yeah," Partheus added, "Xavier appears to be the highest potential mage we've found yet."
"No, I mean he's already," Grandor looked into Xavier eyes, which reflected a desire to hush.  He readjusted, "already manifesting.  He'll be easy to train."

Chapter Five

One
Xavier stayed in a guest room in the upstairs of the saloon next to Partheus and Patricia.  Grandor was either staying outside, or perhaps he fashioned a room in the pavilion.  The wind over the place sounded like it was waging a long war against the roof, and winning.  He heard creaking in the room next to him for a while and some exasperated breathing.  Then came inaudible talking and silence.  He felt alone in that place, as if the room he was in was no more than a hollow between walls, and he was haunting it.
He rolled over, and his bed made that same squeaking.  He stared at the wooden wall.  It was dark and dry and didn't care about him.  He could smell the age on it and thought it might be able to reminisce with the sand.  His mind wandered to the wizard outside, and he wondered just how he would brush off his talent to The Order.  With that his mind went back to where it always seemed to settle these days.
He could see her silver fur and feel her power.  Grandor was right, mages had a way of sensing these things.  But still, she had surprised him that day in the hospital, and he was glad for it.  He wished he could go back and reopen the gash, just so that he could talk to her again.  Xavier was infatuated, and he knew it.  All he could think of was the comfort she brought him.  As he laid there on the verge of sleep, between the world of the waking and of dreams, he desired her presence and a soft touch.  To hear her voice and let his burdens melt away.  So he slipped away from the waking realm.
Two
He woke up before the elf and canid and walked outside.  The red sun was still under the edge of the eastern horizon, but its light had begun to spread into the sky beyond Harkem.  The air seemed unable to move, and the place was silent.  Xavier felt Grandor sitting in the shed with someone else, felt like Hodge.  He didn't like using his radar, as it made him feel visible himself, exposed.  He supposed that was nonsense, and people suspected him anyway.
He reached for the handle on the shed's door and heard the mage speaking inside.  It was reciprocated by a deeper voice.  So many things led up to this, and he knew that if any little thing had been different, he wouldn't be where he was now.  He knew he'd be somewhere better.  But that door beckoned him, and a courage to face obstacles began tickling his abdomen.
Hodge regarded the novian as if he'd been expected.  Grandor gave a look of concern to Xavier and then swiped it over the werewolf.  Hodge then asked the elf to continue explaining what the Leviathans were doing without a king to follow.  Grandor picked up again after a moment and went on about their search for an heir.  He said most of the Trothan family had been killed by either Dahzir or Trothos himself and that finding a relative was proving a challenge.
In a dark corner Hodge replied, "Why don't they find a new leader then?  The country can't go on forever without one."
"They will." Grandor explained. "They'll choose a governor."
"But we're on a campaign to overthrow them."
"Maybe you're to be the replacements.  Can't say.  I never overheard anything good."
Hodge pressed his back into the wall, and the whole shed leaned his way a bit. "I've been around for a while.  What's a little more time?  Still, I wouldn't mind finding a way back home."
The elf cleared his throat, and for an instant his hair was flecked with a magical discharge that glowed a deep orange, the color of embers. "Hello, Xavier.  'Sleep eludes the arcane.' Or so they taught me.  The sun isn't up yet, and Hodge here has an excuse to be up.  But for you and I, I guess that adage holds true."
"Good morning." Xavier said.  He looked at the werewolf leaning in the darkest corner of the shed.  Come noon the whole thing would be alight from cracks in the woods and boiling.  But now it was dark and cold.  Hodge blended into the wood as if his clothes had been stained with wood grain.  Only his young face stood out, but his eyes were hidden in darkness.  He looked hardly any older than Xavier.
Then Hodge's eyes reflected green for a moment, and what bit of his nose could be seen flared.  "So," he said, "let's talk about what you're hiding."
Xavier wanted to spew out a "what?" or a "huh?" but all he could do was gape.  In doing this, he managed to let out a long "uhhhhhhh . . ."
"I can't smell you, Arthur couldn't smell you, and neither could Salina." His back came off the wall, and the blackness seemed to swallow everything but his voice. "Everyone has a scent.  Any canid can tell you that, and any dog could if it was smart enough to talk.  Faint, strong, odd.  It doesn't matter.  But something." The sound of his feet shifting sand. "I gave you the benefit of the doubt.  Maybe you were just covering odor, not like that's unheard of for a young man to do.  Then I find out that you're brimming with magic, enough so that you would be in league with what Dahzir could do in no time.  And enough to spare that you'll probably surpass him."
Xavier couldn't help but feel flattered despite how tense he was.  His mind searched for an answer, while Hodge went on.
"Now, I can believe that you might not be able to turn a mountain into a castle and float it around like Orion, sure.  But you don't belong with the initiates.  And if you're so good, I'd have to be a fool not to think there was more to this scent blocking." He either cleared his throat or was holding back a growl. "I've been a fool plenty of times, not even once have I enjoyed it.  So explain what you're up to, or be as good as you supposedly are." At this last part, he dipped into an accent not native to Destursha.
"I'm hiding something." Xavier said.  He let the words loose, to do whatever havoc they pleased.  He didn't want to lie, but there were things he still wanted to keep back. "It isn't anything The Order needs to be concerned about, just personal issues.  I'm not a spy or an enemy, but I can't go on." But he wanted to.
A beam of sunlight finally passed through the shed and struck Hodge's face as he turned.  Grandor went white, as the blood fled him.  Hodge was on Xavier, driving him through the door and snarling.  A hand as strong as a steel claw wrapped around Xavier's throat just as his back hit the ground outside the shack.  Hodge was above, exposing a mouth full of sharp teeth.  His black snout was wrinkled with rage, and dark ears were pinned back.
The hand around Xavier's neck was still human, and he knew the man above was being careful not to spread lycanthropy.  That's when he realized Hodge wasn't going to kill him.  If he was, a scratch wouldn't matter.  By then, he knew his esophagus could've been compressed to nothing.  More than ever Xavier wanted to go on about his intentions and why he hid his skill.  He couldn't, both from his own resolve and from a hand that while not lethal, was still clamping down.
The grip lightened, and Hodge reverted to his normal self.  He climbed off the novian and pulled him up, nearly tossing him. "I don't know why I believe you." He said.  He paused to watch a timid Grandor peek out of the shed. "So then, let's talk about your motivation."
That he could do.
Three
Xavier explained to the werewolf that he wanted to have lycanthropy and that he would find a way to earn it from them.  As he did this, Hodge's face would light at every point before sinking into a deeper and deeper dread.  Xavier knew he was about to hear something he didn't want to.
"If you're looking for longevity," Hodge said through the cave that was his face, "then you'd be better off using your magic to get it.  Immortality can't just be given, not even if you deserve it." Grandor stepped out of the shack, walking over the busted door.  He propped himself against a wall and tried to look as if he didn't notice the other two.  The rest of Harkem was still asleep. "I've been to hospitals, and I've been through wars.  I have seen thousands of dying children, ones that I knew I could save just by putting a hand on them.  I've watched boys fight other boys because old men told them to, and I've been by their sides as they died in ditches and in prisons and in hospital beds." He replaced the solemn stare with one of pity. "Any one of them and all of them, I could've saved from cancer, from wounds, from age.  But at the same time I couldn't.  It takes more than a good heart to be a werewolf, more than being on a bottom rung, and more than just any of our condolences."
"I'll find a way to earn it.  So much so that you'll have no choice."
"Maybe you will." Hodge sighed. "Hope you do.  Even so, I can't promise you'll get it.  There's an intuition involved that can't be ignored.  I'll give you this choice once.  Leave our company.  Go off and perfect your magic.  Live to an old age."
"Walking with wolves is dangerous, and you're apt to get a hand bitten off.  Or worse.  And with the task ahead of us, I'm going with worse.  I won't lie to you, most of the volunteers that came to us are going to die.  Not happily either.  I've seen this before.  Bright eyed youths line up to fight for what they believe in, and in no time at all everything will be stripped away from them by other youths doing the same.  This will not be a good war."
Four
Partheus and Patricia walked out of the tavern, and Hodge's face eased with relief.
Partheus approached the werewolf and stretched his back on the way.  Patricia walked beside him with her hands behind her back, both wearing the same clothes again that day. "Hey Hodge, good to see you made it." His eyes bounced on the broken door, and his last stretch popped his shoulders loud enough to make an echo in the street.
"And good to see you, friend." The werewolf replied. "I brought a few drums of water for the recruits."
"What about us?" Partheus wrapped an arm around Patricia's shoulder so naturally that it was almost hard to notice.
Hodge smiled. "Ahh, right.  You too.  Almost forgot: you get thirsty too, don't you?"
Patricia chimed in, "If it gets as hot these next few days as they're calling for, you'll be the only one who isn't."
Hodge turned toward the magician. "Then we should get things set up before everyone gets cooked."

Five
Initiates began arriving and setting up tents north of town.  The natives went south after dawn except the tavern owner, while the setup continued.  Their number was smaller than those that had come out of the recruitment session at the castle.
When Solday rolled around, there were a dozen mage trainees and eight tents waiting for direction.  By dusk the temperature had soared and was just beginning to calm again, and that's when the drakens returns from their work.  The dozen recruits went to the pavilion, where Xavier already was, and the rest waited.
Grandor waved at the eager group and turned expectantly to Hodge.  The werewolf strapped two oil drums to his back and motioned for everyone to follow.  Partheus and Patricia stayed to his right and Grandor to the left.  Xavier fell back with the students and did what he could to blend in with them, which proved successful.
They walked for hours after that, making some of the students complain, but most were afraid to raise a fuss and kept their mouths shout.  When it became dark, Grandor released more of that blue energy in the form of a great flame above his head, the light of it causing the tan sand to appear gray.  The air turned cold, and Xavier felt gooseflesh crawl up his forearms and try to stake a claim between his shoulder blades.  Jackals in some indeterminate direction yipped and drove a few of the mages to huddling.  The group stayed quiet, Partheus whispering to the dogman every now and again, but otherwise footsteps substituted any talk.  It even came to a point where three of the initiates ran out of drive and turned around.  It was just Xavier and nine others.
Six
One of the recruits stepped on something metal that sounded like a drain grate in the sand.  The blue flame cast light into a depression.  Hodge unstrapped the barrels and laid them on their heads.  He turned to Grandor and asked him if he could handle any fleeing rats.  The magician nodded, and the werewolf disappeared into the cavity.  The magician turned to Xavier and beckoned him with a flick of his head, and the novian stepped up.
"Help me scorch these bastards if they come out." He said.
Xavier lowered his head and shook it. "I know that you know I've got it in me, but I don't understand it yet.  I really can't do anything in combat."
"Then just watch.  Maybe you'll get it afterwards."
Xavier nodded and looked over his shoulder.  One of the recruits, a green troll in a blue hooded sweatshirt, stared at him with seething suspicion and jealousy.  Good, Xavier thought, rivalry just when I needed it.
A screeching sound and something heavy running into steel.  Grandor drew his hands together, and a blue orb formed in the space between.  Its light was brilliant and put the ghostly thing above to shame.  Xavier shivered, remembering that it was still very cold around him, so he warmed himself with magic thoughts of heat.  Then a five legged bear without fur or eyes came tumbling over the slant.  The extra leg hung from its chest boneless and flopped about.  It grunted just before the crest and slobbered out a mucous far more viscous than ordinary saliva.  Grandor pushed his hands forward and released the blue sphere.  It sailed at the mutant, expanded and then contracted down to a bullet.  It sunk into the thing's chest just above the fifth leg, and the beast stopped.  Then its chest cavity bubbled, and the front half of the abomination splattered like a water balloon.  Yellow pus covered the ground around the hind legs, and some of it found its way to Xavier's boots.  A recruit gasped at the sight and another fainted into the sand.  The smell was noxious.
A snarling man came up over the depression, or what might have been a man.  It was naked but without any noticeable gender.  Its roaring was like gurgling, and it seemed to be perpetually choking.  Its eyelids were sewn together, and it had no ears or hair.  Its skin was loose and white and covered in little black specks.  It tripped over what remained of the bear, and that's when Grandor turned it into paste as well.
A few minutes passed, and Hodge walked up from the darkness.  His clothes were cleaner than Xavier's. "They're all gone from in there." He said. "Everyone hold up okay out here?"
The mage answered and told the werewolf to lead on.  After grabbing the barrels, Hodge did just that.  Xavier looked back again to see who had fainted, but by then everyone was walking again.  The hooded troll looked no happier.


Seven
They entered an old mine.  Fragments of arches and heavy doors laid about, half buried or rusted through.  The great blue flame broke apart and attached itself to the walls, forming lighted corridors.  The flickering light bounced off the walls and what remained of wooden beams.
