Entrances on either side lead to rooms and other tunnels, some of which gave off an odor like the ruined bear.  Broken pickaxe heads were scattered about, one still embedded at the end of a vein.
They came into a great chamber, and the flames behind moved forward and coated the walls and ceiling in all the appropriate places for light fixtures.  The room became bright and cozy feeling, but a must hung in the air like that of a moldy basement.  A large machine, deteriorated to the point of looking sick, rested in the lowest point in front of a rift filled with a dark plastic like resin.  Red rusted rods and step panels were strewn about along with a tangle of delicate wires.  A piece of black enamel shell jutted from some of the wreckage.
Hodge put the barrels down. "Make yourselves comfortable." He looked around and turned to the group. "Just get some sleep.  We'll start the training tomorrow." He flicked up a wrist, and the recruits scurried off to the walls, as if this were the first test.  The troll lingered behind before going and grouping with a pair of novians.
Grandor walked to the tear in the earth and then to the piles of refuse.  He shook his head.  Nothing he could do with any of it.  The mage settled himself against the broken down machine and crossed his arms.  He seemed content.
Partheus and Patricia wandered to the other side of the rift across from the mage and sat up against a wall that had been plastered in that same bruise colored resin.  He was visibly tired, and her yellow eyes appeared misty.  They fell asleep leaning against one another.
Xavier scanned for Hodge, but the werewolf had disappeared without notice.  So he went to the slate red wall with the rest of the newbies, finding a spot as distant from the troll as he could.  He ended up by a female elf that had somehow become pudgy; he was the last before the wall hooked into the ramp that had brought them in.
He sat there for a time, not even imagining sleep.  A few of the initiates had already fallen through consciousness, a few stared blankly at the vast room, and the few who had actually taken Hodge's order in full spectrum gawked at one another and chattered like high school students in a cafeteria.  After a while, the girl next to him kicked up a bout of snoring, and that's when Siegfried started up.


Chapter Six

One
("You handled yourself pretty well earlier.") Said the voice inside Xavier's mind.
Thank you.  He sent back.
("All you have to do is tell them who you really are, and they'll let you in.  I can guarantee it.")
That wouldn't be earning it.  I already told you how I feel about that.
("Stubborn!") Siegfried sent the image of a boulder with this. ("I didn't know it could be passed down so many generations.")
My stubbornness is mine.  I made it.  And I'll stand by it.
Not a sigh came through but something like the rustling of leaves and a breaking wave. ("Listen, you can keep this up if you want.") A pause, autumn leaves and wooden rockers.  Some lemonade. ("But I can't do this any longer.  My soul is tired, and I'm ready to go home.") Clouds and gold streets, pearly gates.
So you're saying I don't have to worry about you tipping scales in my favor, and that I also can't fall back on that?  His lips wanted to move, and he felt as though he might just be talking aloud.
("The fallback is your identity.  Use it.  You were a martyr.") A crystal room, blue flames and a ruined arm. ("But I wanted you to know that something is coming.") The world eater, army under the horizon, a dark wind.
Could you be any more cryptic?  Ruder than he had wanted but just as blunt.
An eye and a map with pins. ("You know I can only see Earth and Destursha, not at the same time and not their wholes.  Just points I focus on.") A dwarf in a great tree and a boy dropping a flashlight. ("I'm not God.")
I'll be sure to be ready for whatever it is.  He thought a moment.  Aren't you holding the sky together?
("I haven't had to do that since.") Black and blue fur and a saloon.
I appreciate all the help.
("I'm going to tell Hodge to be prepared.  Though, since I don't know what it is, preparing might not be possible.") A purple box with a green question mark. ("I just know it isn't the Leviathans or the governors.") A fading man walking into a sunset. ("Then I'm done.  Hope it all works out for you.")
Thank you, Siegfried.
("And thank you, Cecil.  Goodbye.")
Goodbye.  A sensation of loneliness filled Xavier.  His ancestor's presence had left.  It was all on him now.

Two
After feeling the departure, he felt fatigue.  It was heavy and made of sludge, creeping from the base of his skull to the front of his mind.  He missed that endless energy, of not needing rest, of being endlessly pumped with adrenaline.  Xavier could sense a ghost of those old sensations, but not enough to keep him awake any longer.  So he joined the elven girl in the realm of dreams.
The initiates began their training under Grandor's instruction.  He took them one by one aside and gave them a task to perform.  Xavier noted that none of them were able to do anything more than raise a few grains of sand from the floor or create a dim, useless light in their palms.  Grandor reassured each that they were excelling, and Xavier couldn't even discern whether or not he was lying.
His turn never came, as the elf continued cycling through the earnest mages.  He was learning enough just by watching.
At some point Hodge poked a hole into each of the drums and added a tap to each.  Several bags full of plastic cups sat on the first drum and were emptying quickly.  Xavier took a cup and filled it, and the green troll followed after and filled his own.  Xavier drank and pitched the cup into the purplish rift, as the troll followed behind and did likewise.  When he settled himself against the crumbling drill and turned again to the center of the room where the wizard was teaching, the troll rested up next to him.
A hard shove almost made the novian trip over his own feet.  The troll stared at him with jealous eyes and crooked teeth, and that was all fine until he began talking.
"What's your deal?" Asked the troll.
Xavier turned his thoughts toward warmth, toward burning, and thought of setting the troll's hoodie ablaze.  Because of those thoughts, he began something he didn't even realize.
"Well, why are you here?  You seem pretty cozy with the elf and the headman."
Xavier didn't want to answer. "Tell me why you're here first."
"Shove it!" Came as a response.  The troll spit on the novian. "If you think you're better than me, you're not!" He pushed Xavier again, and this time he did fall.  The troll began sweating. "Ain't nobody better than Deidre!" A glance to make sure he hadn't been caught.  He stepped forward, his legs prancing and readying to kick. "No midget is gonna look down on me!"
Xavier did something he couldn't have expected when the first kick landed on his shin and sent a rod of pain up his leg.  He thought again of the troll cloaked in flame but with greater passion and fury, and that's when the troll ignited like wicker.  He smoked like tires and began rolling away from Xavier.
Three
Grandor ran from his place with another recruit as Xavier began sitting up.  He extinguished the flames that had overtaken Deidre.  The troll's hoodie had been burnt away along with his pants.  His face was charred and his left eye sealed shut, and he had nothing but soiled white underpants left to cover him. "Can you walk?" Asked the elf.
"Fool tried to torch me!"
"Can you walk?" Echoed Grandor, not concerned whatsoever.
"You gotta boot him for that!  I been attacked!"
Grandor bent down over the troll and observed the loose dead skin on the left half of the face.  Then he pulled his arm back and slapped the troll with all the might his bony hand could muster.  The dead skin was torn off, and the flaming pink flesh underneath revealed itself. "If you don't answer me one way or the other, I'll finish the torching.  Can you walk?"
After a squeal. "Yeah, but, I mean.  My drawers, man."
Grandor cupped his hands together and held them to his mouth, and he projected his voice as with a megaphone. "Look.  Deidre pissed his pants!" Everyone in the room heard it well.  He removed his hands and lowered his voice. "There, now that they all know, you've got nothing to hide.  I want you to head back up the ramp where we came from." Partheus had come over from his own wall and stood between Xavier and the downed troll.  Grandor gave him a look and shook his head at Deidre. "Can you list him?"
Partheus nodded and followed the troll once he had finally built up enough courage to get up and walk.  Deidre turned a few times to look back at the wizard and the novian, but it seemed that embarrassment held his tongue in place.
Four
That evening when the initiates fell asleep and after Xavier realized he hadn't scheduled off work, Grandor found him along the wall and asked him to come along on a walk.  Xavier couldn't help but think of being back in school again and being called to the principal's office for some squabble or another.  Just being a witness to one of those events seemed to entail a certain amount of punishment, as if having seen wrongdoing was a corrupting act.
They walked into a corridor opposite the one the group had entered from.  Grandor's blue flame flicked onto the walls and made the path light.  Their steps were quiet and made no echo, though Xavier thought that might be because of the wizard.  After they were well out of earshot of the chamber, Grandor stopped and leaned against a wall.
Xavier hung his head low, hoping somewhere in his mind that by doing so he would avoid reprimand.  If he were cast out now, he would have to start this whole endeavor over again in another form.
Grandor pulled a flask from a pocket under his ribs.  Two shot glasses materialized in his freehand and he asked, "Want one?"
"No." Xavier replied. "Burns my guts and melts my brains.  Not into that."
The glasses vanished and Grandor put the flask away. "Well, I'm not going to drink alone." He knitted his hands over his belly. "Quite a reaction out of you earlier.  With a little bit of effort, you could toss fireballs around like toys."
"That's not what I was expecting you to say."
The elf grinned and looked down the corridor the way they'd come. "Were you expecting 'The other teachers and I have been watching your behavior, and we've come to the conclusion that you do not play well with others.' Or maybe 'I hate to do this, Xavier, as this will be on your permanent record, but you'll have to be suspended.'"
"Well," He began.
"Well, this isn't school.  This is army recruitment, and everyone here is an adult.  Misconduct can be self-solved, though prevention would be better." Grandor yawned.  "I saw what he was doing out of the corner of my eye; I'm not stupid.  Besides, Salina checked up on him last week, did that with all those pups."
Xavier perked up at her name. "Did she?  I know she came to see me."
"Told me which ones would be troublemakers and which ones probably wouldn't have the grit to train out here." He sat down, and it became apparent that he planned on sleeping in that hall. "The spineless ones left on the way here, as she said they would.  He was the worst of the miscreants, so I'm not exactly surprised he got himself burned." Xavier slid down against the other wall. "Partheus sent him home with a few bandages."
"I'm sorry I told Hodge about you." Grandor said to the wall above Xavier's neon white hair. "But I'm not getting paid to keep my mouth shut.  I hope you understand that."
"It's okay." But Xavier wasn't sure he believed that himself. "Maybe it wouldn't be if things had gone a little farther."
"Just between the two of us, promise I won't say a word, are you really unaware of your powers?"
Not as unaware as I'm making out to be. "Yeah, I surprised myself with that fire."
"If that's the case, you've got more potential than anyone I've ever met.  That includes the old kings." Grandor closed his eyes and looked as if he'd fallen asleep. "Hey, what do you think of Salina?"
Xavier had plenty of thoughts, but none of them seemed casual enough to share with the wizard. "She's interesting." Then he added, "Kept me from getting killed" again "by an angry squatter."
"I heard about that, but you know what I mean." His eyes opened and he gazed at Xavier. "You have to admit she's foxy."
Xavier agreed fervently in his mind, but he wondered if this was some sort of trap. "I won't argue with that."
"I've got a wife and kid myself, so I'm off the market.  You're a young kid still." He grinned the way a father might at his shy teenage son going on a first date. "She's older than I am technically."
"I don't know how this has anything to do with magic." Xavier hoped that would plug the conversation.
"Not a thing, nope.  But I'm tired and curious, trying to understand what's driving you." His eyes closed again. "I'll throw a hypothetical at you.  Let's say she approaches you, swaying her hips or whatever the women do that drives us mad.  Says she needs to talk with you privately.  Bats the eyes, all that.  How do you respond?"
"I'm not out to . . ." He didn't want to say it.  It seemed rude, but he supposed in the context it wasn't.
"Oh lighten up." Grandor crooned and laughed. "I wouldn't expect anyone, save for the most hopeless of fools, to be ungentlemanly with her.  Not unless they wanted their organs plastered against the Sentinel like Garuza Day streamers.  Your face is telling me everything anyway."
Xavier was glad the talk was coming back down to earth.  He conceded, "She is a part of it.  Not the all, but a part."
Grandor fell asleep.
Xavier had a good feeling that Grandor had started drinking before they'd come into the tunnel.  He couldn't smell it, but he didn't think he was the only one who knew how to cover scents.  He would probably forget the conversation the next morning.  It made him wonder in earnest what he would do if he was ever in a position to court her, no that sounded dated, and the very idea made him flush red.  He didn't want it to be this complicated, this offset.  He just wanted to go back to the night when he'd gathered apples for the dead boy, the night she had caught up with the group.  He remembered her turning and walking away when he had walked down this same corridor three years past, and he didn't know what to think anymore.


Chapter Seven

One
He woke before the elf, before anyone else, and walked down the hall through darkness until the light of dawn struck his face.  He came to the surface, and it was still cool.  An endless horizon of copper colored sand and dusty red mesas surrounded him.  He sat down in the depression that made this other entrance and leaned against a wall of packed sand.  He looked at his small hands and legs and on to the other side of the cavity.
Xavier sighed.  This was where his journey had truly begun.  Not when Salina met him in the woods and not when he woke to the smell of whiskey in Ectoplas.  That had been their journey, but his own had started when he was abandoned, when the stress made him forget.  He wondered if it would've been better had he never remembered, never felt compelled to help The Order.  He knew that was selfish.  People would have died for his comfort.
A wind came, and he heard the individual scraping of thousands of grains.  If he focused, he knew he could control that wind, and so he did.  He formed miniature dust devils and made little clouds of sand float around.  Using what he learned from the troll, he scorched the sand.
That's when he understood he didn't belong here.  It would take them months of training to catch up to him, and decades to get where he would be in a few months.  He stood up and crested to where he could see the whole horizon.  Beyond him, over the dunes and miles, was the Dark Forest.  It had been a bastion for Chrissenia, but now it was overrun by those same mutants that had just been driven from the mine.  If he went there, he could train in solitude.  Maybe then when he became good enough, he could aid his old friends against the governors.
Two
He stayed outside that day, and no one came looking for him.  When the sun came into power, he thought of cold, the kind that had bitten his skin in Redora.  When he did, the sun's torching faded away.
When night rolled around, he became parched, cool or not.  As he went back into the old mine, he turned and saw the two rising crescents.  Orion and Artemis would form a ring that night.  He thought that was just delightful, and continued on to get water.
Three
None of the initiates noticed his return, and the wizard had his back turned while trying to show the elven girl how to disturb air.  Partheus and Patricia were nowhere to be seen, and Hodge was standing next to the water drums.
Xavier took a cup and hit the tap.  The cup filled, and he heard it pushing air away in a whistle.  He drank and nodded at the werewolf.
"I need to talk to you." Hodge said.
Xavier nodded and melted his cup in a bright flame until nothing was left.  This seemed to make the old werewolf happier. "Sure."
Xavier expected to be taken aside again, but went on right there. "I still don't know what to think of you and this cloaking." He leaned forward as if to make himself clearer. "But there's no denying that you're going to be a fantastic wizard."
"I appreciate that," Creased brows, "I think."
"Well, that leads me to another thing.  You're wasting your time here.  Grandor doesn't know how to train advanced students, and this whole thing is technically illegal." The werewolf grinned as if the idea of law was some oddity.
"I didn't think about that, but you're right." He crossed his arms. "And I guess a wizard higher up than Grandor would be too deep in with the Leviathans to risk helping, right?"
"It's like we already had this talk.  I want you to take the train from Harkem to Cynderton and then walk through the Glade Woods until you can see Morphus." Hodge appeared pleased by how well the novian seemed to be listening.  He went on, "Train as best you can there.  It should be secluded, so you don't need to worry about anyone raising a stink about a magic user without Dahzir's crest sewed to your chest.  We're going to take the city," He made sure the others could not hear him, "in two weeks.  At the rate you're going, you should be on level with the mages we have ready to go there now.  We'll give detailed instructions once we arrive."
This was going somewhere, good.  Xavier felt at his pockets.  They were getting thin. "I can do that." He replied.
Hodge noticed the gesture and added, "I'll have you paid when you get to Cynderton.  Then you can stock up on whatever you want before you head into the woods." He bit his lip with enough force to split a telephone pole. "And if you want, I can send a message to your old work.  Tell them you've found a new career.  This is a commitment, you know."
"I'll get right on that." He desired to be away and past Cynderton to where he could begin perfecting his magic.  At the same time he couldn't help but think that this was going too smoothly, that Siegfried had greased some wheels, but for some reason, he didn't care.
Four
He left after that exchange, and by the looks on some of the intimidated initiates, he supposed they were glad he was departing.  He walked around the remaining bones of the man thing and the bear.  Into the cold night he walked, keeping warm with simple thoughts.
The town was still dark when he arrived, and a train wouldn't be through again until dawn.  He avoided the saloon for no reason he could think of and walked beyond the station dock to a little clearing that had never been bothered by sentient beings.  Artemis and Orion formed a red and green ring above him, and he could see the shadows of their bodies in the middle, old friends shaking hands.
That's when the troll reappeared.  Either he couldn't find a train, or he had been waiting for Xavier to leave.  Xavier thought the latter.
Deidre was clothed again and lacked the smell of frightened urine.  He held low a silver glint in his right hand, a dagger.  His hood had been thrown up, but he was as recognizable as the Sentinel, one of a kind.  He was moving toward Xavier from behind the platform, walking fast.
Xavier took a few steps back.  The troll was not large, but in his current form the troll was larger.  If he shifted back, he could overtake Deidre, mortal or not, but he did not yet know how to manipulate the size of his clothes.  Before the jealous green thug was on him, Xavier did the only thing he could think of.
Flames sparked at the man's feet and then lashed upward in a billow.  The sound of rushing hot air, a "foom."  The dagger fell, the fire crackling like on dry wood.  The troll began rolling, ever attentive to what was learned in grade school.  Red tongues coursed over his charring clothes and blackening skin.  But the flames wouldn't die.  Instead they grew stronger, and the troll stopped.  It burnt him through this time.
Xavier had killed before, but never a citizen and never in a way as cruel as fire.  He watched the corpse shrink before him, and he knew he was still fueling that pyre.  He let it burn until nothing but ash remained and scorched ground.  That fire, he knew, was unnatural.  It hadn't needed fuel even for a moment.  It had all been his mind's doing, his heart's vigor.  
When he did let it die away to see the flaky ash below, Xavier conjured up a wind.  It swirled the remains around in a controlled, symmetrical funnel.  Then he let it loft away over the sands.  He pushed it, spread it, until it was lost to the Scarab.  No one had seen, he knew, as he could feel the townsfolk, though they lacked magic.  That was the end of Deidre Hax.
Five
He slept on the train to Cynderton.  When he got off and left the station, an aemon woman with crimson horns caught his attention.  She held up a white scrub board with his false last name displayed on it, so similar to an earth practice he'd never experienced himself.  He approached her, and before he could explain himself she seemed to know him.
"Xavier?" She asked and lowered the board.
He nodded, saw that she didn't respond, and replied, "That would be me."
She pulled a slip of paper from a pocket and held it up beside where he would stand in her vision.  A picture of me.  He thought.  Then she put the paper back and removed an envelope from a carrier's bag strapped to her back.  She handed it to him.
He mouthed a thanks and opened the white fold of paper.  Troths were inside.  Not a substantial amount, but as much as he would earn in a month at the hospital.  Is it customary to tip the messenger?  He wondered and couldn't decide.  So he gave her a few, enough for a fair meal.
Six
Cynderton was beautiful, and he regretted that he would have to leave it.  The houses were all made of stone, cottages with circle windows and thatched roofs.  The streets, though there were few, were paved with cobblestone.  A wooden windmill stood at the far end of the little town, and he saw flowers blooming on most of the windowsills.  The street lamps were old but well-kept and ran on oil.  Yards had green grass and no concrete, outlining each were smooth river stones.  The place was small, the kind of community where everyone knew everyone else, and their business to boot.  An old fashioned place, untouched by the modern world, almost stubbornly so.
He found a general store and walked in, needing supplies and wanting some beef jerky.  Any notion that Cynderton was untouched by the present age was dashed when he stepped through the door.  A bell chimed, and a woman at the counter smiled and nodded, a fellow novian.  Walls to the right were lined in coolers, to the left were dairy and meats, and up the center was an assortment of things from popguns to potato chips, florescent lights buzzing over the aisles.
He wanted to keep things simple, food and drink.  He grabbed a hand basket, thinking to himself that it would be an appropriate proxy size for what he could travel with.  He slapped bottles of water into it from the machines and found the jerky beside the chips.  Beef jerky was good, always had been, but it was also cheap.  Not like it had been on earth.  So he stuffed all he could fit on top of the waters and moved to the counter.
The novian woman was shorter than he was, her hair as red as the sun and her skin as dark as the night.  She didn't seem to think his purchase was odd, hardly took notice to the things she rang up.  It was all just a bore to her.
He paid for everything and left the woman to sit alone in her boredom.  When he stepped outside, he heard a speckled rumble from up the street.  A blue van rounded a corner and came barreling down the road in front of the store.  Its paint had chipped away here and there, revealing an old white coat and in other places rust.  The tires were bald and rolling over the cobble like popping bubbles.  Trailing behind it was a great plume of dark exhaust.  The carburetor backfired as the blue brick hustled past him, and Xavier jumped back.  It went down the road until the cobble ceased and was dirt, and it kept on rolling toward the woods.
He watched the smoke spouting from it mix with clouds of dust until the actual van was hidden in billows of filthy upthrow.  He couldn't help but wonder who was in that van.  It was normal to see a vehicle fly down a street on earth, but not here.  Unless you had a decorated uniform, you probably didn't have access to them, and that looked nothing like a military vehicle.  Xavier slung the bags of jerky and water over his back and followed.
Seven
The tire tracks on the dirt road looked as if they might have been the only to ever touch it.  The houses had stopped after the road changed over and in their place were endless green pastures.  Winter was up north, but it would never truly reach this place, not with its snow and biting cold at least.
He saw the forest ahead and the road leading into it.  He had come down over a knoll and couldn't see Cynderton looking back anymore.  In that place it might have been a world without towns, just soft paths, green fields and grand forests.  It bordered the fields in its own endless stretch.  It was a pine forest, dense and dark even from the open road.  He knew it wouldn't be close to as shady as the Dark Forest and less dangerous, but the size of those trees, magnified by his short stature, gave him pause.
When he came up to where the road entered the treeline, he felt a cool forest breeze pump from under the canopy to his skin.  He walked in, and the warm touch of the sun vanished.  The burden on his back was growing heavy and again he thought of trying to change into his true and stronger form.  Instead he walked off the road and sat against a tree, the coarse bark rubbing his back as he adjusted.  He took one of those waters and a pack of jerky, good stuff, and began replenishing some of that energy he'd lost on his way.
He looked deeper into Glade and listened to the animals scurry about.  He couldn't hear much and wondered if that van had scared things into hiding.
He sat for a bit and let his stomach settle.  There were ferns below his feet, and he reached out to test something.  He thought of cold and of freezing, of the air in Redora and of the night he died.  The ferns had been swaying calmly, but they slowed.  Then they stopped and changed color.  A frost encased them, and they were frozen and dead.
He was pleased with this.  If Hodge wanted him to train, it would be best if he didn't set the whole forest on fire.  He melted the ice and let the plants be scorched, and then he crushed the embers with a freeze.  He enjoyed this and relished that he was doing no more than thinking.
Eight
He picked up his supplies and continued down the path.  The road was covered in pine needles, and his feet crunched them like chips.
After some time of practicing how to levitate objects and make his pack lighter, he came to where the van had stopped.  It was off the road, though not by much, and its doors were open.  Beside it a ring of cinderblocks sat with a hodge-podge pile of twigs in the center.  A blue cooler was next to this and behind it was the skeleton of a yet-to-be-assembled tent.  He kept walking.
Night came fast and hard in Glade, as anyone from Cynderton could've told him.  The thick trees blocked most of the light as it was, but when the sun fell over the horizon, light hadn't a chance of breaking through the branches.  He lit his way with a red flame similar to Grandor's only smaller and less luminous.  Everything was quiet and dark, and eerie.  The trees cast dancing shadows against the flickering light of Xavier's fire, and the silence magnified every sound that broke through.  He wondered if the mutants took up residence here.  Too bright for them in the daytime, but now . . . it was as if he were in an evergreen twin of the Dark Forest.
He came to a clearing with a busted foundation of stones in the middle.  A long time ago someone lived there, but not so long ago that the forest reclaimed its land.  He stopped at the edge and looked at the flame hovering in front of his chest.  Xavier was unsure if it would draw attention or drive it away.  He let it burn.  
He looked up at the sky.  Artemis and Orion were absent from the great black expanse.  In their place was a myriad of stars speckled about freely and cloudy galaxy dust radiating with their dim, stretching light.  Then a formation caught his eye, a cluster of stars that looked like the Big Dipper.  He was no astronomer or Prince Henry the Navigator, but he knew that constellation.  Or at least he knew its twin that could be seen from earth.  The sight of it all made him feel close to his home world, and with that feeling came homesickness.
He let the fire drift away as he sat against a pine.  He had it float over the remains of whatever home might have been.  It looked like it had a basement.  The fire grew, and the whole clearing came into the warm light of it.  Xavier fell asleep, and the fire died.

Chapter Eight

One
That next day he walked until the trees began to give way to field again.  When he came to the edge of the forest, Hodge was right; he could see Morphus, might be impossible not to.  It was far off but big enough to cover most of the horizon with its skyline.
There will be plenty of people there.  I'll have to help kill some of them.  That's what these conflicts always came down to, killing, and burning and suffering.  Sure, the cause was good.  The Order had great intentions, but Xavier didn't think that could justify what it would take to get those intentions into motion.
Between him and the city state was an expanse of field.  After that he made out what appeared to be a few outlying farms, maybe some suburbs, and then the true city.  It looked cut off from the rest of the world, but he knew that was the point.  On this side it would be like that, and on the other there would be highways and roads, and defenses.  He smiled despite himself.  Like Macbeth.
He tied his supplies to a low branch and walked into the field to scout.  He found himself at the edge of a cornfield that was overripe.  He couldn't see over the stalks and wasn't comfortable walking through it.  So he checked his surrounding, made sure he was alone, and stripped down.  The next thing he would work on would be manipulating the size of his clothes, but for now he would just have to do this without them.
He grew in height and width, soon he was a tall dwarf, and then he was human.  He stopped there for a moment.  It had been the first time he had taken his true form since returning from the abyss between worlds, and he hoped it wouldn't be the last.  He looked at his true skin, and it was rippled with burn scars he had never truly suffered.
Two
It reminded him of that night.  Dahzir was lost after the battle he and Cecil fought.  The old aemon's body was mortal and couldn't withstand the abyss.  Cecil's body endured, and he could not die.  He met his ancestor Siegfried in that dark place and learned all he could of magic there.  In order for him to come back to the mortal plane, he had to give up something infinite.  So he cast away his lycanthropy.
He quit his daydreaming and continued changing.  He took the form of an ogre, tall enough to see over the corn.  Beyond the rows was a farmhouse, drab as possible.  On the other side of that was a red barn with chickens pecking at the dirt in front.  There was an orange tractor and a manure spreader attached to its hitch.  An elf in a straw hat was tending to a flower bed in front of the house and another elf was walking a cow out of the barn and parting the chickens.
A dirt road led from the farmhouse to pavement, where there was a T.  The road flowed left to the horizon, and to the right it ran into a stretch of sparse homes with tiny yards, the outskirts.  All populated by average, unaware and uncaring people.
He changed back into Xavier and dressed himself.  An ant had crawled into his shirt and bit his neck while he laced his boots.  He slapped it off, and the spot itched.  Then he went back to his camp to practice.
Three
Some nights passed, and his supplies dwindled away.  It rained in those days and provided him a wonderfully low visibility that he used to practice unnoticed.  When it did not rain, it was just cold.  He worked on magics to keep comfortable, but comfort also included sating hunger, and he did not know how to conjure food.
After a week the jerky was gone.  Water was easy to replace with all the rain, and when he needed to he would freeze the water in the air and let it melt into a liquid.  The hunger started with a craving, and then his stomach seemed to contort and rebel like a caged lion.  When that subsided, the headaches would come.  He knew he couldn't go without food forever; that was a luxury he had lost.
He'd only hunted a few times back on earth, and never met any success.  He could fish, but there were no rivers or lakes or even oceans anywhere near Glade.  Even on those unsuccessful hunting trips, he had a rifle or a shotgun, and his father.  Out here all he had were reflexes and some magic.  Sure, he was bracing to use it in full-scale combat, but that concerned no stealth and it came without any fear of collateral damage.
He woke up one morning with a chill running down his spine and wondered if it was weather or magic that caused it.  His stomach lurched, and his head was full of helium.  He rolled himself up in the thick grass and got to his feet.
After taking care of his morning necessaries, he drank some thawed water, scratched at all the bug bites he'd slept through and then meandered into the woods.  Went the extra step and muffled his steps, wondering if that was cheating or not.
Four
The sky above the green needles was gray.  It wanted to snow, and it wanted to coat all that green in a heavy, branch breaking white powder, but it was too warm.
Xavier walked for a while on the crunchy forest floor, silent and mostly absent from sight.  It was odd feeling twigs snap under his feet and no sound to accompany, and it made him feel as though he was not moving at all, had just stayed at his camp's boundaries.  The branches themselves were well above him, almost in a different wood altogether.  Looking up at those high arms and beyond to the gray made him forget for just a moment what he'd set out to do in the first place.
Then he heard the footsteps.  Not his, but a pair's just a stone throw away.  They didn't see him, and they couldn't hear him.  One was large, an ogre.  The other was an aemon, and both leaned against opposite trees.  Xavier hid behind his own tree and cloaked his appearance.  When he looked around the side again, he noticed the ogre was smoking.
"C'moooooon, man!" Pleaded the aemon. "I'm freezin' here."
"No." Replied the ogre. "Went through way too much trouble to borrow Rodney's wheels." He puffed out a gray cloud that smelled like burnt leaves and hair. "T'morra."
"Man, it's already tomorrow.  Know what I'm sayin'?"
There isn't going to be any game for a mile with this shouting.
"It ain't another day till ya go to sleep.  You've been keepin me up all night buggering me.  I told you to pace."
"Aww, don't be like that."
The ogre wiped his face, as if doing so would clear all the annoyance away.  Then he said while tossing a bag to the other, "but you better sleep in."
"Yeah, yeah, man I will.  Stone tired here. But damn, man.  It's getting too cold for this.  I think we should go furder south."
"Van's low on gas." The ogre giggled for no apparent reason. "And You can't use magic on it."
"You think Rodney could get it down there?" A calm stream of smoke. "His brother-in-law used to be a scholar.  Maybe something of magic got to him?"
Xavier had enough of that conversation.  An urge inside directed him to give the aemon some frost burn, maybe enough so that he would pester the ogre into leaving.  Instead he began walking away, upwind to where there might still be things to catch and eat.
Five
When he finally found a deer, his stomach jumped up and seemed to ask him to attack the creature and eat it like a feral beast.  He wasn't sure, but he thought he might just be able to do that.  If he transformed into a wolf or a bear and then stayed in that form long enough to process the creature without the blood making him sick . . . and that would be harder than just stopping the thing's heart and cooking it.
It worried him how easy it was to take down the deer.  All he had to do was think of its heart failing and its arteries freezing.  Thud.  It fell and was dead, no gunshot, no following a blood trail, and no worrying about hitting that sweet spot where all the important organs were.  It made him sick, and it made him afraid.
Dragging it back to camp might have been hard if he didn't put the doe on a sled of slick ice.  That'll help keep it longer anyway.  He thought.  He'd work on levitation and telekinesis next.
When he returned to his camp, he stopped short.  Voices were coming from his camp, and he was sad to recognize them.  He peeked around a tree and saw the two stoners rummaging through his things.  Looking for munchies and wrecking his setup.  He watched in growing frustration as the aemon knocked over one of his rain catchers and spilled water over his sleeping area.  The ogre stared at the outlying farms and skyline as if he were gazing into the deepest depths of space and life.
A grin came over Xavier's face, as he thought of a new spell to try.  The air was damp for how chilly it was, and he used that.  Hidden behind the tree he condensed the air around his camp and froze the ruined rainwater.  The two were so disconnected that they didn't notice any of it, not even the aemon complained about the cold.
He pulled together enough ice to do it, and now it was down to his animating skills.  If he pulled this off, he knew this would be a tremendously useful.  He looked back at the deer, wondered for an instant if he was poaching, and refocused himself on the task at hand.  The ice took shape, and it formed into a golem that looked exactly like his true self as a werewolf.  Enough water was left over that he even gave this proxy his old sword.
The ice puppet stood as tall as the aemon.  Its rippling texture resembled fur but was as transparent as liquid glass.  Xavier manipulated its very particles and made its movements fluid.  With all his concentration he had it walk toward the aemon, who was just beginning to see it.  Then, conjuring up the memories of his lycanthropic bellows, he ventriloquized the roar he had heard himself use in his dream of Chrissenia.
The guttural sound was a twin of his true roar, and it had the desired effect on the aemon.  The horned, maroon skinned man fell back into the cold damp grass in horror and spun himself back up in one terrified motion of survival instinct.  He ran behind the ogre and toward Glade's interior.  The ogre broke away from his soul searching stare, and it seemed he sobered.  He followed his companion into the woods, and Xavier had the golem follow for a short distance, swinging its icy sword and growling like a wild animal.

Six
He sat that night in front of the fire, eating cooked but gamy meat.  The golem had been slow and a little awkward, but he had kept it upright and made it project the sounds he wanted.  With more practice he could summon up little contingents.
That he would have to use to slaughter people.  It always came down to that.
The deer, frozen when he wanted it to be and cooked when he was hungry, lasted him until the rest of the volunteer army began appearing.  The Order came first almost as if they were meeting up with him.  It was the first time he saw them acting as a group since they abandoned him at the mine, and comparing this to that unsettled him.  Partheus and Patricia were not there as far as he could tell, and there weren't so many there that he thought they could be lost in crowds.
The whole of the makeshift army gathered in half an hour.  It was dusk, and the siege would be just before dawn.  The army, the soldiers and war mages, all huddled together in tents within the forest's lines.  The Order invited itself to stay with Xavier at his camp and talk strategy.  He sat with them, not engaging but not ignoring either.
They went over (once again it seemed) how best to spare civilians and get to the governor's manor.  They told him that governors had less access to crystal armaments and manpower; they would not be fighting Leviathans.
The plan was to pass through the cornfields, cross the paved road, and enter Morphus before rush hour.  Hopefully the "civies" would still be drinking their morning coffee and would leave their front doors closed until the city was taken over.  Hodge explained that the governors were not stupid; they knew The Order would be coming.  They just didn't know which first and when.  He explained that as they were taken down, or hopefully conceded, that the battles would be fiercer.  A process of elimination would make them more and more prepared, but this first one should be easy.  Should.
They told Xavier where the manor would be and that it couldn't be seen through the skyline.  It was on the wealthy side of town just beyond the business district.  If they got to it early enough, there was a chance that none of the workforce would be caught in crossfire.  They were to expect ballistae, gunfire, and limited magical ordinance.
He fell asleep that night wishing he could stay up and reminisce with them, maybe talk to Salina and catch up.  See if Arthur had any words of wisdom or at least a distasteful joke.  What made him happy was that, just before his mortal body drifted away, he heard those things.

Chapter Nine

One
Salina kicked him awake and walked away before he could say anything to her.  He rolled upright and saw the first risers wandering out of Glade.  None of them wore any armor, and that bothered him, and he had none either.
Arthur was directing the soldiers into lined formations, and Nelrene was organizing the lines into sets of five.  Arthur waved Xavier over and pointed to an empty plot next to a line of novians and elves.  Then the werewolf threw a circular motion out with his wrist, and four people lined up behind Xavier.  Nelrene came by after this, told Xavier to stay where he was, and realigned the troops behind him.  Good, he thought, make me responsible.  When she moved on to another line, he looked back to see just what group he was leading into probable death.
A tall woman stood behind him, a dogman that looked like a Labrador.  A rifle rested on her shoulder, and the natural way she let it sit there encouraged Xavier.  She looked down at the novian and either began smiling or getting angry.  She showed teeth, but it was hard for Xavier to know how to interpret that from the canine peoples.  Behind her was an elf without any visible weapons, so he might be another mage.  Behind him was another novian, dark skinned but with hair just as bright as Xavier's, and in his hands he clasped a little red leather bound book with a gray tassel hanging out of the binding.  Towering over the novian was an elf with a serious look engraved on his face and a scoped rifle propped on the tongue of his left boot.
While he was still looking back, the dogman put out a padded hand. "I'm Mara Rosehall."
"Xavier Pontius." He shook her hand, thinking somewhere far back in his mind that it was rude to shake a woman's hand unless she offered it first.
Then the elf behind her, "Branth Milster." Then the novian, "Gabriel Asura." And the stern faced elf. "Alistair"
A voice in Xavier's head started up.  Not Siegfried's voice, but his own.  He was talking to someone, a memory, but couldn't see who. "You don't name the animals that you know are getting butchered." He had said.  And the other asked why, but Xavier couldn't even remember if it was a man or woman, or a child or friend. "You get attached.  And if you kill something you're attached to, a part of you dies."
Two
He never even noticed that his feet were moving forward until they hit the cornfields.  Mara and the elves could be heard behind him, pushing aside stalks.  The one in the rear was grunting and muttering complaints, but Xavier and the other novian had their heads conveniently low.
Then they passed the roads and walked into the city.  Everything was quiet, and with all that silence, Xavier could hear into the houses and apartment.  He could hear the subtle drone of alarm clocks going off and televisions playing the morning news.  If he were still a werewolf, he believed he'd be able to hear rodents in the walls of the inactive houses and closed shops.  
It was quiet until the wealthy district.
There was a whistle from one of The Order ahead, and the five werewolves dispersed and ran forward.  A moment later the footsteps were no longer silent, and fireballs began lobbing through the air and to the roofs of mansions.  A sharp crack, and one of the mages in another line fell down with his skull split in half.  More fireballs, and then the cracks began on this side and sent bullets toward the houses.  Snipers rested in windows and on balconies but were already beginning to be choked by smoke.
Salina appeared in a window in the upper hall of one of the great houses.  Her clothes were riddled by holes and splattered with blood.  Xavier watched her pass from window to window, swinging his old sword.
A thin dome appeared around Xavier and his group as the other novian chanted a simple phrase over and over.  It made a springy sound as bullets began pelting it but not penetrating.  The elf at the back moved up and crouched beside Xavier and began aiming.  Mara did likewise, and the other elf Branth stood beside her.  Gabriel continued chanting with his red book flung open, Alistair took a few shots, and Branth began hurling out bolts of electricity.
Xavier started freezing anyone that came out of the houses with a weapon.  No one on either side had a uniform or a banner linking them together, and it made him wonder if any of the people they were fighting were really just citizens defending themselves from an organized mob.

Three
Their group moved forward as did what remained of the other lines, and the enemy pushed down the street from house to house.  The manor was at the end of a cul-de-sac and protected by gates and surrounding buildings.
A house fell beside them, and Branth staggered out of the bubble in reflex.  As he tried to get back into the protective veil, Xavier looked at him and watched his body be punctured by wild bits of metal.  The elf stumbled in, high on adrenaline and clutching the wet parts of his torso that he thought were only sweat.  The color left his face, and his limbs went limp.  Branth's chest rose and fell a few times and stopped.  And they pushed on.
It had been cloudy that morning, and now it rained.  The droplets bounced off the shield and flowed around them like a car windshield.  Mara stopped firing and tapped the cryomancer on his shoulder. "Can you make me something to steady my shots?" She asked Xavier.
He halted his barrage of little clear spikes and noticed her.  He drew water under the shield and froze it into a tripod with a grooved plate on top.  Alistair did not ask for one, but Xavier made one for him as well before he turned back to the enemies.  They were only a hundred yards from the manor now.
Four
The governor's house had five balconies all with swinging double doors.  There was an iron fence surrounding the property and hedge animals in the front yard.  Behind the house was a vineyard, and wine cellars encompassed the place.  It was made of tanned bricks and white marble with a gray slate roof.  The fence's gate was bolted shut and barricaded with cars and ice.
The army advanced, and the balcony doors flung open and revealed the ballistae they'd been warned about.  The Order members tore down the gate and began moving the cars, as the giant bolts began raining from the balconies.  The opposition manning the spear hurtling machines ignored the werewolves and aimed at the troops.
Death came through the barrier and to Alistair.  His rifle dropped like a toy as he was pushed back and impaled on the bolt.  There were a few moments between the impact and his death when he managed to throw out a few curses and words of regret, but then he only hung in place, pinned off the ground by the spike embedded into the wet street.
Xavier stopped fighting and put his efforts into strengthening Gabriel's shield.  He didn't understand the magic completely or why the other novian had to keep chanting to keep it going, but he added layers of ice and added what understanding he had of it into its force.  The shield thickened and left Mara to do the attacking.
She picked off attackers from the ground, while others took care of the balconies.  Now they were in the courtyard.  It seemed to Xavier that time was moving forward faster than normal, almost as if pieces of it were being skipped over.  Someone was thrown from a balcony, and he knew one of the werewolves had gotten up there.  Mara said something, but he couldn't hear it over the excitement, and she continued firing.
The front doors opened and fighters poured out.  A few were killed, but the rest dropped their weapons and raised their hands.  Not the surrender Hodge had been hoping for, but it was better than total devastation.  Guns dropped and bullets stopped.  The fires and arcs and flying shards of ice ceased, and silence pervaded the air in a slow and calming breath.
Five
Then the governor's voice was heard over the sounds of injured warriors and burning houses. "What are you doing?" Not even a question.  He repeated this over and over, each time with more fluster.  He appeared in the open doorway behind his tired soldiers.  Xavier felt a twinge of anger at his sight, as he could've been Dahzir's twin. "Nothing but bandits!" He said and picked up one of the dropped guns.
As the governor held up the rifle, making to shoot wildly, Xavier froze the inner workings of the gun.  Then he pushed the ice into the aemon's fingers, through his blood, and up his finely dressed arms.  Before the governor had time to look at those his ire burned for, his whole body was utterly frozen, and his heart had been stopped.  A moment passed, and what was the governor of Morphus broke apart.  Salina appeared behind, sheathing the great sword and shaking her head.  The rest of The Order came seconds later.
Hodge stepped out of the manor.  He looked down at the shards that were once a body and smoothed back his short, unchangeable hair. "Well," he began and then loudened, "not the peaceful surrender we wanted.  But it is still a victory." And then even louder to the troops whose ears had been rattled by all the commotion, "You all performed wonderfully!  This is your victory!" He sounded rehearsed and unemotional, but the response ignored that and the streets filled with cheers from the still standing soldiers and even some from the wounded.
Six
There was a cleanup, captures, and then a feast behind the manor.  Xavier, Mara, and Gabriel were commended for their teamwork, and general morale seemed to be up.  That was just great, but there were more cities and more governors, and there would be more death.  But he put that all aside and enjoyed the victory, had to if he wanted to keep going.
Those who had survived grouped together with what remained of their lines, and Xavier was given an opportunity to know the pair that had stuck with him through all the hell.  He sat at a table that had once been in the manor, and he didn't think it would ever find its way back in.  Gabriel and Mara sat across from him.  They all had glasses of good wine, spoils of war he supposed.
The pantries had enough food stored to serve their little army, but Xavier thought that if the casualties had been less, there wouldn't be.  Gabriel pecked away at garlic bread, while Mara defleshed a plate of chicken legs.  Xavier was hungry, had been hungry, but all he could do was roll his food around on his plate and take a bite of something every minute or two.  Down the table were groups of relishing fighters, and the other tables had their own versions of the warriors of Valhalla.
Mara wiped bits of chicken skin out of the fur of her muzzle and in the process got particles of food in the fur of her hands.  Xavier couldn't help but think that was cute.
"So where are you from?" Gabriel asked Xavier.  He stank of garlic.
"Huh?" Xavier heard, but this was a reflex. "Bellshire.  It's over in the Western Territories.  Right outside of Cordun."
"Potomys is the governor of that region, isn't he?"
He had to think about that, not sure. "Yeah." He hoped that was right.  You're being rude.  Ask him where he's from.  And aren't these two familiar looking?  "Where are you from?"
"Mara and I are from Chrissenia, but we both live in Redora now."
I should've known.  His appetite came back, and he began hurting the plate.  Nevermind if it was rude to talk with food in his mouth; these people owed him. "So why do you think they need an army to do this?"
"Well," He stopped, "I'm not entirely sure now that you bring it up."
Mara started up. "This is the people's war against the government.  The Order's just organizing it." She leaned forward, and Gabriel leaned back. "The governors don't have all the high-tech stuff the Leviathans do, but I think they're afraid of being caught again.  The one that Dahzir was puppeting . . ."
"Hodge." Xavier tried not to sound too knowing.
"Yeah him.  Well him getting caught in one of their magic nets is what started the whole mess."
"And got all of us evicted from our home." Gabriel added.
"And they don't want a repeat of that.  I mean, that stuff's powered with crystal, and the governor's aren't permitted to have crystal."
Xavier nodded.  His plate was empty. "But that doesn't mean they don't have it." He wiped his face on his arm, ever the example of good manners.  He looked at his companions.  T-shirts, jeans, they were civilians.  Everyone here was. "What uhh, what are we waiting on now?  The fight's over."
Mara. "I think we're waiting on everyone to finish eating and settle down.  Then I guess Hodge or one of them will give us leave or tell us to march somewhere else."
Seven
Just as she had guessed, once everything calmed down, Arthur showed up and began calling lines up.  Some of them were whole, some were down to one, and a couple had been wiped out or deserted.  Leave, leave, leave, that was what he told them.  Xavier wondered why he would call them up one by one if everyone was getting a rest, but he thought he knew why.  Good thing they were courteous enough to call his work and resign him.
Xavier's group was last to be called, and almost all the others had already left.  They stood up and walked in the same order they had when going into Morphus.  Gabriel, the smallest of the three, wobbled a bit with wine in his belly.
Xavier looked up at the man with his smug face and red hair and bad temper.  He felt the familiarity between them and the disconnection.  Words began pouring out of the Brit's mouth, and the novian knew immediately that he could finish for him.
"Xavier Pontius, Mara Rosehall, Gabriel Asura." I commend you. "We commend you." I was close.  Something, something, assignment. "We mourn the loss of Branth Milster and Carus Alistair with you.  They fought well." He cleared his throat and handed Xavier a letter that didn't seem to be in his hand the moment before. "The three of you had some magnificent displays of teamwork out there and, trusting in your capable synergy, we have prepared a group mission for the three of you." He yawned, and Xavier knew it was just for effect. "Nothing too dangerous if you keep your heads straight.  We need you three to scout out Pathosia, a sad place really." He laughed, proud of his Greek pun.  It both irritated Xavier and warmed his heart. "The details are in there, but the REAL details will be made by you three."
Xavier nodded, but the other two saluted and voiced a "thank you, sir." Perhaps he was being just a bit too familiar with his old pals.

Chapter Ten

One
The three were alone behind the ruined manor, and Xavier opened the letter.  He supposed he was the lead, which also meant he was responsible for them.  As he began reading the orders, he wondered for a moment if either of them had known Folas.
Gabriel stood on his toes to see over Xavier's shoulders, which the former werewolf found amusing.  Mara bent down behind.  At some point she had picked her rifle up again, and now it rested propped on Xavier's back.  They read together, and the assignment was simple.  It came with directions to the city and some suggestions on what to look for.  It seemed to suggest that The Order would try to make the city cave from within, if they could.
There were orders to stop in an outlying town called Sul and pick up a money drop off for the three of them. "We'll pick up new clothes there." He said and pocketed the orders. "And think about fake names while we're there.  And fake business.  I imagine the governors will be looking for anything suspicious in their front lawns."
"Should we leave now, or wait for morning?" Mara asked.
Good good, they were asking for decisions, so he was the leader.  He looked at the clearing sky, wished he had a watch or could remember what he did with his phone.  Afternoon, early. "We'll get on the train.  Sul will take a full day on the train to get to anyway, unless we can commandeer one of the Leviathan's airships." He watched Mara sling her rifle onto her back, and he cloaked it.  She looked back, as if thinking the strap broke. "They don't need to see that."
Two
Morphus was on lockdown.  Even though only the wealthy section had any collateral damage, the whole city kept its doors closed and its streets empty.  The walk to the station was cold and damp and silent.  Water dripped from roofs and splashed on the gray pavement like it was the only thing to ever touch the street.  Blinds were closed and lights were off.  Nice, quiet, and unsettling.  Xavier took glances at the windows and roofs, half expecting someone to blow his brains out from one of them.  His companions followed, their boots striking up water and adding to the white noise.
The station was a monument to everything colorless and drab.  Slate roof on the station house, bleached concrete at the loading dock, locomotive that appeared retired, a Roman numeral black and white clock mounted next to the ticket window, and a schedule board that looked like it had been taken from an ancient schoolhouse.
Xavier stepped up to the counter and looked in.  There was a desk, papers strewn about, and a door leading into a back room.  A press bell was bolted to the counter, but it probably wouldn't be loud enough to get through that door.  Grandor had been right about mages, and Xavier sensed someone behind that door.  Not a wizard by the feel of it, but a guy who could light his cigarettes without a match.  He pushed down the plunger of the bell, and it chimed resonantly.  The man behind the door was afraid, and he would not come.  So Xavier tried a new magic.
They live just down the street, nice folks.  Didn't even know about the incident at the governor's house.  You'd be the first to tell them about it.  Off to visit friends, that's all.  He pushed these thoughts at the mind behind the door.  There was some resistance, as the man knew deep down that his mind was being invaded.  But Xavier overpowered the doubts and caution, pressing these thoughts into the man's consciousness.  He hammered it in until he was certain the man believed it, unaware that a bit more would ruin the attendant's psyche.
The door swung open, and one of the papers on the desk slid off and drifted lazily to the floor.  A stout dwarf with wavy black hair covering the majority of his head lumbered from the backroom, swiping breadcrumbs from his shirt and beard. "Hey, there!" He said, resting his thick elbows on the counter behind the wall of glass. "The city's on lockdown.  There was a battle up at the governor's house with those Order people and their militia."
Mara began to say something, but Xavier cut her off. "Then it might still not be safe here, huh?" The dwarf nodded at him. "But we have to get to Sul, and it would probably be safer to get out of the city altogether."
"Yeah, but the line's down, bud.  All trips cancelled.  The train won't move with the engineer and conductor holed up in the engine 'til the green light's up."
These folks REALLY need to get to Sul.  Give them a break.  He felt something in the dwarf's mind crumbling and pulled back there. "If we can convince them to take us, can we go?"
"Y-yeah." He rubbed his forehead.  A headache was coming. "If it's ok with them." A wince. "Here, just hop over the turn.  You have . . . ?"
"Yeah, we all have passes."
They jumped over the turnstile and walked across the boarding dock.  The train's doors were locked, but Xavier worked the mechanisms and unlocked the door before pulling it open.  They all stepped in, and he walked to the front of the train.  He pushed thoughts into the engineer and conductor, and the train began lurching forward.  With the last bit of room he had before breaking their sanity, Xavier convinced them to stay in the engine until his party got off.
Three
The ride was quiet for a time, as the three sat in a cracked leather booth.  The cab shook on bad track every few minutes, a tremor here and there.  It was as if the farther one got from the capitol the shoddier things became.  Mara and Gabriel sat together across from Xavier, who knitted his hands neatly in his lap and daydreamed as he looked out a foggy window.  Somewhere far away he could hear his companions whispering to one another, before he realized they'd been chatting for some time.
Gabriel's voice rose and broke Xavier's listless chain of thoughts. "That was some impressive telepathy you used.  Who was your teacher, if I might ask?"
He thought about lying, use some other made up name of a teacher from that false homeland, but he decided to tell the truth, part of it.  If it was too ridiculous, they wouldn't believe, would probably let it go as personal. "Self-taught." He replied and then, wondering if it was arrogant or not added, "That was my first attempt."
Gabriel's face contorted.  It was obvious that he was considering if such a thing could be true and if he should call Xavier out on it.  Then he looked at Mara, whose invisible rifle was resting standing up between her knees, and shrugged.  He was letting it go, maybe thought Xavier had an odd sense of humor.
A daydream crept back in, and he wondered if there was any good music on Destursha.  He tried thinking of a song that would fit this moment, but he stopped.  That was a good thing he would ponder about before falling asleep later, but he didn't think his partners would be able to reminisce about his tastes.  He looked at Mara's rifle, visible only to him. "I'll uncloak that when it's safe." He reconsidered. "We may have to hide that somewhere."
"What about my spellbook?" Gabriel asked.
He hadn't thought of that. "That too, if there's anything in it that could suggest you're a mage.  I imagine there is."
"How?"
Xavier wanted to say he once planted a sword in a stone Arthurian style, but that wasn't believable even if they did understand it. "Let me worry about that.  It wouldn't be my first time hiding something valuable." Good, now you sound like a thief.
Mara looked down at the novian beside her. "I could use a shower when we get to Sul.  My fur's starting to get matted."
"We'll be tired when we get there, and by the time we're stocked up for Pathosia it'll be late.  We can stay the night in Sul, somewhere with a shower."
Gabriel asked, "Should I write down lodging expenses, in case we can get reimbursed?"
That seemed so far from importance to Xavier that he was tempted not to answer.  He did. "If you have a way to write it down, or just ask for a receipt, go ahead." You don't know anything about espionage, and clearly these two don't either.  Think of something though, this is important.  But he couldn't.
Four
Time and miles passed by.  The crew kept the whole thing chugging along.  They'd wonder why they did it later, what with the lockdown.  By then Xavier and his team would be on their way to the next governor.  
The red sun set.
There was a dining area in one of the cabs.  Though it lacked fresh food and attendance, the three managed to find less perishable foods stored there.  Bags of crackers and peanuts mostly, which reminded Xavier of airline food though he'd never flown and knew these two never did.  Mara and Gabriel shared their food casually, and signs of something between them became apparent.  They were too buddy-buddy to just be acquaintances from Chrissenia.
They ate, and the once werewolf considered this.  Just what he needed, a couple.  If anything happened to either of them, he'd get to feel all the worse for it.  He could only hope they weren't prone to spats and quibbles.  There was no time or patience left for that.
The train rocked, and he began to pry.  The two gave him a little of their personal lives, but it was all edged with fence.  He knew they were hiding something, and he couldn't get it out of them.  After some time the conversation settled on past jobs.  What were you before all of this?  What do you want to do when it's all over?
Now it was dark outside, and he couldn't see the terrain, and he was tired.  This whole day was a rush leading to a crash.  He rolled over on the leather and looked up at the steel ceiling.  There were rusted bolts holding the roofing panels together, and he fell asleep.
Five
He was talking to Salina after going on a long journey with her.  He was taller than her and felt as light as the air that surrounded him, so he knew he was a werewolf again.  They stood in front of a great obsidian cliff side, as grand as Everest and as smooth and dark as space.  Tunnel openings were carved into its side, and Salina said something about Dracula hiding in there, for it was really not a mountain but a cavernous castle built into one.
She told him that once this was taken, they would be in charge.  Maybe the Leviathan's had some way to travel back to earth or at least open another rift.  Yes, as soon as Ichabod was overthrown.  Hadn't it just been Dracula?  Oh, these must be codenames.
He was happy to have gotten so much time getting to know her and told her so.  She reciprocated the sentiment, and he felt even lighter.
Just as soon as he felt the warmth of being appreciated by someone he cared for, it turned to ice.  He felt weight for the first time and saw his hands shrink as the world grew taller around him.  Salina began walking toward the black mountain castle, and he reached out and called for her.  After long adventures and all the bonding, all the struggles, he would settle for death and for the mundane if he could only feel that warmth another moment.  But as he called out to her, she kept walking, never turning back, never hearing.  She had never known him.
Six
It was still dark when he awoke, and his eyes were welling.  He knew it had been a dream, but at the same time he knew if he didn't choose his path wisely, that abandonment would come.  As he sat up in the booth, the others still asleep, he leaned against the cold metal wall by the window and wished he could just give this whole thing up.  Go live a simple life, as simple as a wizard could and let The Order pursue their own dreams.
He had died once and secured the title of martyr.  He thought he had it in him to let his dreams die as well.  So if they turned their backs on him again and his goals quashed by apathy, let it be.
The cold walls pressed ice into his veins, and the quaking of the train and its outside noises gave him the sense of ghosts.  As if they might be riding the black train through Hell.  He looked out the window at the darkness, no fire and molten magma, just cold and lifeless nothingness.  He wiped his face down, and the chill in his spine seemed to recede a bit.  That was enough rest for him.

Chapter Eleven

One
Mara and Gabriel woke up some time after the sun had been up.  No one was hungry or in a talkative mood, and Xavier returned his attention to what laid beyond the glass.  The two sat across from him and made their own glances at the expanse and then back to him, perhaps waiting for an order or some kind of decision.  He understood he was no more qualified to explain spying to them than a kindergartener, and he ignored their expectant glances and watched the land roll by.
A lifeless and barren place was outside the window.  Red dusty rock rolled out from the tracks like the wrinkles of an elderly hand.  It was porous and desolate, with endless crevices and nooks and nasty places to fall into.  He hoped the train would not roll over here, as this place seemed as hospitable to life as the surface of Venus.  Someday this would erode down into a new desert, but for now it was nothing more than an ankle breaking wasteland, devoid of all but a horizon that promised change.
He sat, and he thought, and he determined.  Hours passed, but his companions let him to his ponderings, maybe hoping he was building a plan or just praying he wouldn't snap at them.  His mind skipped over the subject of Pathosia continually and rested on thoughts of his old friends, of his old home and family.  If not that, then it would go forward to his end goals, the things that would take place after they trusted him again in that time of "what do you plan on doing after all this?" Pathosia would come and go, and he supposed it was nothing to worry on.  It would come and go, and he would not let it barricade him from his plans after that, so he didn't even concern himself with it.  The big picture was all that really ever mattered.
Two
"So I was thinking." Gabriel began.  They were only a few minutes from Sul. "You pick up on magic pretty quick, and you already know about cloaking spells." He waited for Xavier to take his eyes from lumpy rocks. "Maybe after we get an idea of what the place is like, you can get us all hidden and we can just go to wherever the governor will be and listen in.  Maybe we can get our hands on some papers.  I don't know."
Xavier met the gaze of his fellow novian and then glanced at the rifle.  Hiding from normal eyes was one thing, and even if another mage couldn't see something didn't mean they couldn't sense it.  It would be almost impossible to hide the three of them if there were mages around the governor, unless you were exceptionally skilled, which he might or might not be.  He couldn't reply.
The train began to slow, and he became aware of how fortunate they were not to have derailed on the way.  It stopped with a screech, and the first passenger door passed up the boarding dock.  To exit from it would promise a mild drop, some sprains, and a few good bruises if you were feeling lucky.  They, on the other hand, were several cars back and got off without incident.
Gabriel and Mara stood by him on the cemented platform.  He had to look up to meet her eyes; he had to look up at everything.  Even in this offset town, the world was big and over his head.  He had a moment to wonder how the novians coped with staring at people's knees their whole life before Gabriel broke the thought.
"Do you think they're already here?"
"Yeah, they have someone here."
Gabriel crossed his arms.  He was almost comical next to Mara, like a gifted child trying to appear as serious as an adult. "Can you sense them?"
It hadn't even occurred to Xavier that he should try to . . . scan?  This was all intuition, good old fashioned, honest to goodness insight.  He thought so anyway. "Not that strong, but I guess you could say that."
After leaving the station and passing up its cold metal line rails with no one to direct, was a lone ogre, a man, standing like a statue who has lost all admirers.  He nodded at the three upon approach and handed them each an envelope.  He didn't need their names and handed the appropriate yellow paper pouch to each. When they had a firm grip on their credits, the ogre began slipping away as slyly as a basement shadow.  The idea that such a hulking man could manage to nonchalantly stroll away made Xavier think of something dark and clandestine, like the Frankenstein monster being a mafia informant.  The man didn't walk toward the station but down an alley, and Xavier didn't care to keep a tab on the messenger.
Three
They found a burger joint and went in to eat.  Sesame was a chain restaurant all over Destursha, a fast food place with slow employees.  The sandwiches were cheap and greasy, and Xavier passed on fries.  There were militias that would try to kill him, no need to get his heart in on the action.
The three sat at a booth with their identical meals and their lidded paper cups of soda.  The syrup in Xavier's was low, and it tasted like stagnant water.  He decided to dump it and get something else from the fountain once he finished his artery clogger.  But if that bacon cheeseburger didn't taste good.  No wonder these places flourished on every other street corner.
Mara's rifle was resting against the wall beside her.  She was on the inside of the booth, and Xavier had forgotten that she'd carried a military grade firearm into a bum eatery.  He wondered if she had more ammo or if The Order had supplied.
Somehow he was the first to finish eating.  He wasn't even hungry, but he thought Mara would pack away food quicker than the two halflings.  She was bigger for one, and she was also . . . careful, you're stepping into racist territory.  Am I?  I mean she's.  He considered.  Yeah, a dog.  Watch it.
The fountain was a long rectangular piece of plastic with steel nozzles and a stainless tray underneath with a grate overtop a water channel.  There was ice and cold morning coffee sitting in the channel, not flowing into the drain.  He had tried OceanLime, but that was no good.  He found birch soda and applied pressure to the little white PUSH label with a thumb and let it whine the stuff into his cup underneath.  He stopped it with just a bit in the container, took a swig and was satisfied.  He filled it up.
He didn't see how much the others got for pay, and he decided not to bring it up.  There was a feeling, not in his gut but right in front of his mind just behind the eyes, that he might have been given more money.  Of course, if he was the commanding officer, that would only make sense.  But when did he get to be a CO?  Did he even have a rank?  As far as he was concerned he wasn't above them, not one inch.  And getting a few more troths to spend on beef jerky, he knew, could be a nice seed of distrust.
He sat back down at the booth and poked a straw into the lid of his cup.  He didn't sip right away and thought he would probably take it to go.  Gabriel and Mara had finished their sandwiches while Xavier was up.  Now the three of them sat with Mara's basket of cooling fries in the middle of them.
"You can both have some." She said, glancing at the boys.  She focused on Gabriel. "Can you grab some ketchup?  I forgot."
"Sure thing." The novian replied.  But there was something withheld about it.  He was going to add "honey" or "dear" or maybe even "sugar baby." Not sure, but it was something like that.
Xavier became tempted to read one of their thoughts.  Something about that seemed low, like he'd be reading a diary.  He looked up at Mara, who had remembered to throw a packet of salt onto the potato sticks. "No thanks." He said and meant it. "I've never been a big fan of fries, more of an onion ring man myself."
"That's fine." She replied. "Gabe loves them, and I know he was just waiting for me to give the green light.  Honestly he should've just ordered his own.  Orion knows he'd shovel that away."
Xavier shakes his head, partly to humor her and partly because the idea of someone loving the grease fries so much baffles him.  Be careful not to call them French fries.  They're just potato fries here. "At least they won't go to waste while he's around."
Gabriel sat down next to Mara with some red tomato paste. "Are you two going to help me?" It was a large basket.
"I'll eat a few." Mara replies.
"I'll pass." Xavier says. "But thanks anyway."
Gabriel began packing away fries, and Xavier starts on his refill.  He sat contently; these two are okay in his book.  Then he listens, not quite intentionally, to a conversation three tables away being tossed back and forth by a pair of old dwarves.  They were talking, almost arguing as dwarves do, about some sort of game from the night before.  Xavier wasn't up on sports, especially since he hadn't seen a television in a month, but he thought things here weren't much different than they were back on earth.
Four
His cup of soda was almost empty again by the time they crossed the street to a plaza of stores.  The air had a sharp chill to it, and the sun was hidden behind a wall of floating gray water. They were farther north now, not as far up as Redora but closer to the pole than Morphus.  He tossed the cup into a trash bin.  There was still some liquid at the bottom that the straw couldn't pick up.
There were no shopping carts in what he thought of as the parking lot.  No spaces either.  Next to no one owned a vehicle, and he supposed people must carry bags and walk.  He'd heard that in the East Region, especially around Daskin, cars were somewhat normal.  
There were four stores and a bank.  From left to right a hardware store, grocery market, furniture rental, and a discount shop.  The bank was between the grocery place and the rental store.  People were going in and out of each every few minutes, a slow afternoon.
He frowned.  None of this would do them a bit of good.  There would be food in Pathosia, and he was sure there would be couches, too.  Maybe even some two by fours.  He looked over at his companions who were standing and gazing at the single divided building.  They looked like tourists staring at the Grand Canyon, gawking about how big it was and how small it made them feel. "Let's find a place to stay and maybe a place to get a change of clothes." He said this and looked about unconsciously.  He knew no one was watching or listening, could feel it he supposed.  He did it anyway though, couldn't help but to.  
A scrawny draken man walked out of the hardware store.  He looked to Xavier a lot like one of those Jesus lizards that could run on water.  He also looked shady, like the scrawniness was from self-abuse, probably some kind of addiction.
