"I," he began and quickly thought just what would sound believable, "was promoted recently.  A rather big leap, I'll add.  So far this," he waved a hand downward in front of himself, over the clothes he actually liked, "has been fine.  But I have a meeting soon with my old boss, now equal, and my new bosses.  This will not work for that."
The elf nodded with a creased smile, perhaps happy that a working man had risen through the ranks, or perhaps looking to gouge an unexperienced customer for a commission. "Congratulations on your success, sir.  Might I ask what you've been promoted to?  It may help find you a proper wardrobe."
"Absolutely not." Xavier replied.
The elf laughed without opening his mouth and waved Xavier to follow him. "Well, don't worry, sir.  Here at Helios, we have never not," another short chuckle, "double negative.  Ahem, we always find the best fit and make the best dressed."
Xavier wondered if that was a trademarked slogan.
The elf led Xavier to a set of triple mirrors, prodded at the novian's ribcage, and ran a measure over his arms and legs, before baying him stay while he found suitable attire.  Another employee, a buxom female canid of the collie variety, peered down at Xavier overtop of her horn rimmed glasses, trying to believe what she saw.  The elf shot her a glance that said "I know, he's a real fixer-upper." Xavier tolerated the whole exchange the way one would handle the snide remarks of a principal when you had been caught sticking gum to the water fountain, not because you felt guilty but because you wanted to humor the authority until their head was satisfied with a sense of respect.
The collie woman made herself busy with another customer, an obtuse ogre with a mustard stain on his shirt and a strong fascination of the necklace the collie wore.  She did not bother to peer over her spectacles for him, only dealt with him curtly and coldly to show her lack of interest in anything to do with him but business.  Seeing this, or rather observing it through the extra peripheral sight Xavier called a third eye, gave him a sense of cheer.  It was good to see there were things these people curled up noses at other than commoners.
"Sir," The Partheus twin said, holding the parts of a navy blue suit on a hanger, "this, I believe, would complement you well.  It is reserved but very serious.  I'll say you are rather tall, so this was actually made for lean dwarves, unless you would like a custom made-"
"No, that will be fine." I give him credit.  That was fast.  I've never found jeans that quickly.
"As you wish.  Will you be wearing this out?"
Xavier thought about it, and he knew he had nothing to say to the Tower yet. "No.  The meeting isn't today."
"Then I will box it for you, so that it will be ready and unwrinkled when you do need it." The elf nodded, or perhaps it was a form of bow. "Will you be needing anything else?  Shoes perhaps?"
Shoes.  He had forgotten about those. "I will need a pair, won't I?  Can't wear these boots."
"I'll prepare this for you then.  Would you like assistance?" The woman was now at the counter, having sold the ogre a new shirt.  He was standing there holding it and gabbing at her, trying to win her affection through ceaseless babble.  As if swinging enough times would eventually land him a hit, common ground, and she would take an interest in him.  Xavier did not consider himself wise on such matters, but even he could've told the ogre that he wasting his and her time trying.
"May I try the shoes on?"
"Of course, sir."
"Then no, I'll be able to handle that myself."
There was an expression on the elf's face, or maybe it was just under his face, that seemed to say he wasn't so sure Xavier knew what a proper shoe would be. "Well, whenever you're ready, I'll check you out."
The novian nodded, and the elf nodded back.  It was a pleasant wordless communication, one that had both conversers on level ground.  They walked away from each other, Xavier to the racks and the elf to the aid of his fellow worker.
Six
Xavier let out a sigh in the middle of the aisle, staring at black leather and hard soles.  The boots he had on were sizes 4s, large for a novian but small to him.  On Earth he would need children's apparel, more than likely finding some sneakers that lit up and glowed in the dark.  Here he was on a foreign world, looking at . . . what were they?  Loafers?  Oh, it didn't matter, so long as he found some that fit and matched his clothes.  Black was always good for that, and plenty of it was here, even 4s and 5s, wides and slims.  A part of him wanted to grab 12s, just because that was his true fit, and how awkward they would look with that tiny suit.  The ogre finally left, a bell that Xavier hadn't noticed on the way in rang as the large man squeezed through, and he took a box of black loafers.
When he got to the counter with his shoes, the elf almost looked surprised that Xavier had such fine (albeit common) taste.  The collie, who was leaning against a wall behind the checkout desk with a nail file grinding at her dark and even claws, was passing another one of those sublanguage expressions at the elf.  This one said "Watch him pull out a bad card and then try to write a check, just you watch." He hated it when people had these wordless condescending dialogues.
The elf worked his register, a new machine with a rustic styling, with less enthusiasm than he had showed the novian minutes before.  Maybe he was taking credence in the collie's suspicions.  At last though, he calculated the total price, and it was exorbitant.  
Xavier could've bought three more sets.
He reached into his back pocket, and both the collie's and the elf's eyes narrowed.  Here comes the gun or the knife is what they're thinking.  I'm sure he has a finger sliding over some kind of panic button right now.  Xavier withdrew his wallet, where he carried, perhaps foolishly, perhaps wisely, all the money he'd gotten so far in envelopes and began setting troths marked with hundreds on the counter one by one until he reached the price.  The elf and collie looked puzzled, amazed, and embarrassed.
"Thank you, sir." Said the elf.  He handed Xavier his boxes and change and bid him have a fine day and fair luck at the meeting.
Xavier thanked him back.
He had told them he would not be wearing it today out of caution.  Something inside told him he didn't want anyone knowing what he was doing, and to him it sounded like paranoia.  But after thinking on it, he decided not to investigate the Tower too closely today.  He hadn't thought of an entry guise yet anyway, and it seemed brash to go charge in so soon.  Perhaps a casual observation of the property wouldn't hurt though.  Maybe he could toss out his hook and see if he snagged any mages hanging about.
Seven
Xavier is sitting on a bench a block from the Tower pretending to be a birdwatcher.  People in fine dress are strolling through the street with no particular goal in their eyes other than to absorb the scenery.  A woman walks by him with a poodle on a silver leash, and Xavier thinks it is the first time he has seen a normal dog.  The air is warmer now than it had been when he set out, but he still sees his breath come puffing out in little white clouds of wispy matter.  It looks like smoke to him.
He takes a deep breath and uses his third eye to scour the area.  For the most part the people on the streets have as much magical talent as gum wrappers.  One person, a draken man, has the skill to light candles and another can turn pages of books without hands, but otherwise they are not mages.  Xavier reaches out farther, still nothing of interest.  Then farther yet until he is touching the Tower with his mind.  He doesn't know what he looks like, but he hopes he isn't drooling in a trance state while his mind is floating through the block.  
In the tower there are three people of interest.  One is Festus, who has the power to lock and unlock doors.  There are two others with far more power.  Xavier cloaks his presence as best he can to keep them from feeling his senses over them, and he seems to be successful.  These two are near the governor, perhaps outside his suite door as guards.  They know how to burn people alive, use shieldings, and short circuit nervous systems.  The last sounds dangerous.
But that was it.  He could feel traces of others there, but they hadn't the foggiest idea how to manipulate reality.  He drifted away from these two and back to the governor.  What was Festus doing?  He put more effort into that extra presence, hoping he wasn't giving himself away.  The governor was sitting at a desk, staring at a monitor.  He was talking to someone, perhaps through video chat.  Xavier tried to see and hear the monitor, but it was a thing of electricity and wiring, not of crystals and magic.  To him the monitor was a quiet silhouette of square darkness, and Festus was a wooden puppet flapping his jaws.  Xavier had a hunch he wanted to know who Festus was chatting up and why, and maybe even how often.
He pulled back his vision, as he didn't think there was much more to garner without blowing his cover.  Upon returning to his base of operation, which was his body, he found himself staring dreamily at the Tower.  His boxed monkey suit was still beside him.
He went back to the Paragon and stashed his getup under the bed.  After this he went back out, ate at a pasta house, and began wandering his way through the streets listening for information and gently scanning the minds of passersby.  One of the streetwalkers happened to be a steel worker, one of the ones that worked on the Tower.  He had nothing to do with the interior, but Xavier thought that skeletons can say a lot about the flesh that wraps around them.
There were two elevators in the Tower and a fire resistant cement staircase on either side of each floor.  Or at least, the steel had been laid to accommodate such a safety feature.  All fine and good info that he would relay.  He wondered if Pathosia would be like Morphus.  
Would there be a battle?

Chapter Eighteen

One
Xavier stood behind the great rock and listened to the water splash along.  He had a few minutes, and the sun had some time as well, but he thought his meetings with Mara and Gabe might collide with his calls.  Hopefully they wouldn't be here much longer.  Hopefully the whole campaign would end soon, and he could tell The Order who he really was.  Then . . . then what?  Joy and comradery?  Grow up, Cecil.  That's not how this story will end.  Honestly, why don't you ever form realistic goals?  Ahh, but I'm you, so maybe it's my fault.  Thinking like this made his arms and legs feel weak, as if pressure points had been prodded.  He leaned against the rock to alleviate himself and turned on the phone.
The ringing stops, and he speaks up. "Hello, Salina."
"Anything new?"
He bites his lip.  He wants to go off tangent, ask her about her day and how she's doing.  Of course, he knows how she's doing.  He can remember that feeling he had had the entire time he was a werewolf, so there's no point in bothering and no reason to be such a dolt. "The river offers a good entrance.  There aren't any defendable points for them along it.  The whole place is undefended, really.  Except there's a Leviathan vehicle factory called Scylla on the north side of the river, not sure if Festus has any friends there or not.  The governor himself lives in a suite at a place called the Spiraling Tower.  He had two mages guarding his door today.  I don't think they were ex-Leviathans, too amateur.  But they knew how to shut down nervous systems."
"Are you alright?"
He didn't think he phrased it in a way that sounded like he found out by being attacked, so he was a bit shaken by the question. "No, I," He began and looked at the setting sun, "sensed their skills.  I don't think there was enough trace of me there to give anything away." He rubbed his face.  It was burning, and he was very glad that he was only on a phone. "He might have had more around, but if so they weren't mages.  I'll have to get in closer.  He was talking to someone on his computer, but without magical output, I couldn't hear or see."
"Are there civilian residents in this building?"
"I believe so, though I'm sure the neighboring rooms are friends of his.  He doesn't seem concerned about the campaign, and I don't know if that's a good thing."
"Gather information on his involvements and make sure to find out if there's anyone from Scylla on his side.  A rogue group of Leviathans is not what we need to be surprised by.  We don't need to know what he brought to Show and Tell in third grade, so pull out and head back to Fraushein all three of you, once you've decided what we're up against."
"I shall do exactly that, ma'am."
There was a pause, and Xavier thought she might have ended the call.  Then she spoke, and her voice seemed different.  It was more casual and reminded him of the night by the campfire.  It made him feel warm. "Salina is just fine.  I don't care about courtesy references.  Good luck, Xavier."
He had time to voice a thanks before the line died.  Xavier took a deep breath that fought the hot air in his lungs and the boiling blood of his face.  He held down PWR until the gray screen flickered out.
Two
Tonight they had a personal revelation to tell him.  Turned out they were engaged, not such a surprise, and wanted to clear the air on that.  Xavier didn't think they were so nave as to think he didn't already have them figured out.  What he did think was that they wanted to be open about it, to breed trust.  He congratulated them, and they appeared relieved that he wasn't about to spew them a lecture about racial purity.  He could imagine they'd gotten plenty of those.
Beyond that they had little to add to enemy intel, but that was alright.  They had given him stepping stones to do the heavy lifting himself, and he didn't mind one bit.  He supposed part of him looked at other people as incompetent and always had that motto "If you want something done right. . ." ringing inside.  Xavier would rather put himself in danger.  It was a trait he feared he may one day regret.
Three
He paid Ang's rent for another night and retreated to his room.  There was a good winter storm brewing outside, the kind with the sticking snow.  He'd have to wear a coat tomorrow unless he wanted his hot air pocket getting noticed.
He sat with the lights on for a while, pondering everything he was doing and working toward.  Hodge and Nelrene had, once upon a time, told him about where lycanthropy started and how Siegfried had gone to bar the rift between worlds with his soul, but none of it actually made any sense, and he wondered how much of it might be true, even if they all did believe it.  There was no reason that a group of large sentient wolves could make werewolves out of men, not even as a dying wish.  And how did that tie into interdimensional travel?  It did not, and none of it fit into place.  It was almost like a jigsaw puzzle where all the wrong pieces had been forcibly put together.  There were parts sticking out and images that were warped and displaced, and he had no idea how to put them into their proper order.
Xavier thought also about his talk with Salina.  She made him feel at home; they all did.  The Order, the only ones he had any familiarity with, saw him as a native and very possibly a deceptive one.  The people, like Mara and Gabe, didn't click with him the way any normal group of strangers form bonds over time.  In the end he was outcast by both sides, wasn't he?  He didn't belong with either, and despite being surrounded by allies, by friendly faces, he was alone.  It would be like this for Mara and Gabe's children, he knew.  While they might share characteristics with canids and novians, neither group would ever accept them as anything other than mutts
He fell asleep late that night, as he often did when his thoughts consumed him.  His best revelations had always come to him in that special time of solace just before sleep.  The thoughts carried him into dreams, and the dreams brought relief and escape.  As he had done many time before, Xavier wished he would never wake up.
Four
Festus had no tricks, not even a single moment of preparation spared for the coming storm.  Xavier used an opportunity to apply for a position at the Tower to get an interview and a tour of the building.  Festus had been doing nothing but squandering Dahzir's tribute funds to the city on an elven mistress of his in Hurlinge.  Scylla, Xavier discovered, was their own operation and was not associated with the governor in any way.  He relayed all of this to Salina and added points brought up by Mara and Gabe, who had discovered that Festus was planning to throw up a white flag when The Order came marching in.  
With their work in Pathosia finished, Xavier and his team boarded a train for Fraushein, back to base.
Mara and Gabe sat across from him in a red leather booth beside a clean glass window.  Their hands were knitted together, almost absentmindedly.  They were all looking out the window at the passing scenery in a train not unlike the one they had hijacked.
"So Xav," Mara says, never taking her eyes from the window, "wanna talk about why you're in this?" Her hand squeezes Gabe's, and he bounces his eyebrows.
No one can just forget.  They all have to be curious all the time. "I said I would, didn't I?" His mind races around to concoct believable lies. "After all, you guys did tell me about the nuptials." He feels like he should've whispered this, but the cab is empty.  Winter has finally come, and the sea of white stretching from horizon to horizon is a deterrent to travelers.  He decides to go with truth. "I won't lie, you had me pretty well pegged back in Sul." His mouth feels dry, and he reminds himself to only tell them so much. "I need this to stay a secret, of course." To this Mara rolls her hands, a 'get on with it' gesture.

Chapter Nineteen

One
It is a hard thing for him to sum up without blurting out "Hey guys, I'm actually Cecil Fauden, and I'm aiming to become a werewolf again without using The Order's obligation to me."  He scratches his chin and gazes at the white.  Just start off slow. "I'd be lying if I didn't say someone was on my mind." He took a breath, and it came in thick as honey. "There's something I share with them, but they don't know it."
"Is that why you cloaked your scent?" Mara asks.
"Yeah." He hopes that doesn't give anything away. "When they first came to" He almost says "this" but corrects himself without tripping, "our world, I got to know them.  I was different back then.  For one, I was no mage.  They don't recognize me now, and I don't want to just be sniffed out, you know?"
Gabe chimed in. "You want them to remember you for your person, not simply your face, am I right?"
"Yes." It almost hurt to be understood like that. "I accepted an offer of Salina's a long time ago." Careful now. "There were no strings attached.  It was like a gift, but I still want to repay the kindness." Memories swell to the surface, and it's almost too much to bare. "We shared a bond, all of us.  It's sort of been broken, but I want to mend it.  If they knew about me, they'd do it before I could, and then I couldn't return the favor."
Gabe was visibly perplexed, but not Mara. "Might I ask what she gave you?"
"You can, but I can't tell you.  It's something that was just between the two of us."
Gabe now. "If the two of you had such a connection, I don't think all the scent cloaking and magic in the world could hide you from her recognition."
Xavier grinned and set aside his reservations. "Who ever said I was only hiding my scent?"
Mara and Gabe exchanged a look.  A month ago that look might've been fear and suspicion, but now it was a clear look of intrigue.  Somehow he had actually won their sympathy.  Mara went on to ask, "So, can you tell us what else you're hiding?"
"I could, but I can't tell you what the truth of it is.  I trust neither of you would go blab," and that was truth, "but just having it in your minds would leave it to whoever wants to snatch it out." There was a bump, and Mara's fully visible rifle toppled over from her knees to Xavier's.  He pushed it back to her. "It's public information that Xavier Pontius never lived in Bellshire, that he never lived at all.  The Order knows this, I'm sure.  And I know they're keeping an eye on me to see what game I'm playing.  It defeats my purpose a bit to have earned that distrust, but at the same time it makes my work all that more meaningful.  More challenging."
Mara went on, "And of course you can't tell us your real name."
"I was comfortable before," Gabe said, "but now this is going to keep me up at night."
The urge to reveal his whole plan had grown strong and tempting, but he resisted. "If Salina, who I believe would be the first to see through all my nonsense, does realize it, my work will be done.  Then I'll be open about it.  You two will hear it from me, if I can help it." The train rocked again, as a cloud of powdery snow brushed up against the window and then dispersed into a million tiny clouds. "I grew up pretty normal." He said. "Ma and Pa worked, and I spent most of my time at school or in bed.  I was a very sick child.  It wasn't so much that anything was wrong with me, I don't think.  I don't remember ever hearing that I had a condition.  But I was very prone to getting sick.  My dad said it was because I picked my nose, and my mother said it was because I bit my nails.  That might have been true, I can't say."
"I left home when I was fifteen.  It was too early, and I wonder how my family took it when they came home, and I was nowhere to be found." He wasn't talking to them so much as himself, recounting that there was a life before all this. "I wish I could go back and have five more minutes.  I left in a hurry, and it would've been nice if I could've left a note, tell them I would be fine.  Of course, they wouldn't believe it, but still." He half nodded, half shook his head. "I haven't been sick since, and I don't know if I can even get colds anymore.  But even with all this newfound power I seem to have run into, I can't find my family.  I have no idea what happened to them.  They're beyond my reach.  Isn't that just ironic?" He shrugged and turned away from the blinding window.
Two
One of those imperceptible looks passed between Gabe and Mara, and he let go of her hand, readying to make gestures. "Mara and I grew up in Chrissenia.  She was a year ahead of me in school."
"And you have a thing for older women." She said with a strange expression.  Canine facial turns was still new to Xavier.
"I have a thing for you." Gabe replied. "Well, I was the one head-over-heels.  Sort of the shy type though, I was trying to find a way to talk to her without looking like a dork." There was a warmness on his face, clearly thinking of home when home was still there. "Our last year of school is when they taught us magic.  Some could learn, some couldn't, and some were good like you.  I was able to learn it, but I needed disciplinary aid, so they had me write down focusing phrases in a little journal." He patted the square lump in his travel sack. "This red one isn't the same one, but it's similar.  By some luck, they said I had trained well enough to go into operative work a whole year early if I wanted to.  It was dangerous, but I knew Mara was a part of that."
"I never was good at farming mushrooms." She said. "That was really the only other thing to do."
Gabe went on, "And then I caught my break.  I was assigned to go on a provision mission for wheat and flour in Hurlinge, and Mara was on the team."
"I was wondering why you were so nervous that day we set out." Mara had not heard this before.  "Those were the least dangerous missions."
"But even on the way there, and when we grabbed the supplies, I was too chicken to say anything to her.  Some of it was bashfulness, sure.  But then we also had others around, and I didn't know how everyone would react if I was open."
To Xavier. "You would think by now, people would be more accepting.  They advertise it as being fine, but it's amazing how many people spit at the idea."
Gabe cleared his throat. "So I kept quiet, and while we were headed back out of the city, we got ambushed by some thugs.  No big deal, but the guards were nearby and came running.  The thieves hightailed it, what with their nonexistent luggage, but we got stuck trying to explain ours to the Leviathans.  We got detained, stripped of all our weapons, supplies confiscated, and sentenced."
Xavier didn't think these two were all that much older than he was, so he asked, "For how long?"
"Life." Gabe replied. "The actual sentence was ten years, but when they send you to the mines, you're there forever." He looked at Mara who had turned to the window. "We went to the one north of the forest in the Scarab.  They're using it as a training grounds now for The Order's army, which is also ironic, huh?  While we were there, I made it my job to make sure that the beetles were always angrier at me than they might get with Mara.  I have plenty of love stripes on my back for that." He took a few moments. "And then one day, that Cecil kid showed up."
Xavier's heart dropped.  The mine had been a horrible turning point in his life.  It had been when his body was so baffled by what was happening that it sought death, but death fled from him.  His heart had stopped in the mine, and had restarted there.  A shiver went up his spine.
"I'm sure you heard of that." Gabe continued. "Cecil showed up at the mine and freed all the slaves.  It was actually that same night that the Chrissenians were sent into an exodus, which our imprisonment saved us from.  Well, as you know Dahzir showed up, sporting Hodge's body and cleaved all of us to pieces.  The other two who had been on the mission with us were shot down on the way out, so they were blessed not have seen what we saw.  He was so fast, so angry, and so happy.  Everyone was torn apart.  I was beside Mara, and we got spattered, but I used that.  I think it might have been the only time I used magic without the help of a journal.  I pushed Mara to the ground and laid on top of her, while I used magic to make us appear dead." He was staring at the seat beside Xavier in all its emptiness.  There were some things too hard to be passed from eye to eye. "You know, I don't think that would have worked either.  Dahzir was a powerful sorcerer and checking the bodies, decapitating some of the dead.  Just for good measure, I suppose.  I was so afraid, but if I was going to die any other way . . . I don't know.  That I could pass with Mara was comforting."  Mara wiped at her eyes and laid another hand on Gabe's knee.  Xavier was doing everything in his power to hide how close to home this was. "Then Cecil came out of the mine, and Dahzir got distracted.  I feel bad for that kid.  He was really trying to help, and when he was done fighting with the old king, he ran off sobbing.  I'd have told him thank you then, but I didn't know if Dahzir was gone yet.  There were jackals, I think, in the distance making a bunch of racket.  When we finally stood up, I wanted to bury everyone.  I mean, how could I leave them to wild beasts?  But Mara got me moving.  I know now it would've been stupid to stick around."
"We stayed under the radar for a while after that.  Eventually though, we reunited with Partheus and helped him sabotage the king's plans.  We even drove buses that night Cecil led our people out of prison.  After that, things cooled down.  The Leviathans took the bounties off.  And when The Order said they were looking to find a way back home and that they needed help toppling the remnants of the Old Kingdom, we were some of the first to sign up.  We owed them.  Our people had been oppressed by the royal family for such a long time, and Cecil and The Order broke that.  They gave us our home back, the people anyway, so it only makes sense to help them get to theirs."
Xavier turns from the screaming white which is the outside world and looks upon the faces of his friends.  He gives them a sincere smile that reads "We've all had it rough, then." But in his mind he is telling them they are welcome, and he only wishes he could've done more and done it better.
Three
They pass the time on lighter stories.  Gabe explains that Steel Forest had been Mara's musical inspiration growing up, and that she played the guitar "wickedly." Mara went on to tell Xavier that Gabe was a skilled artist, though he denied it, said she exaggerated.  Xavier let them in on some of his own childhood memories, none of which he thought were interesting.  He liked these two, and he was glad to have been paired up with them.  They spent the rest of the ride to Fraushein sharing stories and opinions.  A voice inside told Xavier he was letting his guard down, but he was okay with that, this time.  
This time he was with friends.
They handed in their reports at the Sentinel.  Salina took Xavier's personally, while Arthur took the other two.  That side of the building almost felt haunted with just the two werewolves in it.  Xavier remembered how lively it had been last time, when he had to wait in line just to get through the door.  Now the old prison side was filled with air and paperwork.  There were rooms beyond this first one, and he could hear people behind them, but this first belonged to its own private space.
They were each given a week's leave and were told to return for further assignment thereafter.
They left and ate at a nice steak joint up the block from the old castle, Xavier's treat.  He ate as much as both his friends combined, and they ate well.  They parted at the door.  Gabe and Mara told Xavier they were thinking of going on a camping trip, somewhere off the beaten path where they could relax.  He told them he'd be floating around town if they needed him.  
The group disbanded, and he was left to contend with himself again.

Chapter Twenty

One
The novian returned to his apartment, no eviction notice yet.  He pulled his keys from a pocket, surprising himself that he hadn't lost them, and opened the door to his, as he liked to think of it, den.  The place was as barren as ever and horribly unfurnished.  He wondered if the feel of home would be stronger if the fridge was letting out a rotten odor.  The "I forgot the milk" feeling.  The feeling of knowing you were the caretaker, and without you it all fell apart.  But there was none of that, only a gentle hum from the bored refrigerator and the heavy feel of emptiness.
He walked over to the drawn window, opened the blinds, unlocked the lever, and slid the glass up to let winter's cold bite howl in.  The room had already been somewhat cold, just warm enough for maintenance.  Now it was frigid; it was enlivening.  He took a deep breath and leaned over the sill, arms crossed, gazing at the frozen city.  So many people out there living their lives, working toward . . . what exactly?  Fortune?  Stability?  He thought it was probably just living for tomorrow and to wash away today's pain.  That's how people knew how to live best, though it was never the best way of living.  He watched people go in and out of buildings, some restaurants, some bars.  One was Saint Braun.  Living to live tomorrow and kill today.  He smiled, and his aching cheeks burned with cold.  He was trying to get away from today, too.

Two
He walked to the Sentinel the next morning, wearing one of the coats he'd bought with his janitorial money and a pair of boots that hadn't seen anyone get killed.  The first room, that old jail office, was open to the elements.  No shut door, no heaters running.  It made sense to him.  The werewolves didn't get cold.  If Partheus, Patricia, or another mortal came, there were warmer rooms further in.
He stepped into the office where the clerk had crawled out from under her desk and shot the goblin boy.  It was very clearly not a business day, and he wondered if no one at all was there watching the place.  His face felt cold and dry, as if it wanted to be splashed with warm water.  But his hands were hot with a sense of him trespassing.  Xavier walked up to where the old desk had been.  It had either been replaced or repaired, because one just like it sat in its place.  There were no papers on it, but there was a paperweight, a paw shaped piece of glass with a wolf mural staring up from the bottom.  The door to the old prison was blocked by boxes and worn chairs, but the door leading into the Sentinel was standing fine and ready where it always had.  He leaned against a wall, the cold trying to overtake him but failing, and waited and watched.  Xavier just needed a few minutes to accept the history here.  He believed it would be a step toward promoting a healthy mind.
But fate had a way of being corny, and Salina came walking through the Sentinel door.  She closed the door behind her and stopped when she saw him.  She was wearing a T-shirt that actually fit her properly, jeans, and brown leather boots.  Not winter apparel but comfortable stuff.  Her blonde hair was tied back in a ponytail that seemed to want a complementary baseball cap.  Plain as a stack of cardboard, Xavier thought she never looked more beautiful.  In her eyes he could see the feral thing he had once owned himself, and on her back was that colossal sword in a sheath that could not be drawn from.
"Need anything, Xavier?" She did not seem in the least bit surprised that he was here, but she didn't seem to expect him either.
A long list of desires popped into Xavier's head. "Nope." He said and looked over at the desk with the paperweight.  He understood looking at a person was a part of normal conversation, but he didn't feel that he could handle looking at her for too long of stretches.  He might blurt something out.  Or worse, his eyes might get caught and never leave. "Need?  No.  But I could write an epic about the wants."  That sounded better in his head. "I actually didn't expect to run into you here.  Or anyone, really." 
Alright "Xavier," I'm going to list to you a few things you can do now, since you're backed into a corner.  I mean, how are you really going to explain why you decided to stop by?  Option one: you can try to do the small talk.  You know, see if she ever casually visits places where she died, or how about that weather.  I will say, that's probably the safest route.  Option two: you can ask her out.  Horribly insubordinate, forward, and stupid.  Doesn't need to be a date.  You can play it off as wanting to know The Order better.  Remember she doesn't HAVE to eat or drink, but she can.  And put your appetite to shame, if she wanted to.  Coffee or a walk might do.  She'll say no.  She's busy, and despite never having seen evidence that she's not single, she is undoubtedly taken.  I mentioned it's stupid and forward, but it does show interest.  It has that one small thing.  Option number three, and this one's my personal favorite: you spill the beans, all of them if you want.  You tell her who you really are, maybe talk about the night she gave you lycanthropy to prove it.  And if you really want to be a sop, you can follow this by option dos.  I admit, it doesn't play into the heroic scheme we have going, but boy it's a lot simpler.  And who knows, it might even be better.
"Xavier," She went on, "are you sure?  Why did you stop by then?"
Xavier felt those cold cheeks blister with heat.  Don't you even think about guising that with magic.  Blushing is cute, it's endearing, it shows meekness, and maybe that will separate you from all the horny jackasses. "Uhh," Oh, don't go 'uhhh, doy' for too long.  You'll wear out the cuteness and use up all her time.  Have some respect for her time. "I guess since you're here, maybe I could have your ear for a while?  I want you to know I'm not a bad guy.  The Order doesn't have to worry about me." It's like you've never spoken to anyone before.  But I like this Order angle.  It shows you can be business, maybe even shows that you can keep it in your pants. "I'm not really using the leave, so maybe, if you wanted, that is if you're not busy." Calm down.  Your face feels like blacktop on a July noon.  Man up.  The cute part is over. "You know what I'm trying to say.  I'd like to talk to you.  Somewhere other than here, where the cold is fighting me." You did it, almost rudely toward the end, but you did it.  Now brace for rejection, because you just spewed the most ridiculous string of sentences out of your gob.  It might have also helped if you had the spine to make eye contact.
"Are you asking me out?" In a tone just as emotionless as that desk. "You know, that's unprofessional."
"No," He stumbled.  His thoughts held in place, perhaps not wanting to jinx him this far in. "I just wanted to, well, talk and . . . yeah.  I don't know what else to call it."
She thought for a moment, and it looked like she really was considering. "Does the phone in your apartment still work?"
He hadn't checked, but he knew he was still paid up. "Yeah." I'm sorry.  This is cold.  She's about to say 'If you just wanted to talk, you could've used that.' This is gonna sting to hear it from her, though.
"I'll call you later.  Not today, I'm busy today.  But one day this week, before your leave is over, we'll get together and talk." She wasn't smiling physically, but there was something in her tone and in her eyes that seemed coy.  She walked past him to the door and slapped his shoulder on the way out. "Don't be so nervous." 
And with that, she left him in the cold.
Three
He sighed and rubbed his face.  He couldn't see it, but he knew it had to be as red as the blood making it so.  That went better than I thought.  Bridging the gap between you two.  Very good.  Now all you have to do is bring out that sincere charm.  I mean, she liked you enough when you were Cecil, didn't she?  How can she think any less of you now that you're a walking pile of lies and secrets?  He took a few minutes to cool down.
When the red in his face dissipated, it left his skin feeling worn and colder than before.  He left the Sentinel with a sense of regret, as if he had just derailed everything he had ever worked for.  But another part of him was exuberant.  He wouldn't call his exchange with Salina a smooth operation, but it hadn't fallen apart exactly either.  Still, he had his nerves to contend with now.
He cannot shake the feeling that Siegfried ratted him out, and a mixture of fury and delight brews at the thought.
Four
Did she mean she'd call him today and make plans this week, or would the call and the plans both happen another day?
The phone in his apartment indeed still works.  He sits down on the couch and impatiently knits his fingers in anticipation.  He then gets up and paces for a few minutes.  Then he sits again.  There is a book on the table, Of Moons and Dreams by a Marser Vanderbrook.  He picks it up, needing something to keep his mind off phantom phones ringing.
Hey, he thinks to himself, do you even know how healthy this is?  It's about time you started making a move.  But she isn't interested in me like that, he reflects back at himself, only humoring me because I'm hiding something.  He closes the book over his thumb and scrolls through memories.  He opens the book again and creases the second page.  A Sir Winsome is rallying knights to go into battle with a cult of Orion zealots.  So what about her intimidates you so much?  You overthink some things, and I'll have you know that that is slave labor.  He grins.  Let's be optimistic for just a second, please.  Say the two of you do hit it off, what happens then?  Hodge was right, they don't turn anybody into a werewolf that they think is nice.  If you want to be one of them again, you'll have to let them know who you are.  Xavier bites his lip and drifts away for just an instant, thinking about Salina's warm embrace.  I'd have to have a definite way of proving who I am.  Nothing I did in my time here is very identifying.  Everyone seems to know who I was here and what I did.  But maybe if I talked about that night my sickness went away, and I found her in the woods with her . . . the second person, demeaning side finished, Yes, her gorgeous silver fur, book in hand, a single apple tree looming overhead.  The moon had been waning that night, I think.
He went back to the book.  Sir Winsome was powerless without a reader, after all.  And those zealots had gone too far, hadn't they?  He was still nervous, still letting that voice of reason, and often cynicism, run his gambit.  But it was his own voice, and sometimes its counsel was sound, if not a bit disheartening.  Let it speak.  He'd glean from it what applied, as he always had done.
Five
The night ran on, and the book grew shorter.  After a few twists and turns, Sir Winsome's son was close to the bottom of a mystery his father had uncovered decades beforehand.  There had been betrayal, love, and revelation.  All the ingredients for a sound story, if done right, and this was.  It even had heart, for what it was worth, and a great dynamic between Winsome Jr and his brother-in-arms Sir Cedric, real mismatched best friends.  Chapter sixteen ended with Jr learning that his superiors had been covering up details about cult functions and allegiances, and Xavier hadn't the energy left to go on with it.  The forty-five pages to the end would have to wait; he was too tired, and it was too late.
He put the book down on the table and added a new crease.  He spent his whole afternoon on that couch and most of the night.  Xavier wiped his face, expecting a layer of crust to flake off, and twisted his back.  The joints connecting his thighs to his pelvis popped when he stood, and the ones in his elbows gave a similar snap.  Mortal bodies did that, he had to remind himself.  He looked at the phone again, no missed calls, no calls caught either.  It would be another day, she had forgotten, or never meant to at all.  He stopped the petty voice inside before it could wind around the idea and turned the living room light off.  Time for bed.
He brushed his teeth with his left hand, a habit he made years ago, though he was naturally right handed.  The paste tasted like mint, just as most of it had back on Earth.  He could only hope it worked as well as or better than its terran version.  He was young, but he hadn't had many chances to brush over the last month.  Being mortal had its worries.  He read that gum disease was closely related to heart disease.  After what his heart had been through, he didn't think he wanted to put it under any extra stress.  Then again, perhaps with magic it didn't matter.
He stripped down to his underpants, shut off the bathroom light, and stumbled into his bedroom and into bed.  It wasn't until he was in bed that he realized he could have lit his way with a false flame, a cold one even.  No point in dwelling on that now.  He rolled over and stared at the alarm.  It was a quarter past midnight, but when did he want to get up?  It would be nice, he thought, if he could set the clock to "coma." He supposed he might as well burn through this leave, so he didn't touch the clock.  Sleeping in would be nice.  It would kill time.
As the clock's digital face ticked from twelve-nineteen to twelve-twenty and Xavier was about lost to the living world, the phone rang.  His eyes widened and his legs began kicking without conscious input from his brain.  Sheets whiffled about, and he kicked the nightstand.  Pain shot up through his leg, while he hobbled furiously out of his room.  The door of his room was shut, and he came close to blasting the thing away with a spell or two, or twenty.  Someone below him told him to keep it down and started banging the ceiling under him.
He grabbed the phone, panting and begging that she was still on the other side. "Hello." He said.  The pain in his smashed toes had settled to a sore throb.  Please don't be a telemarketer.
"Xavier," Salina replied, "I didn't wake you, did I?"
"No, no.  I sleep like a rock.  The phone never would've been able to get through to me."
"Yeah, I had to kick you to wake you up in Morphus." The person below him stopped their scolding. "So," She went on, "sorry I called so late.  I thought I had your number on my own phone, but I didn't.  So I had to go back to the Sentinel to get it."
"You didn't have to do all that for me.  I know you're busy."
"I am, but I have the next six hours somewhat free.  You know, when everyone else in the world sleeps.  Also I would feel bad not giving you a call, when I said I would."
"Thank you, Salina.  You're too kind to me." You always have been.
"Well, even if you weren't asleep, I'm sure you're exhausted, just getting back from the mission and all.  So how about we go to Medister's on Friday.  I mean Fairday.  After I'm done sorting names.  Around eight?  My treat."
"Sure but," Medister was expensive, "I'll pay.  It wouldn't feel right of me to let you pay.  You don't even have to eat.  It would make me feel selfish."
She laughed. "Xavier, I have a food budget that hasn't been touched in decades.  I can handle it.  It's also an order."
He wasn't happy about her footing the bill, but he didn't want to push the point.  Hopefully she'd let him pick up the tip. "I'll see you there."
"Sure." She stopped for that moment of silence that always seems to precede goodbyes. "Oh," She started, breaking that moment, "how's your head?  You had a nasty bump on it."
He had forgotten about the head injury and the afternoon she had spent talking to him at the hospital.  He couldn't care less about the bruise that had long since passed.  Xavier was lifted by her effort. "That healed up weeks ago.  But thank you for asking.  And thanks again for saving me from a worse bump."
"It was nothing, really." That pause again. "So I'll see you Fairday."
"Yeah." He wanted to add more, but it would be too easy to say something stupid.
"Take care, Xav."
"You too, Salina." And the line died.
Xavier put the phone back on its dock and leaned against the wall.  His face was red and hot again, his fingertips cold and numb, and his legs wobbly.  Never mind the throbbing toes.  They might as well be a million miles away from his pain receptors.  He had an urge to do a little dance, make a little noise.  But after how his neighbor reacted to him getting his toes stubbed, Xavier thought it would be just as well to go back to bed with a smile.

Chapter Twenty-One

One
The sun was up, halfway between dawn and noon, but what light it had to pour into his room was broken by a nearby building.  It made him feel like he was living in a kind of wall or crawlspace, but he didn't exactly hate the feeling of it.  He slid out of bed and held his toes up to the light.  They were sore and bruised.  He pulled the injured foot up over one knee and covered the discolored flesh with his hands.  He thought of soothing, of white magic, of regeneration, and energy flowed from his fingertips to his toes.  When he took his hands away, he saw unmarred skin and nails just as they should be.
Healing aside, he sleeps in, and his stomach reminds him he's running late.  He walks out to the fridge, opens it hopefully, and stares at the bare shelves inside.  He wonders why he even has the thing plugged in and shuts the door.
He takes a shower and throws on his civilian clothes.  He's not sure if he can think of it like that yet.  He grabs his keys and a coat and steps into the hall.  There is a fair sum of money in his pocket, and he walks down the corridor, pulling it out and counting it.  He stops at the last door on the left.  To the right is an exit door to the stairs.  There is no working elevator; that is to be added later, supposedly.
He knocks on the door and is met with the wonderful reverberation of hollow wood.  Something flashes over the peephole.  Locks are undone, and a gray wolfman in a pink robe opens the door.
"Xavier!" The man went with a grin, arms outstretched, and hem of the robe sliding up over his knees.  Xavier was glad it didn't go any higher. "How've you been?  I haven't seen you in a while."
"Fine, fine, Borsi." Xavier recounted the bills.
"Still working at the hospital?" Borsi brought his arms to his chest and clamped them over the robe's opening. "Annah's robe.  I didn't have time to fetch mine."
Xavier half-nods and bounces his eyebrows. "Right." The money is counted. "No, I found a better job.  Has me running a lot though." He holds the money out. "Unless the price has been jacked, that should cover three more months of rent and utilities."
Borsi takes the troths and ruffles them quickly. "That'll do." There is a creak behind him.
"Who are you talking to?" Annah asks.  She's wearing Borsi's robe.  Its own blue hem is down to her shins.  She's a lighter gray than her husband, and she reminds Xavier of Salina. "Oh, hi Xavier." She leans against her husband's back and rests her long chin over his shoulder.
"Hello, Annah." Xavier looks away from her and to Borsi.  He doesn't want to look at her any longer, too similar.  He nods again and starts inching toward the steps.
Borsi understands the gesture. "Well Xavier," he goes and pecks his wife's fuzzy cheek with a kiss, "don't wait another three months to come and stop by.  We should all go out to dinner sometime.  The three of us, and your girlfriend too, if you want."
"I'd like that." Xavier replies, and he means it.
Two
He stops at a diner to get out of the cold and to fulfill the wishes of his stomach.  The sign inside by the checkout says he can seat himself, so he finds a nice alcove by the window and flips through a menu on the table.  He looks for something to catch his eye, but he'll default to bacon and eggs with some juice or milk, if he doesn't find anything.  There is a searing sound coming from the kitchen, and the smell of cooked eggs and sausage steams out of a window between the kitchen and dining areas.  A line of steel coffee brewers is against the wall by the bar.  There is a self-serve salad bar to the side of this with a decrepit elf scooping tomatoes onto a plate.  On the other side of the coffee station is an assortment of glass and plates that appear to be clean.  A well-endowed ogress is taking the orders of a novian family closer to the entrance.  The wife is doing the ordering, so as to not let her husband talk to the balcony with legs.  Xavier thinks the ogress is a lot like the woman who worked at Steaks For Goodness Sakes back in Redora, or at least what she might have looked like twenty years ago.
He decided to go with an omelette, three egg.  It seemed like an age had passed since he had one of those.  A tall elven busboy, as gaunt and pale as death, cleaned up a table in the second dining section and tossed a glare at Xavier.  With the way the elf's face was structured, it might just as well have been a greeting smile.  To be sure, Xavier reaches across the room with his mind and into the more secluded second area where the elf is.  He prods at the elf's mind while innocently gazing out the window at a flurry.  The elf pauses gathering up dishes in his waste basket and stares ahead, eyes glazed over.  The elf thinks Xavier is someone else.  Xavier releases the elf.  The boy has been getting flak for being slow.
He sits and considers why, when there are half a dozen customers, no one can get their drink earlier than the rest of the food.  He can't wrap his mind around how pouring a glass of juice could take five minutes, but then again he isn't very thirsty.  He looks out at the street and the glass buildings.  Everything is white.  Back on Earth cities had a hard time letting the snow lay, but that might have had something to do with salt.  They didn't salt here, because no one drove, at least not here.
Three
A Rottweiler man walks in, as the family gathers up their leftovers and heads for the counter.  The man sits at the bar for a few moments, observes the staff and Xavier, and walks over to the novian's table.  He's wearing a gray down jacket and beige cargo pants.  There is a pack of cigarettes hanging from the front pocket of the jacket, a brand called Epsilon.  He sits down across from Xavier, folds his scarred hands neatly in front of him, and draws on a face without expression. "Enjoying your breakfast?" He asks and lays his hands flat on the table.  There is a shaved patch of fur on the back of his left hand which form the letters "RB."
Xavier puts down his fork.  He doesn't recognize the man at his table. "It's fine." The novian replies and takes a swig from his quarter full glass. "It could use some more mushrooms, I guess.  But I like it."
The man looks at the plate.  The skin around his eyes wrinkles. "That's a good bit of food for someone your size, isn't it?  That's as much as I would eat."
Who is this guy?  He can't be on to me. "Well, I was hungry." He glances out the window, wondering if this new friend doesn't have more friends waiting outside. "I'm Xavier Pontius." He lays down the fork and extends his right palm.  He once read that offering your left hand to shake with was rude. "And you are?"
The man grabs the novian's hand firmly but briefly. "No, you're not.  There is no Xavier Pontius." He reaches into his jacket and withdraws a slip of paper. "But my employer is impressed with your skills." The man slides the slip across the table, careful to avoid a splotch of water. "He's willing to offer you a rate three times higher than what you're being paid now."
Xavier examines the paper.  It's filled with contact instructions, and he happens to recognize the area code on a phone number. "This is from Daskin.  This is from Governor Malachi, isn't it?  How did you people find me?"
The man glances around. "Just think about it.  He's willing to offer you special privileges." He leaned forward and took the paper. "I'll keep this.  I hear you have a fine memory." He stands up and holds the paper out in front.  He begins talking again in a much louder voice. "Oh, I see.  I have to go up Main until I get to Hill Street.  Then make a right onto Hill, not a left.  Thanks, buddy!" He shoots Xavier a cold look and puts away the paper.  He walks out.
Xavier finishes his breakfast and leaves a good tip.  He means to tell The Order about that offer, but he doesn't want to do it by phone.  Fairday is tomorrow, so it probably wouldn't hurt to wait to tell Salina.  Or would it hurt?  He had a good hunch he wasn't the only one getting recruitment offers.
Four
After stopping at a bookstore, he returns to his apartment.  The history here is different, and he wants to learn it.  After all, he had technically dropped out of school.
When he gets back to his apartment with his arm load of books, he finishes reading Sir Winsome Jr's story.  It all comes down to Sir Cedric lying in a pool of his own blood with Winsome holding him and not saying a word.  Cedric retells a thousand lives' worth of stories that the two shared on their journeys, and Winsome does not interrupt.  Even when death comes and breaks all vows and puts man's ideals to the blade, Winsome holds onto his gallant ways, having been taught to let a dying man have his words.  When Cedric finishes his flashbacks and good talk, Winsome claims all those efforts won't be in vain, and the two exchange farewells.  The book ends with Cedric dying and Winsome being left to finish the work his father started, alone.
Xavier likes the ending.  He had been a martyr once, and it had been Salina who took up his sword.  But at the same time, the ending is hollow and lacks closure.  He goes to the front cover and inspects the lamellar paper.  Just as he suspected, below the title and above the author's name is "A Moonscape Trilogy Book.  Number One." That was fine and good, and it justified another trip into town.
He goes down to pick up the rest of the series and then heads over to an electronics store to blow some of his credit.  He buys a TV and spends a great deal of time convincing the salesman that he can indeed carry it home without help.
He installs the idiot box in front of his table of books.  Local stations will have to do.  He's only interested in the news anyway.  Xavier has a feeling that if he started watching a purely entertaining show, most of the context would be lost to him.
When the news comes on, he is delighted to hear it is just like it had been back home.  A murder here, a robbery there, why carrots might actually be bad for your eyes, and inaccurate weather.
A report comes on about a newly discovered comet.  An astronomer in a Dying Day metal band T-shirt is speaking to the reporter about a fast moving comet they've dubbed Ganymede Seven, a name which makes Xavier wish scientists would be a little more creative.  According to the astronomer, the comet has a very long ellipse and hasn't passed Destursha since before written language.  He projects that it will pass the planet in early fall.  The reporter signs off, claiming that his station will keep up with all the latest news.
He lets the TV run a while afterwards.  He isn't interested in any of the programming, but it's good time-killing noise, and he loves every moment it destroys.

Chapter Twenty-Two

One
Xavier is standing outside of Medister's Ronian Restaurant on the corner of Hamlet and Orson on that cold Fairday evening.  His new phone, a device he would claim to be the most nerve-racking clock on the market, tells him it is seven fifty five.  He whispers the time and something about all the soft "f"s makes his mouth feel dry, or maybe it's something else.  He is wearing part of his posh disguise.  Medister's is good, but he knows it isn't grand, and he doesn't want to overdress.  A male and female pair of ogres passes him to the door.  The man holds the door, and she remarks what a kind gentleman he is.  Xavier wonders how many couples are inside.  The snowy air wraps around him and chills his core, relieving him of some of the nervousness.  His mind tells him he ought to calm down, since he is becoming a great wizard.  Soon a day will come, he knows, when he'll be able to literally move mountains.  Why then, should a date derail his nerves so severely?
Salina arrives walking up from Orson.  She is wearing a robin's egg blue blouse, the counterpart of his own attire.  For this Xavier is glad, as he believes he would be choking on his own tongue had she worn a dress.  She approaches him with a nod and a smile.  She is wearing lavender perfume that he thinks must be overwhelming to her.
He nearly trips getting the door for her.  She waits for him to hold the door for a departing family of novians before approaching the matre d and confirming that there are only two.  Xavier follows Salina, who follows a waitress, to their table.  They have a corner booth, the seats of which are high and thick, rather isolating.  The waitress asks if they would like anything to drink, and Xavier ask for a root beer.  Salina asks for the same.  When the waitress departs, Salina explains that taste is the only redemption in food for her.
They thumb through menus.  When the server leaves to let them decide, Salina opens the conversation. "Let's get this out in the open." She says. "There is no Xavier Pontius on record, and no one living in Bellshire or had lived there can say they know a man with that name.  But Hodge says you're sincere.  Or at least enough so that you'd rather leave it at that than cover yourself up with more lies.  And you know what, I'm fine with that, too."
He shrugs and his hands feel cold.
"There.  That's out there.  So what's on your mind?"
Two
Things picked up after the waitress wrote down what they'd come to eat.  It started off casual, Xavier asking her about what she enjoys doing outside The Order, and Salina asking him about why he hadn't ordered a glass of wine.  Their food was served, and Salina's plate rivaled Xavier's.
"So," She went and deepened the talk, "you're not a Chrissenian, and no one can place you with the sympathizers.  So, why join us?"
"Oh!  That reminds me.  I was eating at a diner the other day, and a man approached with a paper of contact information.  It looked like Daskin area codes.  I think Malachi was trying to recruit me."
"Yeah, I've heard about them doing that.  They're trying to sow dissent.  I have a feeling Malachi will be the hardest to overthrow, and that's before taking his fortified city into account." She bit into her steak and a piece wedged between her teeth.  She dislodged it with a claw. "Sorry, I don't eat often.  And back when I needed to eat, I never went to a place this nice."
"You don't need to tell me.  I'd do the same thing if I had claws."
"So what did they offer you?"
"He claimed I'd get my current pay tripled, and that I would get 'special privileges.' Whatever that means."
She leaned forward, her chest pressing against the fabric of her shirt. "But you didn't take it, and I can't see why." She noticed that, while he was using some effort to not stare at her breasts, he was keeping his eyes on her face. "Can you tell me then?"
He wanted to say something about his allegiance to his old friends, wanted to say it was because of her.  That was five steps ahead of where he was now, and he wasn't about to blow it all. "I like you guys and what you stand for.  It's hard to say, but there are some things more valuable than money."
Salina was glad he didn't spew out some romantics, as was expected.  It was obvious to her that he was interested, and in her experience, men had two minds: the upper mind and the lower mind.  Both tended to be stupid, but the lower seemed dumber most of the time.  She'd seen them have the upper take control just long enough to let lust take over, when it thought the game was won.  She was willing to humor his mind, try to get a read on him that way.  Men were horrifically stupid about a woman's awareness.  Men thought exposed cleavage was magic and that a woman had no idea how much skin she was showing.  A tryst was fun every decade or so, if you could stomach the shallowness of it, but it was good to have someone to talk to.  She knew he wasn't aware of it, but his ability to keep his eyes up was earning him more credit than his words. "All right.  So what do you plan on doing after this is all over?  You probably don't want to go back to the hospital.  Honestly though, you would make a fine Leviathan."
It took some effort to not say he wanted to go with her and the rest, that he would follow her to any world she would take him to. "Well, janitor work is off the table.  That's far too dangerous for me." They both smirked at this.
Three
She finishes her plate before he is half done with his and slugs away her drink.  She places it all aside and wipes her mouth with a napkin.  When he finishes his, they go on to talk about their childhoods.  It is the first time he'd heard her story firsthand, and he's glad to hear it.
"The Lord giveth, and the Lord taketh away." She said, leaning back and pressing her golden hair into the booth. "Except when something good happens.  Then it's witchcraft." She explained that the people of her community wouldn't accept her recovery when she became a werewolf.  No one would believe that her legs came back to life without satanic intervention, and they ostracized her.  They claimed that the fire that had taken away most of her loved ones had been divine and meant to kill her as well.  When they came to finish what the fire started and found that they could not harm her, they went after what few remained. "So I ran away, hoping they would leave those ones alone, the ones I still had left."
There are a hundred comforting phrases that come to him at this.  Ninety or so of them would reveal his true self.  The rest are heartless shamblings of recycled words.  So he chooses what is usually best.  Quiet sympathy.
She asks him about those he might have left behind, and he responds with as much detail as he dares. "I had a girlfriend." Go on, say her name.  Salina doesn't know it. "Her name was Cathrine, and I was willing to believe anything she told me.  Heck, I'd even slap excuses onto the things she said for her."
"Do you miss her?"
"Perhaps a bit.  For a long time the missing was strong.  Which is silly, when I think about it.  I wouldn't be surprised if she had a lineup of replacements waiting for the moment I tripped up." He looks away from Salina.  Something inside tells him that talking about old flames while looking into her eyes conveys a want.  While that is true, he isn't ready to voice it. "But I do miss my family.  They always goaded me, when I made a bad decision, made it seem like my fault.  But I know that was just their way of trying to put me on a better path." He feels comfortable looking at her again. "I've lost all track of them, and wonder what they're up to.  I also miss . . ." He comes close to saying 'driving to town to see my friends' but shortens the thought. ". . . my friends."  He hopes she didn't catch the pause, but he is glad there are a million or so things to fill in the blank with other than Earthen means of transport.
Four
They go for a while.  He tells her how he had always been prone to bronchitis and infections growing up, to the confusion of clerics.  Whenever the waitress stops by, Salina waves her on.  They share their interests, and Xavier is reminded of the night by the road.  He wonders if she had cared about the boy Cecil had been and if he shouldn't be forthright with her now.  It makes him think of the mine and how they abandoned him.  He had been her responsibility, her pup to teach, and they let him go.  Of course, now that he thinks of it, they had told him to come with.  What would've happened had he turned and gone with The Order?  Certainly he wouldn't have set Mara and Gabe free, and he wouldn't have been driven to kill those dragons, creatures which needed no help in going extinct.  He might not be here, eating with the woman who had taken him away from home, as mortal as when he had gone with them.  Then again, they might all be dead.
He begins asking questions that have bothered him for a long time.  He starts by asking her things anyone would ask.  She explains the language similarity.  Then he asks, "Are there other werewolves that didn't come here?"
To this she replies, "At least two.  Friends of Arthur's.  One was named Barnabas, and he was dangerous.  You wouldn't know the movie we got the idea from, but we managed to trap him in cooling magma.  Enough that his strength wasn't great enough to break it.  But who knows, he could be free now.  I hope he isn't.  The other I can't remember, but he never caused trouble, so we never looked for him."
He asks another. "What did The Order do back on Earth?"
"We mostly kept track of imbalances between our two worlds through Siegfried.  Used his insight to make good investments.  Became obscure millionaires.  And since the way here seems like a one-way road, we're looking at more conventional methods.  We rarely intervened in wars, though we did a few times.  Never openly.  People would've wanted to use us as weapons."
He asks about the different peoples of Earth, and she tells him they were all human, which is a word in Destursha that can refer to any of the sentient races.  She elaborates and explains that people all looked like The Order members, some shorter, some taller, and with many different skin tones, but no one was drastically different by and large. She adds, "Arthur also took it upon himself to spread lore.  He would go to remote villages and towns, howl at the moon -we only had one- and beat on doors.  He made myths about werewolves, yetis, and sasquatches." She explains what those were. "Hodge gave him an earful about it, but Arthur never hurt anyone.  So we let it go."
Something else rises to his mind, a question he wouldn't be able to go to sleep with. "You were the one that gave Cecil lycanthropy, right?  Hodge tells me it isn't something you can earn.  Why did you give it to him?"
She glances instinctively at her shoulder, searching for the relic Cecil left behind. "I was on my way to a place called Maine, and I happened to be going through the woods he was in.  It looked like he was cutting up roasting sticks, but it was dark.  Clearly he forgot to do that earlier.  Anyway, he had this flashlight that he dropped, and I stopped to hear him react.  Teenagers, especially boys, always seem to like swearing and throwing a fit when they're away from their parents or with friends.  So I waited for the tantrum.  That way I could chalk it up to how great mankind was.  You know, just being cynical.  But he didn't yell, and he didn't blame the world for his clumsiness.  He just accepted what he did and went to fix it.  He mumbled something about dropping it right there, but that was it.  I thought that was very rare.  That's when I came out of hiding, made the offer, and he accepted.  After that I decided to stay in the area and make sure he didn't get into trouble.  I had no idea he was a descendent of Siegfried's.  Needless to say, and I'll say it anyway, I never made it to Maine.  Since Cecil becoming a werewolf is basically what got us here."
Xavier is flabbergasted.  He had believed that night had been nothing more than whimsy on her part.  If she had a reason, even a small one, then perhaps this quest wasn't needed. "Because he didn't throw a fit?"
"I can't tell if it was a poor decision or the best one I ever made.  He was the youngest of us, and somehow he was the first werewolf to die.  He shouldn't have gotten killed for us.  It was our fault we walked into a trap."
Xavier pulls the curtain from his veil for just a moment to say, "He died because he wanted to save you."
"I know, but I still wish he was here now." And that hits him.
The waitress stops by again and asks if either would like dessert.  Salina says she'd eat if he did, but Xavier adds that some people had to watch their figure.  To this she rolls her eyes and asks for the check.  When it comes Salina makes sure she gets to it before he, and with her longer arms, it was no contest.  He asks her if she'll at least let him cover the tip, and she allows him.  He remarks that next time, he would like to cover the meal, and she doesn't disagree.  It isn't the idea of him paying that makes him happy though, it's the idea of her consenting to another outing.
Five
He walks her out and holds the doors again.  He then offers to walk her home, but she declines.  Not that she doesn't enjoy his company, she says, but that the walk is a good distance.  They exchange goodbyes, and Xavier does all he can to show how sad he is to see her go so soon.
He permits himself to watch her walk away, until she rounds a corner.  Again he is glad she dressed modestly, but even so her gait intoxicates him.  She looks back only once to send a smile and wave, and he reciprocates both before she disappears.  To him she is beautiful in a way that neither his mind nor manhood can understand.  There are plenty of women with minds to talk to and fine forms to look upon, but he knows there is more to her.  It is such a dumb thing to feel, so sappy and adolescent.  But the heart wants what it wants, and one can't argue it.
He stands there in the cold for a time, amazed that his face isn't red and hot, before returning to his books.  When he gets home he changes into clothes he isn't afraid to stain and sits down on his couch.  It isn't late yet, but he is tired.  Tiredness, however, is not the same as sleepiness, and he knows that if he went to bed all he would do is toss and turn and think about her.
After some reading, he eventually fades, wondering how quickly he can burn through his leave.

Chapter Twenty-Three

One
Midasday rolled around.  He had spent a while the morning after the dinner pacing in front of his phone, wondering if it was protocol to call Salina.  That applied to dates, but had that really been a date?  After some thought, he decided he wanted to talk to her anyway, practices be damned.
When the leave was about up, he went to the Sentinel.
Arthur is sitting at the front desk with his feet propped up and a tune whistling from his mouth.  Xavier can't place it, but he thinks it might be an anthem of some sort, definitely one from earth.  The tall British man regards the novian passively and continues whistling.  His red hair has grown long in the back, forming a curly mullet that should never be worn without a flannel jacket, a ball cap, and some stonewashed jeans.  There is a stack of yellow envelopes with red seals on the desk next to the feet.  Arthur cuts his whistling, pulls an envelope free of the pile and tosses it to Xavier without ceremony.
"Your new assignment.  It's important but simple." Arthur's British accent might have actually grown thicker.
"Anything I should know?"
"I don't know.  Have fun?  Go get 'em, tiger?" He clasps his hands behind his head, the image of relaxation. "Just get to it.  And do me a favor, burn it when you're done reading.  Precaution and all.  And don't read it here.  Go somewhere discrete." He begins waving his arms in a dismissive manner.  He looks beyond the novian at the door, seeing no one coming. "Lover boy."
Xavier leaves and wonders if he ever would've gotten along with Arthur and decides probably not.
Two
Just as Arthur asked, Xavier reads his new assignment in a bathroom stall at a Sesame's.  And for added flare, Xavier flushes the burnt ashes.
He goes back to his apartment and grabs supplies, clothes mostly since he doesn't keep anything else.  From here he'll head to the train station, and he makes sure to grab his nice getup.
His new assignment is not dangerous but likely just as stressful.  He is to head south to the port town of Ares, which is at the southern tip of the northernmost continent, Atticus.  From there he will sail to Sensen on the southeast continent of Barimede.  Then he will have to walk from there to Achron, which is a southern ally city of The Order's.  There he is to be the army's representative along with a few others not mentioned by name in the orders.  He'll delegate, he'll be an ambassador, and he will hopefully help attain the aid of their southern allies against the governors.
Xavier approaches the station, and with his yearlong pass, boards a train headed south.

Three
Toting his supplies in a knapsack, Xavier got off his train in Ares that same day.  Down here by the coast the air is warm and balmy with an edge of salt to it.  The streets are paved with sandstone and packed with merchants.  He can smell exotic spice shops before even leaving the dock, and in the distance is a cacophony of vendors shouting to drag in customers.  There are tourists and travelers occupying every square inch of walking space, and he jostles into a few.
The train station is on the north side of the town, and his ship is waiting at the south end, where land meets sea.  Xavier knows how to swim, but he has never been on a boat before, and the idea of sailing sounds adventurous, if not a bit unsettling.  He supposes he has from this side to the other to accept having sea legs, whether he cares to or not.
As the novian pushes through the swamping crowds and is assaulted by the smell of fish, he understands the sailing might be a relief from this hectic place.  The waves lighten a bit as he approaches the docking area, and the street vendors and open market give way to brick and mortar shops.  More spice shops and specialty businesses seem to crop up, perhaps to help mask the odor of the fishermen.  There is a group of children standing at the threshold of a candle shop with what appears to be a chaperone.  They are all listening to a gaudily dressed woman explain the process of candle making and oil extraction.  Among the children is a bald orc boy, and while Xavier knows baldness is rampant among their people, he has to look away.
Farther along he comes to a fountain with benches nearby.  Knowing that's he's made good time, Xavier elects to sit for a few moments and take in the scenery.  After this part of town, there is a drop off and he wants to absorb the view.  Over the houses is a long stretch of beach that ends in a high cliff being pummeled with ocean foam.  The outcropping cliff reaches beyond the boundary of land and hangs high over the water's surface like a rocky diving board.  Xavier wonders how many people have leapt from that precipice, and the morbid thought makes him look in the other direction.  To the other side of town is a continuation of the beach.  It runs the length of the horizon and is dotted by white rocks and white birds, things oblivious to this world's troubles.  
He envies them both.
Four
The sight of empty boarding docks displeases him.  He walks down a landing and examines a passenger ship titled Amarita.  A man wearing what appeared to be burlap was toying with a length of rope hanging over the starboard side, knotting and unknotting and lashing it out as a whip.
"Ey, down 'ere!" The man shouted as if Xavier hadn't already been looking at him. "Ship don't sail till sunset.  Ye can git yer ticket then.  Samson will be back 'round four or so.  He'll get yer papers and payment!" The man hacked up some phlegm and instead of spitting it over the deck, he aimed it back at the floorboards.
"All right!" The novian replied and began walking away.  He was not interested in having a whole exchange out of shouting.  He'd probably need his voice to delegate after all.
He decided to try an old habit of his.  Xavier wanders out of the town and pulls his phone from his pocket.  It's a quarter till three.
Ares looks like a stationary village from the top of the outcropping, and the ocean seems to be a great warbling greenish blue carpet.  He sits down at the edge, letting his feet dangle over the lethal fall, and inhales the salty breeze.  Somewhere beyond the immeasurable waters is the continent of Barimede.  The ocean looks so flat, and he's up so high, and it makes him consider how far away his destination must be.  He had never traveled so far on Earth.
The air is calm all around him, and if not for the whoosh of spray below him, he would think he might be the only living thing on this side of the water.  He is waiting for the tickets, he is waiting for Salina to return the affection, and he is waiting for life to begin again.  All of this is just an elaborate setup, a lengthy and overdrawn prologue.  How he wishes she were here to sit by his side, while they watch the ocean tide.  He has been holding back for so long.  So he sits here on the precipice overlooking the glassy sea, which cares not for what troubles him, alone with his thoughts.
He reaches into his past, slipping his mind through the scar of memories he has created.  With a sigh, his heart relents and allows him into the worst times.  He marvels solemnly at how the mind works, that he could repress such sweet memories because they came up bitter.  What a great waste, he thinks.  He had hidden for two years, and what kind of statement was that?  If he had truly cared for her and for them, he would've made a conscious effort.  The past.  What a deep grave he has dug for himself.
The ground under him groans and shifts, but he does not throw up his arms and race his heart.  He doesn't squeal or jump.  Instead he only waits for it to go, for God to tell him this quest needs to end.
Then it happens, as predictable as the rising sun.  The dirt and rock he has perched on loosens and begins its plummet, and he is in freefall with it.  Face forward, Xavier watches the fast approaching white foam and the jagged rocks it washes up against.  It all seems to be happening inch by inch.
