Every foot contains enough time for him to relives a year of his life, and that isn't many.  He is still well above the port town when all his life has rolled before him.  But unlike the nightmares after his heart's failure, he sees pleasant things.  Salty air in his false white hair, he watches a stream of times he'd spent with his friends, people who would care if he became paste over the waves.  He sees Folas, running from the Sentinel in his bare feet and standing on his.  The boy seemed to think of him as a big brother, and he too would care if Xavier died here.  Then his mind settles on Salina again, as it so often did.  She seemed to have cared when Cecil died, but he wonders what it would mean to her if this Xavier form he took on was found washed up by the southern shore.  Of course, he knows once he died, the magic shaping him to this form would fade, and they would find the gory remains of Cecil Fauden, resurrected only to die again.
He thinks of the promises he's made.  He cares too much about The Order and about Mara and Gabe as well to turn in now.  Even if, he supposes, he comes to the end of all this work and finds no one there to welcome him into their arms and thank him, he wants to fulfill what he started.  With no more than a few dozen feet to the rocks, he begins slowing.  Dirt passes him and powderizes against the hungry stone.  His clothes press into his chest the way a seatbelt might, and he is caught in a softness of his own making.  He is now falling into a giant padding, the kind that you might fall all the way to the ground through, but by the time you hit earth you'd be moving too slowly to break an egg.  One might even want to jump into it again, just for fun.
He is levitating over rocks that were denied their sacrifice.  He rights himself so that his cheeks aren't feeling the resonating cold of the water below.  This is the first time he exercised his ability to make himself ignore gravity.  That was the way of things for him.  Whatever he wanted, he needed only to set his mind to it, and he could.  What a freeing concept that was to him.  It was an ideal he could remember only being meaningful as a child.  When he grew older, it seemed that the reality was that the word "can't" actually held power.  But here he was, hovering over what would have been his sudden death.
He floats back to the shore.

Five
He waits for the gravitas of what happened to sink in, for him to take a sigh of relief.  But it doesn't come, and he doesn't bother to dwell on the fact.  Back in the town the people have begun to push over the south side, readying to do all the foreign business the world can't live without.
Samson was a blue skinned ogre who sat behind what looked like a lemonade stand to sell tickets.  The ogre was nearly the size of his trade stand, the kind of muscular that looked like a sack full of watermelons.  He traded for all the ships collectively.  A ticket to Sensen there, or one up the coast to Andus, whatever floated the customer's boat.  And he assured them that that was water.
Xavier paid for his faire and was pointed the way to his ship.  It wasn't the Amarita that he'd already seen, but one called, rather lovingly, Whorl Hag.  He caught a few breaks in the crowd of people on his way to the vessel, but he was mostly being bumped into and shoved as they bottlenecked down the boarding channels.  To him it sounded as if everyone by the pier was talking to everyone else, and struggling to get anything across at that.
There were old galleons tied up around the pier, things he'd have thought would be long retired.  Great ships of wood and iron and cloth sails.  But the Whorl Hag was no such craft.  A sleek, streamlined construct of smooth metals and gas power was this ship.  Stick it on an Earthen dock, and you could sell it as normal, Xavier thought.

Chapter Twenty-Four

One
He has his own room in the galley of the ship, though it is little more than a cubbyhole.  At times like this, he's glad he is so small.  There are no windows, but the lighting is soft, not florescent, and doesn't give off an artificial and sickening buzz.  There is a cot bolted to the floor.  At the end of which is a flat, lap sized table jutting from the wall.  He lays his things out on it that he'll want for the trip, or he would, but he realizes that he forgot to bring any of his new books.  He puts his knapsack on the table.  It might make a fine pillow.
He lies down on the cot, his head resting comfortably on his pack, and stares at the steel gray ceiling.  He knows there is someone above him, lying on their own cot, and very possibly someone below him.  For an instant he feels sardined, and a tinge of claustrophobia creeps at him from the walls.  The intercom sounds in the hall outside, and a man begins talking.
"This is your captain speaking." He begins, "We've got fair waters all the way from Sensen to here.  Should be a smooth trip.  Ask an attendant if you need assistance, and enjoy your voyage on the Whorl."
Another voice came through the speakers, but this one was a woman's.  One who sounded like she could say this in her sleep with her tongue cut out. "For your safety, please remain in your cabin until we get out to sea.  In case of emergency, each room is equipped with two life vests.  Please take one if you hear a siren and see the red alarm light.  Directions on the safest course of exit are posted in each room and arrows are in each hall.  In case of sickness, please use the bags found in your cabin under the cot." She buzzed out.
He took a moment to look at the safety instructions before deciding he probably wouldn't need them.  He took a greater interest in the sickness bags, which were made of plastic and smelled like pine needles.  He supposed that might alleviate symptoms, but he wondered why it didn't go full circle to mint.  He put the bags back under the cot, and his mind caught on the idea of having to stay in the cabin.  It was one thing to be in this small space and quite another to be in here by order.  Claustrophobia again, which was followed by sleepiness.  The ship rocked gently, and he took a nap.
Two
When he wakes, the Whorl Hag is at sea, bathing in what remains of the daytime sunlight which is skipping red across the lapping waters.  He turns his phone off and takes note of the time.  There was a schedule of meals posted below the safety instructions, and dinner should be served soon, if he could only find the mess hall.
His stomach lurches as he walks down the corridor, and he wonders why that organ never learned any patience.  He walks up the hall toward the bow.  There is a T in the walkway, both ways leading to more micro rooms and branching out to all parts of the boat.  At this juncture is a paper sign nailed to the wall and covered in a glass frame.  A map is on the paper with a "you are here" red arrow pointing at this hallway on a 3D sketch.  There is a legend on the side, explaining the symbols which mean everything from engine room to captain's quarters.  The mess hall is in the floor above him and near the bow.  There is a stairwell to his right, going up to the deck and down to the final passenger lodging areas.
The top floor hallways were roomier than the ones below, and this made the air seem lighter as if the halls below had been compressing the gases.  He passed a maintenance worker, who was opening a cleaning closet and rummaging for the mop bucket.  Xavier wanted to give the man a friendly nod, a sign of comradery, but the man never turned his back.  The paint in the halls was a dull swamp green, which he imagined was easy to make look clean.  Rooms were numbered like an apartment complex.  Here on the first floor, everything was A-number.  His floor had been B, odd numbers were portside and evens on the starboard.  It was easy to find what you were looking for.
The mess hall was not labeled with a letter and a number, but had the privilege of using its name as its identifier.  It had two steel swinging doors with narrow windows by the tops.  On the left was a black framed plaque with golden letters.  It read "Dining." The right door had the same, but its plaque said "Room." Alone, Xavier thought making a plaque for that word was a bit excessive.  A red and white sticker on the nearby wall warned to not stand in front of the swingers for too long, lest you be toppled and succumb to "serious injury." Seeping under the doors and between them was chatter and the smell of food.
Three
Xavier walks in and takes in the large room.  The walls are far away, and the ceiling high, but both boast that unappealing green paint.  Off to the left is door leading to the kitchen.  On that same side but farther up is a line of sneeze-guards and heating lamps.  In the center of the ceiling is a glass sunroof, which is letting in the abysmal aura of dusk.  Up the middle of the floor are eating tables and passengers scattered about.  Most are talking to another, and the room is somewhat crowded.  Even so, Xavier thinks the people here are a paltry sum of the ones he saw boarding with him.  At the end of the room is an emergency exit.  There is also an exit to the right, which leads to the deck.  There are circular white tables with green cloths as covers and cream colored chairs surrounding.  Every table has five chairs, except for two.  There is an empty table of four chairs next to a full table of six dwarves, all of which are shouting.
The dwarves stand up and walk toward the wall of sneeze-guards.  A line forms behind them, while cooks begin setting plates of food under the warmers from behind.  Xavier notices now that each door is accompanied by two sizeable trashcans, all of which he is sure will be filled by the end of the night.  He gets into line behind a plump aemon woman, whose exposed tail is tattooed with butterflies.  More people join the line, which has now wound around a few sets of tables in a haphazard fashion.
It moves along smoothly, and when Xavier gets to the food, he sees why.  All the plates are identical.  Some fish, a piece of chicken, and an orange slice.  At the end of the food procession is an open ice cooler with the options of water, milk, and orange juice to select from.  Xavier takes a juice and his plate and starts walking along the edge of the room.  He knows he'll have to share a table with strangers, but he wants to be the first at a table, so that he can be the one welcoming newcomers instead of being the one to barge in.  In the end though, he knows the results will be that same.
He finds a vacant table near the wall by the deck exit.  He sets his food and drink down and thinks the portion is appropriate for him, were he really eating for a novian.  They're rationing.  Definitely no luxury cruise.  But he knows that he wouldn't recognize a luxury cruise even if he was eating at the captain's table on silver platters.  He sits down and pops his back on the rest of his chair.  There is plastic silverware wrapped in napkins on the cloth.  He takes out a fork and knife and begins eating.
Three people come to eat at his table.  Two are a married elven couple.  The third is an orc man who decided to sit down without the consent of anyone else at the table.  The woman, pouting over some loss, gives half her food to her husband, a man that appears to have finished her meals many times before.  Xavier tries not to look at the man.  Obesity was always unsightly, but he thinks it is particularly grotesque among the elves and aemon.  The orc eats with his elbows out, ready to jab anyone who comes too close to his food.  Xavier decides to use his third eye to inspect these nutcases.
Four
The elves are Mr. and Mrs. Oswitzen, coming back from a trip north to attend Fillia's (that's Mrs. Candus Oswitzen's sister) wedding.  They both agree she could've done better, but that "it's a start." Mr. Octavius Oswitzen is a lawyer at a firm in Achron, and the idea that he might run into these two again, even in passing, makes Xavier sigh on the inside.  Octavius believes that his wife's family is mostly rubbish and that she is lucky to have him.  Oddly enough, Candus believes the same thing about her husband.  She also believes that whoever bumped into her on the boarding plane and made her drop the candles deserves no less than to be whipped and crucified.
The orc is named Thor Allens.  He is a photographer specializing in events, such as the wedding the elves just came from.  His business cards show a picture of him snapping the perfect shot of a bride and groom at the base of a snowy mountain with pink petals swirling in the breeze.  The picture was heavily edited.  The original, Xavier knows, had no mountain and no petals.  Thor has an unhealthy obsession with photographing children.  He wonders continually how he could get away with a few more shots, how to make a nice little collection without getting jailed.  He thinks he'll figure it out someday, but he won't risk it until he does.  In fact, he tries to avoid direct contact with children, makes a great cover.
After being thoroughly disgusted by his eating partners, Xavier steps out of the extra presence of his special vision.  He ponders if he can toss these three overboard.  He's certain he can get away with it, if he throws his magical weight around a bit.  But there would be too many loose ends, too many people who would be held responsible.
Five
He goes out to the deck and breathes in the ocean air.  It isn't as rank as it had been at port, and for that he is thankful.  The crew is tending to navigation, and a few other passengers are about to stretch their legs.
He leans over the railing, if only just, and looks behind the ship.  There is no sign of land that he can discern, which he knows means little.  Still though, from here the whole world might be nothing but saltwater and sea creatures.  This could be the only vessel, the very throne of sentient life over simple waves and animals.  For a moment, he wishes that it was, that the world could really be so simple.
An aemon man that looks a good deal like his acquaintance Felix (that is the iconic devil) leaves the company of a group huddled in the center of the deck and stands next to Xavier.  Xavier knows small talk is coming, knows it as sure as he knows they will find land again, but still he can wish it won't be so.  He has never understood the casual stranger that delights in engaging others.  Why would you, when you were already amongst friends?  He readies to answer what will surely come.  Such trivial things as "Is this your first time to Barimede?  Business or pleasure?  Have you ever sailed before?" And he wonders what kind of aches he'll have if this man wants to engage in politics.
The first question comes as predictably as can be. "Have you ever been to Barimede?" The man asks almost to the ocean, and if it doesn't sound flirtatious, then perhaps purely derelict.
Xavier hopes he is not being hit on.  He more or less had the idea that people could gauge orientation, and he didn't think anything about himself would make the aemon get the wrong idea.  But jumping to conclusions wouldn't solve anything, and it was just as likely that the man was smooth talking a sales pitch, maybe trying to stick Xavier in a pyramid scheme or some beach front timeshare.  He forgets that a question was asked of him.
"Have you ever been?" The man repeats, not to the ocean but to the novian.
There was a part of him that wished ignoring the man would cause him to leave.  Xavier bit the bullet. "Nope."
"Beautiful place." The aemon continues, "Rolling green fields on one side of the continent, and snowcapped mountains on the other.  And everything else in between.  The variety is spectacular."
"I'll find out soon enough." He regretted saying that.  Xavier knew it was fodder for the man to feed on.  Gabby types could run on with even the smallest amount of push.
"I'm sure you won't be disappointed.  So tell me, are you traveling with anyone else?  The view is best taken in pairs, if you ask me." He imperceptibly inched closer to the novian.
"I'm meeting people." He lamented believing it would be inappropriate to ask the aemon to get lost.  Something about that seemed rude.
The aemon's upper lip lifted in a bizarre top row smile. "My name is Ansel.  I own a fabric chain in Barimede called Silk Tapestry.  I've been trying to open up shops in other regions, but it's proving difficult." He reached into a pocket and withdrew a card.  He held it in front of Xavier, which is to say he held it over the side of the deck. "I should show you to my store.  There's one not far from port, and you look like the kind that dresses finely but also enjoys comfort."
"I'm Xavier Fau- Pontius.  Almost said my middle name." He took the card. "I'm a hired gun, mostly bodyguard.  Got important business to get to after we land."
The man stepped back. "Oh, I see." But he persisted. "A dangerous type, eh?  I like that.  Have you ever, you know . . . ?"
"I've killed.  Is that what you're asking?" People are the same here.  It's like he's asking a cop if he ever had to shoot someone.
The aemon turned and leaned his back against the railing.  He kept on wearing that odd grin.  It was clear that he didn't think Xavier was really a paid killer. "If that's your job, I guess no one can blame you.  Or shouldn't at least.  You're just a weapon, right?  You're not the one pulling the trigger."
The elves, Xavier decided, were free of punishment.  But he thought now, having been pushed by this pompous entrepreneur, that Mr. Thor Allens will not be given so much mercy.  Ansel was wrong about one thing; Xavier was the gun and the executor both.  He didn't answer the elf this time, and to his amazement, Ansel returned to the group he came from.

Chapter Twenty-Five

One
Sitting on the cot in his room, the man in a novian's body thinks back to one particular incident at St. Braun.  It had been a few weeks after he started his janitorial duties, long enough to get the hang of things but not long enough to be grandfathered in.  A canid man had come in with his wife in his arms.  Her face was battered, her lips torn, both eyes blackened, and her tail appeared to be bent unnaturally.  He told Erica that his wife had fallen down the stairs, and the dwarf had eaten that up like it was vanilla pudding, but Xavier read through it.  The man was right about her falling down the steps, but he had pushed her.  Had pushed her because she was going to visit her sister without his permission, and he had to teach her a lesson.  She would know better next time, the man surely thought.  It had infuriated Xavier to watch her get treated only to be sent back home with him.  She'd been too afraid to deny what her husband said and blamed her own clumsiness.  At the time Xavier didn't have the power to do anything about it, but if he could go back now, he would teach the husband what it was like to be abused.
The door to his room is locked, and he double checks to be certain.  He isn't going to be very much aware of his surroundings tonight, because he and Thor are going to play a little game.  It will be more merciful than leaving him to swim the ocean, but not by much.

Two
Thor Allens is in the floor below Xavier and closer to the stern.  The orc is lying on his own cot, daydreaming.  Xavier decides this will be a wonderful thing to interrupt, so he enters the orc's mind.
In this particular fantasy, there is a smooth skinned elven boy playing and giggling with the orc in a field some billion or so miles from watching eyes.  Xavier thinks for a brief moment that the elf's size is humorous.  Even though he's a child, he might be able to overpower the man, who is also giggling and grinning.  Everything about the field is sterile, no loose dirt under the grass, no bugs, no temperature.  It is a perfectly comfortable environment.
Thor grabs hold of the child and pulls him in for a lengthy hug.  This is when Xavier chooses to step in and replace the boy.  He doesn't keep up the mantra of "Xavier" though, and goes straight to his full form.  He does this partly because Thor will never be able to connect the dots, but mostly it's because he wants to tower over the orc, to ensure the orc knows he is powerless.  
This is Cecil's dream now.
Thor at first is jubilant that the elf is suddenly bigger, but when he looks up, he unlatches his arms and stumbles backwards.  Thor tries to open his eyes, after all, it isn't a real dream.  He's still awake.  But he cannot.  In fact, his whole body is paralyzed.  The orc begins moaning in fear, not of the man, not yet, but of his sudden loss of control.
Cecil stares blankly at the mess of a man, as he begins tearing this false world apart.  There is a strange delight for Cecil, knowing that Thor is so unquestionably powerless to resist what is being done, just understanding that the orc will have no hope of weaseling out of what is to come.
Three
Cecil veils the world in absolute black, and Thor backpedals on the liquid dark, gasping now in dread.  Then Cecil brings things into light, monsters he had seen at the mine and at the Dark Forest.  Thor is dropped into a forest of Cecil's creation, and the creatures begin to chase him.  Thor struggles to wake his body, but he can't.  So he runs, crying and hysterical into the darkness, while abominations follow suit.  Cecil plants into the orc's mind senses of despair and fright, and so powerful are these that the orc loses control of his real body's bladder.  When the orc finally becomes cornered, and his terror has risen so high that he borders fainting, Cecil extracts him from the woods.
Cecil is sitting across from Thor in a neat little white room with no doors.  Between them is a sky blue table with loose nails lying about.  The orc tries to speak, but Cecil has stripped him of the privilege.  Then Thor's hands raise up against his will and reach across the table to the nails.  He clasps them tightly and looks puzzled.
The puzzlement changes to anguish, as the nails begin to heat up and burn through his skin.  Taking on a life of their own, the pieces of superheated iron pierce the orc's skin and begin wriggling up through the veins in his arms, scorching and shredding tissue as they go.  Thor's eyes bulge, his teeth clamp down, and his body strains against the need to scream.  Cecil denies him such a luxury.
In the next place, Thor is forced to wade through a river of spiders.  In the one after that, he is slowly skinned by a pack of jackals.  Still, in the next he is plunged into an endless fall.  And it goes on like that all night, until the man which was once called Thor Allens is nothing more than a broken lump of flesh and bone.  
It isn't until after Thor's consciousness recedes into hiding that Cecil ceases the terror and pulls the man's being back into the forefront.  He sits down at the same table where the nails had been, and Thor is made to sit across from him.  The shattered being of the orc can no longer moan or struggle, but is instead a ragdoll to listen.
"Hello, Thor." The large man says.
Thor cannot reply.  He cannot moan, nor nod, not even sigh.  But he does blink to show he can still understand, that his core essence has not completely vanished.
"I won't tell you why I'm here.  I made it very clear without words why I'm here."
Thor thinks of a playground.
"That's right.  Glad I didn't destroy you outright.  I could've.  Good news for you, the torture is over.  You're free to go."
Thor would smile, but whatever joy life had has been sapped by this long evening.
"But . . . there is one stipulation." Cecil leans forward, and the table creaks at his behest. "I will come back and do this all over again, if you ever come a mile," He rethinks that, "a few trots to a child.  You will go and burn the pictures.  Do this, and we'll never have to have another session.  Understand?"
Thor's thoughts are disheveled, but he manages to blink.
"Fantastic." Cecil plants a trigger in the orc's mind, one that would bring him through all the horrors of the evening, if he did disobey the restraining order he was given.  Cecil releases the orc and sees him bolt upright and stare at his soiled pants before sobbing quietly.  It seems that adults didn't handle nightmares any better than children.
Cecil returned to his miniscule body and went to sleep.
Four
He woke the next morning with the Hag rocking back and forth, groaning in its underbelly.  He wondered how much closer they must be to land, and they might have arrived if they'd flown.  He sat up in his cot, thoughts of the orc floating away from the top of his mind, sick afterthoughts.  What he'd done was, though perhaps justified, a bit much.  He didn't want to think on it anymore.
He went out to the deck.  This time it was to search for land.  The sea was making him anxious.  It wasn't the small room, and he knew how to swim.  But the fact there was such vast nothingness all about him made him restless.  He wanted to be able to run with his own two legs.  He stomached his anxiety, knowing he ought to enjoy the ocean while he could.
Five
A week later he was standing on the same part of the deck, when land came into view.  The intercom clicked, and the captain told all the passengers to return to their cabins.  Cecil lingered a few moments longer, and he put a gentle aura of unimportance around him to keep the crew from prodding him along.  They'd want to have everyone sardined and orderly for docking, but he wanted a few more minutes of fresh air, a few more minutes of peace.  He knew port would be the beginning of a very long day, or weeks, or life.
He was swept along with the streaming mass like a river of living bodies pouring from the Whorl Hag.  Thor and the Oswitzens were not among the people in his immediate proximity, but he knew they were behind in the steel gut, waiting for their turn to join the flow.
When the crowd subsided, and everyone was set on their own way, it was like a fog had lifted from the pier.  Xavier could see Sensen now, and it wasn't disappointing.  Everything was proper, organized, and modern.  It gave to him a sense of the present.  In a place like this, he thought, citizens might be able to take plane rides.  Might be able to drive their own cars and bicker on social media about the price of oil.  It seemed untouched by the policies of the North, but he wasn't certain if that was good or bad.  It was different.
He took a map from a welcoming station on the other side of the ticket stands.  It folded out into a detailed depiction of town with a small rectangle in the bottom right corner showing a far less detailed layout of the northern region.  Achron was on the rectangle as a white dot surrounded by leaf green southeast.  There were a few other cities, but he imagined there were plenty of small towns scattered about, too.  Xavier folded the map and stuck it in his back pocket.
Though he felt a great deal of cooped up restlessness, Xavier was a tad reluctant to leave Sensen.  At the border, where streets broke apart into outroads, he stopped to look back at the electric lights, the modern homes, and all the aesthetics.  If it weren't for the lack of cars parked beside the sidewalks and an abundance of fairytale denizens, he would think he might have sailed right back to Earth.
Six
The green on the map turned out to be a pine forest that covered most of the northern portion of Barimede like a wooded hairline.  Xavier was perhaps two or three miles into the thick of the woods, checking the fine tan lines on his map for when he'd have to change roads.  The traveling itself was smooth, and the roads were fine, even though it didn't seem they'd been traveled all that often.
Shortly after turning off onto another path that should take him straight to Achron, dusk overtakes the forest.  It is pure luminescence compared to the Dark Forest, but that isn't saying much.  The heavily needled branches swallow up most of the natural light, and he decides to stop and rest for the night.  He supposes he could keep up the trot all night, as it wouldn't be much for him to conjure his own light.  But stopping to sleep when night came was natural, on Earth and Destursha.  An excuse to imagine he is traveling the roads of Earth is more than enough to make up his mind.
He walks into the shroud no farther than he could throw a stone, into a place that might never have felt a person's footsteps.  He takes the pack from his back, suddenly aware of the strain it was causing.  He clears a patch of ground of fallen needles and makes a little stone circle.  It is now on the edge, where dusk becomes outright night.  He lights a fire and raises it to a warm and bright yellow.  Then he sits and rests his back against a tree, ready to enjoy the steady flame before dozing off.
He thinks again of how solitary this place is, of how befitting him it is.  He has cast himself out of The Order, but he never belonged to this world.  He ponders how alien he is, how truly foreign he is to all parties.  Perhaps, he thinks, this is precisely where he belongs.  Out in the Lands of Nowhere, a place he can be separate from that in which he doesn't belong.  A place where he can learn his magic, maybe grow old and eccentric.  Get a beard and wear a pointy purple hat, and perhaps make a staff for no real reason that he could use as a conduit.
And the sense of loneliness seeps in.  He marvels at how intrusive loneliness is.  Every other emotion needs to be provoked in order to well up to the surface.  But loneliness needs only absence, and it agitates itself.  It is one of the times he wishes Salina were here.  To sit by the campfire with him once more and send him her own warmth of presence.  He is aware that at the end of all this hard work, he may come up with nothing.  He can't force her to grow fond of him, and he wouldn't want to.  He wants things to be as natural as they can be at this point.  Anything else would be hollow.
Seven
He hears footsteps beyond the light of his fire and tries to use his special sight to know what it is.  He can't detect a thing, as if it had just been imagination and boredom mixing in the worst way.  He presses up against the tree and waits for a possum or a raccoon to come bobbing out in search of scraps.
Instead he sees a wolf woman walking out of the darkness.  Her fur gleams like polished steel, and he knows exactly who it is.  He leans forward, a smile seeding, "Salina?" All the feelings of rejection flee.  As if she had come just to console his troubled mind, there she was.
Her lips parted, and her teeth showed.  She snarled at him and began growling.  Xavier turned and looked behind him, thinking she must be seeing something sneak up on him.  But when he looked back at her, Salina was lunging for him.  Out of reflex, a wall of ice saved him from her attack.  But she quickly shattered it and struck him.  He went reeling to the ground, confused and hurt on levels not entirely physical.  Standing back up, he thought his very soul might have been bruised.
He can't fight her, he knows.  Not that he couldn't restrain her with some well-done spells, but he knows he simply can't.  He can no more resist her than he could put in a contact lens with his little toes.  So he begins running and setting up barricades to slow her down.  His mind is in such a whirl that his barriers suffer, and she keeps pace easily.
He manages to run through the trees, displacing the molecules like one does with the air.  He sinks himself fully into the ground and changes directions.  Even she would have a hard time keeping up through solid earth.  Xavier goes on like this for some time before deciding to resurface.
When he comes back up, he glances about and sees no one.  He's relieved, but only until she falls from a tree onto him.  She pins him down on his back.  Her breath is warm, as she continues to growl.  Her knees are squeezing his legs together with enough force that he thinks bones are about to snap.  Her face inches closer to his, and her jaws open.  He thinks that if he had to go, this is probably the most appropriate way and that she ought to be the one doing it.  Then she tears out his throat.

Chapter Twenty-Six

One
He wakes with a start, his legs spazzing frantically and his arms quivering.  He reaches for his neck, even though he knows it's fine.  He sighs and looks at the steady fire.  It had no concern for his nightmare.
Xavier looks across from it, and his head flies back into the tree.  Sitting across from him as a werewolf is Salina.
"You had a bad dream?" She asks, and her voice is concern.
At first he cannot answer.  This is real. "Yeah," He says, "you'd think I could grow out of those." He wants to say more, but he's stunned.
She walks through the fire, not around it, and sits next to him.  The sword she inherited from him is leaning against the tree she'd come from.  Salina stretches her legs out next to his, and hers are longer.  She takes one of his hands and opens the palm, while he is again powerless to resist.  With one quick stroke of a claw, she draws his blood to give him lycanthropy.  Then she closes it and pulls his hand over to her abdomen, and he can feel the warmth of her core.
Xavier begins turning back into a human. "This is the secret.  I'm-"
"I know, Cecil." She leans over and pecks his neck with a kiss, the very same neck she had gorged upon in a dream, the one she still could.  She squeezes his hand. "You didn't have to hide yourself, you know.  I've been wanting to return the kindness."
The next thing he knows, Salina is on top of him.  She's still coated in fur, but this does not bother him.  She is leading him, while he tries not to faint from all the stimulation.  He pulls her in, and she stops her domination for a moment to allow it.  All while running his fingers through the silk at the nape of her neck.
She begins removing his pants hungrily, and his face begins to burn.
Two
Xavier awakes on his side, his pants feeling much too tight.  He sits up, looks around, and Salina is not to be seen.  
The loneliness climaxes.
He stands and tries to wail, but all he can do is sob.  He isn't sure why he is so broken up over a dream, but just so Xavier wishes he could go back to sleep and never wake again.  He stands there, possibly the only example of mankind in this great emptiness, and cries freely.  He does so because he can and because it helps.  Xavier Pontius, soldier for hire, once martyr by the name of Cecil, once immortal, slayer of giants and highwaymen, wept.
When he's done, and his eyes feel sore and heavy, he walks over to the pine Salina had been sitting against in the pleasant dream.  He decides it's time to make a memento, in case this journey proves to be too much, or if something happens to him.  He pulls a chunk of heartwood from the tree and walks back to his spot.  He looks it over and thinks it won't do.  So he takes a stone from beside him and holds it up to the light of the fire instead.
The stone is light gray, as if the sun had been bleaching it for centuries.  He begins molding it like one would clay and makes it into a disk.  It's too large, so he trims the edges until it fits nicely in his palm.  He takes a few moments to think of something to inscribe on it.  A memory only Salina and he would share.
He presses the stone into his palm and thinks of the night he had come to see her after his first transformation.  When he looks at the face side, there is a perfect carving of two werewolves in a field.  The first werewolf is sitting against a tree with a book and the second is standing in front with a puzzled look.
He flips the stone around and presses the opposite side into his hand.  He remembers, and when he looks at the back side he sees it carved in as precisely as if done by machine.  It's the last thing he told his friends before he died.
He slides the stone into his back pocket, where the map is.  He isn't quite sure what good it will do, but it comforts him to know it might be out of his hands.  These missions are dangerous, and he supposes it wouldn't be farfetched to have Salina come and assassinate him.  He was suspicious, after all.  But if that did happen, or if he found the end of the road on one of these excursions, that stone would reveal the truth.  He's almost positive that his magic would wear off if he died, and Salina would see who she killed.  But suppose it didn't, he wanted her to know.
The fire crackled as a real one would, and Xavier fell asleep.
Three
He doesn't wake up to threats or teasing arousal but to weak beams of sunlight and rustling underbrush.  A rabbit hops up to the edge of his campfire, sees the novian stirring, and bolts into the forest, zigzagging on its springy legs.  I don't care how steady that tortoise was.  There's no way he could beat one of those.  He stretches and yawns, noticing his throat is dry.  Must've slept with his mouth open, drooling most likely.
He stands up and pops his false back into place.  The fire chokes out at his thoughts, but he leaves the ring of stones.  He walks past Salina's tree, which he thinks he has executed, and finds another to relieve himself on.  Somewhere in the back of his mind he thinks of watering a lemon tree and zips back up.
He grabs his things and heads back to the road.  Xavier is mildly aware that his stomach is beginning to plead for food, but thoughts of the day ahead overshadow this.  Achron is maybe another four or five hours away, he guesses based on how long it took him to change paths after leaving port.  He isn't sure where exactly he is to go when he gets there, but they always seem to have someone waiting for him.  Why would this time be any different?
Four
It was cool in the forest, and he supposed it must be winter here as well.  He wasn't south of the equator then, which made him wonder what the lands down there might be like.  As for here in what might be Yosemite, things were temperate.  If he weren't moving about and couldn't supplement heat with magic, he might be more comfortable in a light jacket.  That might be too much, perhaps just an added vest.
While the road was clearly visible, it did have a sheet of browned pine needles and the husks of old pine cones littered about in abundance.  His footfalls made a crunch, and it was that sensory input that made him sure of his travels.  What with the trees being so similar, and the road so endlessly uniform, it was all he could do to wonder if he'd made any progress.
As he went, the air became further chilled.  Moving ever farther from the coast and in heavy shade attributed to this, but it was still pleasant.  A crisp chill to complement the snapping underfoot.  It made Xavier think he was walking on a path of ice chips, in some winter where the air was still enough not to burn.  The stone in his back pocket felt like a cold plate, and a noticeable pocket of warm air was trapped between his back and his pack.  The whole atmosphere was energizing, like a splash of water.  As if the air was oozing adrenaline, he thought he could sprint the rest of the way without being winded.  These sensations circulated around him, and he wanted them to stay.  It was a poor facsimile, but it was the closest he'd felt to being a werewolf since returning.
After the exhilaration of brisk air, he had a few crashing moments, times along the path when he wanted to stop and lay down.  He was careful to avoid resting and wondered if he wasn't succumbing to some kind of depression.  He didn't think it was all that natural to desire to curl up and be left alone until starvation came to motivate, not for a happy, strapping young lad such as himself.  The world was his oyster, and all that filthy nonsense people tell teens.  It was as if he had been Icarus, and his wings of wax had melted away.  Now he was plummeting back to where he belonged.
He kept moving.  At some point the road became clear and paved with obsidian.  It took him some time to realize his feet were coming down with thuds and not soft crunches, and he was impressed by the pristine layout under his feet.  The dark stone was polished and evenly laid, with gutters along the edges.  He was approaching Achron.
Five
At the city's edge, the blackened road opened up and was gilded by chrome lampposts.  They were unlit, but their reflectiveness was brilliant in the soft sunlight.  A ways into this display of civilization was a golden sign standing on legs of white marble.  It read WELCOME TO ACHRON.  And under that was an addendum MAGIC IS FREE.  As if to make a welcoming effort, the forest began to recede from the road and open into a great field.
Xavier stood at the end of the woods for a long while, such was a privilege to those who made good time.  Before him was a juncture of roads, all paved with that same ebony stone and adorned with those ostentatious lights.  In the center was a city walled by concrete.  It spoke to Xavier of a bastion.  It seemed to say, "To all wanderers, come.  Come to the seat of mankind, for this is where man goes to rest his head." Buildings of ornate design surpassed the walls and looked more like beacons than anything else, as if each might be a lighthouse to guide nighttime sailors safely to shore.  Of the seven roads and two railways leading into the great fortress city, only Xavier's was barren.  The rest teemed with life, and merchants were set up in open markets about the entrances.
As he approached his destination, Xavier noticed hairline cracks running along the walls.  That wasn't right though.  They were more like veins, and he could faintly see that they were coursing with light.  He wasn't sure if it was only for display, or if there were some kind of warding on the city.  He wondered what a ward like that would repel, and he tried to understand its purpose by sending out a tendril of his own power.  It came back unknowing, and that scared him more than any sign of strength could.

Chapter Twenty-Seven

One
There was a hum to the city, a heartbeat.  The ground crackled under his feet with its own secret pulses.  Everything felt living and drawn forward, as if nothing was just a detail.  He wondered if beyond those gates was not a city, but another world, a portal of sorts.  The idea did not sound unbelievable to him, not here.  A living city like this could take you anywhere.
He passed under the arch of the wall and into Achron.  On the inside was a light that complemented the day.  The dark pavements glowed with lines of golden illumination that seeped into the air like pixie dust.  The chrome street lamps were off, but their polish added to the resplendence.  The sidewalks were pure alabaster, and everything was free of litter.
The citizens walked about with purpose and hard smiles upon their faces.  It was as if everyone there was both a charmer and of grit.  There were cloaks and draping robes, hats with points and broad brims.  An air was about them all, and it took no effort for Xavier to see most were adept mages, an illegal display up north.  The energy from these people exhilarated him.
Two
As expected an alley came up on his left with a peculiar draft.  When he looked down the corridor between the arcane buildings, Xavier saw a tall figure, a man he thought, waiting expectantly.  Xavier stepped into the alley.  Unlike the cities of Atticus, he was being sure to keep his guard up.  Down here he wasn't so special.
The figure was indeed a man, and he was an elf.  Scrawny and pale, even for his kind, but he didn't seem the least bit weak.  Just shady.
"Xavier?" The man asked, and his voice seemed too deep to be real.  Xavier decided it might not be.
Xavier nodded.
The man looked around, felt around it seemed.  Then he pulled a small white slip from a pocket and handed it to the shorter man. "They want you to check in at the Whitestone.  There's a room reserved for you already.  In your name.  We are among friends here."
The paper was folded, but before unfolding it Xavier asked, "Is there anything else I should know?"
"Not that I'm aware of." The elf stiffened and saluted the novian. "Good luck, sir."
Xavier was taken aback by the gesture.  Had he been granted a title, or was this elf just assuming?
He opened the paper once the elf had left the alley.  There was no money this time, but he supposed they wouldn't use his subordinates to pay him, if he really had received a promotion.  He wondered exactly what the hierarchy of this army was.  It was hard to tell without uniforms and insignias.  On the paper was a simply scrawled note, one sentence long.
Go to room 151 after you check in.
Now the only question was where the Whitestone was at.  He wasn't about to run after the elf.  Friends or not, the army had its own business, and he wanted to keep it that way.  Well, it wasn't as if he was a stranger to wandering.  It felt like he'd been doing just that for a long time, maybe here and there being funneled in a certain direction.  And besides, this city intrigued him.
Three
He took up the local flare while exploring the streets, having his feet dangle over the obsidian and alabaster while the rest of him rode the air.  It was strange to be displaying his power so openly, and a bit embarrassing.  Almost as if he had been told it was fine to go out in his underpants, and that would be okay here.  He knew it wasn't like that in the least, but in some ways it was.  He had to fight his own inhibitions to allow the false flight, but it felt good to do it, to so freely use the skills he possessed.  If nothing else, he knew the practice would do him good.
He landed on his feet in front of Whitestone.  The hotel might have stood out more, if every other building weren't an icon of its own.  What set the Whitestone apart so slightly was just that; it was made entirely of white marble.  What little bit was not white was a gleaming silver, almost mirror like.  At night he was certain it stood out against the brilliant lights.
He pushed through the double doors and into a warm gray room.  It was a bit of a stretch, but the color reminded him of Salina's fur.  Such a dead color filled with vibrant life.  It smelled of cinnamon and deep autumn.  The clerk's desk was a Siberian tiger white with black year bands running along the wood.  For all its migraine pumping brightness, the theme of white seemed muted and comfortable.
The clerk recognized him as Mr. Pontius, welcomed him, and told him his room was ready.  She handed him the keycard (#163) and told him his room was on the fifth floor.  Then he was asked if he would be wanting help with his luggage, but he assured her his back could handle it a few more minutes.
He set his pack of clothes on the bed.  The rock was still in his back pocket, but he didn't think he'd be taking that out.  That was going to stay with him.  He looked at the note, and he knew the room was just down the hall.  He was hungry again, and his stomach wouldn't be accepting excuses any longer.  He hoped in the back of his mind, almost humorously, that whatever business was in room 151 was catered.
Four
He knocked on the door, while his gut rolled around in a little tantrum.  It was as if it wanted to prove it was louder and more important than whatever and whoever was behind that door.  Xavier supposed an ambassador from Earth could be behind the door with news telling him he could go home, and his stomach would still claim supremacy.
The door opened without question, and it gave Xavier a reinforcing nudge of understanding.  That they really were among friends.  The elf behind the door looked down and smiled, revealing that many years had been at work weathering his face.  Partheus waved Xavier in.
"You're early." The old mayor said. "But that's why we had someone wait for you.  I hear you're usually early, like you don't have any distractions."
Xavier shrugged and looked around at the room that was identical to his.  Light gray furnishing and egg white tapestry, a TV monitor with no cords attached, and shaggy white carpet.  There was a window at the far end of the room that looked out onto a street filled with exotic shops, or exotic to outlanders.  The ceiling was speckled.  There was an opened, empty briefcase on the bed with a few suits laid out besides.
"So when did I become a 'sir?'" Xavier asked.  Partheus was already wrapped up in a pile of notes, shuffling sheets about, proving Xavier did come earlier than expected perhaps. "I was never given a uniform, and I didn't graduate officer's school."
"Really?" The elf asked the universe. "Why wouldn't it be right on top?  Where else would I put it?" His face brightened as he retrieved what he wanted from the disheveled pile. "There!"
"Find what you wanted?"
"Yes.  And about that, that was supposed to be a surprise.  I told the messenger not to treat you like a superior.  He wasn't supposed to know, but it slipped.  Is that where you heard it?"
Xavier nodded.
"Well, after our little palaver Solday, just a formality really, we planned on officially awarding you your new rank.  Army Rep isn't a rank.  You certainly can't be a grunt anymore.  And since this whole thing is new, these positions need filled anyway."
"What rank am I?"
The elf became jubilant. "Aha!  There's still something left to be a surprise.  That's great.  Just act fully surprised, will ya?  The Order holds these positions in high esteem."
Now he was curious. "I'll try my best."
"I know it's hard, what with the lack of fluffery and all.  It would be really nice if you could go in uniform, but we both know there are none.  Do you have a suit?"
"Yes, I brought a suit.  I take it we're talking?"
"Yeah, and we have to look the part.  I don't want to say it, but this'll probably be a real pissing match.  We have to prove we're organized enough and strong enough that their aid will be wise.  Basically we have to make sure they think we'll win, and they can be in on the spoils for some help."
"We've done well so far.  Why wouldn't they know that?"
"Oh, they do Xavier.  It's just that we're not to the hard part yet.  There are a few governors that are going to be harder to topple.  And then there's Malachi."
"Is it really going to be that much trouble?"
Partheus walked to the window and tapped on the glass with a finger. "This place is pretty grand.  Big walls and strong people, a perfect combo.  Malachi's Daskin makes Achron look like a rotten shack with a dead body protecting it.  While Trothos and Dahzir were having their squabbles, Malachi was smuggling in Leviathan tech and other goods.  He was going to try and overthrow the king, but then the werewolves showed up and did it for him.  He's not happy about the lack of support from the king's old guard, but that won't matter.  He's got his own army, and now he's just waiting.  And when he does strike, it's going to hurt."
"We have our hands full." Xavier wondered if his ignorance was showing.
"Exactly the opposite of what we want to portray when we speak to these people." The elf sighed.  It was an old sigh filled with the sound of whining lungs.
"What happens when it's all over?  You know, after we win and the Leviathans concede?"
"This is why you're getting that promotion.  You just look at this as a matter of time, as inevitable as a rolling rock getting to the bottom of a hill." He yawned and sat down on the bed beside a black leather belt. "Then The Order gets the space program back up and open to the public.  And they go home someday, probably in a couple hundred years.  After I'm gone.  People will keep fighting, but there might be a few good years afterwards before the dust gets kicked up again.  You're a wizard, so you just might get to see them leave.  Heck, you might get to be king someday.  But all that is after we get rid of Malachi, after we tour the rest of Barimede in search of aid, after we talk to our friends here in Achron."
The idea of being king wasn't appealing.  To lead a people he neither knew nor understood.  Being left behind sounded worse, though.  To be stranded on this strange rock, while the only people he considered family went off into the great unknown without him.  That was the worst case scenario for him, he thought.  Being killed on this journey by friend or foe paled in comparison.  What a mad king that would drive him to be.
The elf snapped his fingers. "That's the other thing I wanted to tell you.  We're going to be doing some traveling after our delegations.  It'll be smoother than this.  We'll get to a city, speak, and go to another the next day or the day after.  There are thirteen in all, not counting Achron.  Three weeks work, I'd say."
"That doesn't sound so bad."
"You haven't been in politics very long, then." Partheus pushed out a puff of air.  It sounded like a single guffaw. "You know what, you remind me a lot of Cecil Fauden.  I had a conversation like this with him in a hotel room back in Mirwa.  He gave me quite a start.  Jumped at me from a corner in a dark room, picked me off the ground, and listened to my pleas for help.  He took it in stride, just like you do."
"Maybe he and I would've made good friends." Xavier replied.  He was not the least bit coy.  He almost believed it himself.
"I don't doubt it." The elf said and popped a knee.  Age was catching up to him at last. "Hey, why don't I show you around town?  I've been here before a few times.  I know where they keep the good food."
"I'm starving."

Chapter Twenty-Eight

One
They stopped for food at Novus, which was themed around astrology.  The tables were orange and teal, and depictions of Orion and Artemis in battle formed murals on the walls.  Lights hung from the ceiling in the shape of crescents and stars.  A gold red sun stood on a pedestal at the center of the eating area.  Other things, which appeared to be astrolabes, floated about on nothingness atop the backs of the booths.  The ceiling looked to be a starry night sky with a comet passing by randomly every now and again.
They ordered their food and began chit-chatting.  He cut through the questions of how he was enjoying working with The Order or what he thought of the city. "I haven't had this position the entire time, have I?" This was going to come out rude, he knew, but plain and simple would fit best. "I've been getting a good sum.  I'm sure the other recruits weren't."
Partheus was sipping at a glass of ice water.  There was a lemon wedge floating amongst the cubes. "Don't worry about that.  You've been getting exactly what you earned." The elf leaned back and stretched.  The lighting took years away from his face. "I'm not sure if you're familiar with how the Leviathans operate, but we basically copied that.  Regular serviceman get a pretty nominal rate, but soldiers that take magic training get a significant boost.  Our patrons weren't thrilled to be shilling out so much at first, but when we assured them it was going toward mages, they hushed up.  Mages are useful for healing and killing.  And apparently espionage."
Xavier pushed up against the seat and peered over.
"It's all right." Partheus said. "That's all been declassified.  What's already done, anyway." He took a large gulp and paused as if fighting off a tremor of brain freeze. "You've been getting mage rates.  No mistakes."
"What am I going to say to these people?  I'm just a hired killer, really.  I can't speak much about our campaign strategy."
"Again, don't worry.  The werewolves do the 'grand scheme' explaining.  You'll just have to talk about your battle accolades, maybe toss a fireball at a training dummy."
"What if I light the whole city on fire?"
The elf rolled his eyes. "Then we'll have more enemies."
Two
Partheus showed Xavier around.  There were theater houses with rippling bands of unbound light streaking across their signs like ethereal snakes, grabbing up the wonder and awe of passersby.  There were arcades and sports centers, an arena for magic dueling, another arena to play field tackle, and a celestial amphitheater.
In the back of Xavier's mind he wondered if Salina would like the sights here.  The amphitheater might make her think of home, and he'd like to give her that comfort.  Farther up in his mind, where the cynicism rested, he didn't think he'd ever bring her here.  Would never have the authority, would never have her consent.  But he would very much like to sit and watch the stars with her.  It would all be rosy then, he thought, but deeper down he knew nothing would ever be right.
Eventually they ended up back at the hotel.  There were a few other things they'd seen on the way, but Xavier had found himself lost in his thoughts again and following Partheus without any conscious effort.
They each went to their own rooms.  Xavier sat down on his bed beside his things and looked out the window at the cityscape.  The red sun had crept under the horizon somewhere beyond the wall, and golden light poured into his room.  The walls and ceiling turned a somber wheat hue like sepia.  Xavier became homesick, surrounded by this soft shade.  He gazed out the window where all the light rushed in, longing marring his face.
Three
Xavier went out the next day to buy a few books.  He took another good look at the city but left Partheus alone.  All day long there was a nervous tension building up inside him.  As if there was a net around his lungs that was slowly shrinking.
When the day and time for the meeting came, Xavier was sitting in his room, almost done with the next Of Moons and Dreams book.  He was thoroughly enjoying it for the story it told and for the escape it gave.  It was a small comfort to be temporarily lost in the affairs of Winsome Jr.
Partheus knocked on the door, because who else could it be?  Xavier asked for a minute and changed into the suit.  Putting it on he thought of mustard stains and all the millions or so things that could ruin a nice piece of attire like this.
When he was clothed in his monkey suit, he shut off the lights and greeted the elf at the door.  As Xavier's fingers left the handle, he could feel his mouth tinge with cotton.  He wasn't looking forward to this.
Four
"Just let me take the lead, okay?" Partheus said.
In his thoughts and worries, Xavier hardly heard the old man.  He had to take a moment and think about it, as his mind played back the dialogue, trying to decrypt the message he should've heard.  It proved successful, though he knew enough time had passed that a reply would be omitted.  He went for it anyway. "Yeah." He said through a dry mouth.  
He flexed his hands, which were curling like dead spiders.
They came to a pillared gray building.  To Xavier it looked like a courthouse or one of the Roman constructs.  It had a rolling concrete roof with a flagpole at the center of a great circle.  The windows were narrow and barred with iron.  A set of steps led up to the entryway, where a colossal overhang of stone stood.  People were bustling in and out, half of them on phones.  Above the row of a dozen or so double doors was a great golden star made of nothing but ethereal gases.  It glowed with such intensity that the underside of the overhang made the world beyond appear dusky.  There was a resonance to it like the veins on the walls.  Xavier instinctively put a hand up to shade the light, but it refused to burn his retina, so he dropped it and flexed it a few more times.
Partheus led him into the building, where a blast of warm air greeted them from vents above.  Until then, Xavier had only been mildly aware that it was winter outside, and he had to make a conscious effort to stop his sweat glands on his false vessel.  On the inside were desks and hallways, not unlike those at the Sentinel.  The floor was white marble and seemed to have a surety about it that Xavier couldn't quite place.  As if the marble here were quite literally a cut above from any slabs he might've set foot upon anywhere else.  Partheus began down a hall as naturally as the staff.
The hallway widened, and on the left side were six elevators.  The doors of which were painted with a thin coat of deep brown, the sort that might be seen on lunch trays.  Partheus walked up the row of elevators and clicked the up switch on the panel by each.  Partheus walked into the first available and almost overlooked the novian who had followed behind, double checking to make sure he was not still outside.  He pressed a circular chrome button that lit up with a brilliant blue "13" and waited for anyone else to board.  The buttons, Xavier noticed, went up to 19.
They stopped at every floor on the way up, people boarding and departing on each.  The extra time made Xavier's nerves sink below the surface into a place that was cold and unstable.  He still had a quake to him and some cotton mouth, but he seemed loose again, unbound by the tension.  The lifting of the elevator dropped his stomach at every upstart, and it prodded at the recessing nerves, provoking them to come forward in force.
They got off on the thirteenth floor, sidled through a rush of boarders, and went down the corridor.  There was something atmospherically different about this floor, as if a low pressure zone were contained within.  Everything seemed sharper.  As they went, the odd tingling in the air grew as if they might be within a tesla coil, energy tugging at the electrons.  Ozone is what it was, what it had to be.  The air could not exist normally, not here where energy coursed so freely, so wildly.
Five
Partheus pushed open a door, and Xavier followed into a great room that used the dome as its ceiling.  Where the other six floors went up to was now a thing of curiosity.  The edges of the round room were cased in steps leading down to the center.  There in the nexus was a thick glass table, a pitcher of water in the middle and a stack of foam cups beside.  A man who might have ties to border collies and poodles stood by the table with both thumbs hooked into his belt.  He stood watch over the table, a stun gun his only companion.
"We're early." Partheus said.
"How early?" Xavier had either forgotten to ask when the meeting was held or had forgotten the time itself.  With his nerves strung up like this, he tried not to worry much about which of these it was.
"Only a few minutes." The elf paused and coughed into his elbow, sounding his age. "They'll be here right on time.  Not a moment earlier.  Not a moment later."
That want to escape welled up in Xavier again like a volatile chamber of magma bursting through cracks and channels to the surface, desiring only to be free and maybe char a few villagers in the process.  He looked at the far wall behind the bailiff.  It would be so easy to just blast through that wall, float on down to the street, and hoof it.  There was still time, he thought frantically, to run off into the great wilderness.  He wasn't certain how, but Xavier had a nagging feeling that these people would see through his ruse, and that everything he had worked for would come crashing down.  This was, after all, Major League business.
The delegates did not so much walk through the door at the far end as appear from it.  Their presence broke Xavier's thoughts.  There were four of them, three men and a woman.  The woman was an elf, and all three of the men were aemon.  Their business suits clung to their forms oddly, like the painting of dogs playing poker.  They moved down the steps, and the bailiff backed away and leaned against a pillar.  Partheus bowed and moved toward the table.  Xavier did the same.
Six
Dark suits in a gray room.  Outside the sky was clouding over the southern continent, and a cold rain was waiting to be born.  The two cadres came together, the elves shook hands, and everyone sat down.  The woman introduced herself as Athia and welcomed the strangers to the city.  Then she made introductions for the three men, brothers by the last name of Desim, one the mayor, one captain of the city guard, and the youngest, city comptroller.  She was the real delegate, the one tasked with speaking with "foreign" bodies.  But as she said, this called for "the whole gang." It was friendly and laid back, and Xavier didn't buy a word of it.
Partheus introduced himself as a low rumble of thunder passed over the building.  None of them could hear it, but outside the water was falling.  Xavier greeted them and was surprised he kept himself so mellow.  Hopefully Partheus would carry the conversation from then on.
"So what can Achron do for you?" Athia asked.
Partheus began by explaining that it would be a mutual favor for both parties.  It was cheese of the rankest political kind.  Xavier had heard it on earth, the dip and twirl of half-complete statements.  Flowery words and legal loopholes.  He strained to keep himself sitting upright through the jargon but knew that soon enough he would need to participate.  Stratagems were brought up, and at some point the mayor asked Partheus how the quality of life would change for the Achrons if they rendered their aid.
Seven
It dolled on like this until the captain broke into the palaver. "My men will fight at my word, but I would have that mean something.  Can I trust your forces to share some of the burden?  I wouldn't send them to their deaths on your statements alone."  He was still sitting, but his voice filled the room with his presence.
Partheus nudged Xavier, who had been sitting listlessly for about an hour.  Nothing came to the novian, save for a robotic instinct to turn and look at the captain.  Words began to spill out of him, and he wasn't certain if they were the right ones. "Our soldiers are the best.  Your men would not be shouldering the load alone." They didn't even feel like his words.  It was as if he were breathing, and this was the sound of his wind.  It was far away and foreign like the lies parents tell their children to make them eat their vegetables.
There was a heartbeat in the room.  It reverberated off the gray like some lonesome ping.  The rippling waves of its sound bounced against Xavier's skin, and he knew immediately whom it belonged to.  Xavier connected the dots he'd been ignoring, either because of his novice or because this whole setting made his consciousness want to retreat.  The captain was a wizard, and his blood was electric.  Xavier had to wonder if the aemon could read the words spilling out of his mouth, the hoopla nonsense that made voters complacent.  A wizard would at least be able to smell it on the current, and the idea put Xavier on edge.  His whole purpose here was to appear as powerful as possible, never mind understanding his own power or being fully comfortable with it.
He went on. "This is all just to minimize casualties.  Get the governors to surrender before this war drags on, let them be frightened by numbers.  But every one of ours is worth ten of theirs, so a real loss is out of the question."
The brothers looked at each other.  At some point Athia and Partheus had splintered off into pure politics and were having their own parley at the far end of the table.  The youngest brother stood and bowed.  He patted his siblings' shoulders and told them he wouldn't be useful for anything more and left.  The mayor waved him off and sent a look at the captain, suggesting he handle the military emissary.
The captain, whose name was Bor according to Athia, stood up and shook hands with Xavier.  Perhaps he would not win an arm wrestling match an ogre, but Bor was twice Xavier's height.  The novian's hand was all but lost in the aemon's, and Xavier had to remind himself that sorcery did not concern itself with the stature of its wielder. "I believe you." He said and added. "But may I challenge you to a friendly competition?  I've pegged you within a certain range, but I'd like to know exactly how strong The Order's finest soldier is."
Feeling confident and a bit brash Xavier replied, "Of course.  As long as you aren't afraid of losing." He wasn't certain if he meant that.
The mayor got up and moved down the table to where Athia sat, giving his brother a blessing as he did.  Bor waved the bailiff over and asked him to escort Xavier to the courtyard.  He assured him that he'd be down soon.  The canid, unable to refuse but visibly reluctant, nodded and looked down at the halfling the way he did to any pompous official.
Eight
The mixed breed guard led Xavier from the room and into one of those sterile hallways.  To Xavier the hall appeared brighter than the one he'd been in before, like a layer of grime had been wiped away and new bulbs installed.
Circles were the theme here in the chilled rain.  Patches of trees over here, rounded up in an oblong stretch of soils.  Red and yellow flowers outstretched their petals in the center of the court and were arranged in a spiral pattern.  There were greens and blues and violets, all gilded by white and careful trims.
He stood outside, alone for a few minutes.  At the far end of the court was an open concrete space glistening darkly with water and appearing to spark with the falling droplets.  Like blinking an eye before being struck, Xavier constructed an artificial umbrella over his head.  It was an act he did not think to do, hadn't even made an effort to do it.  All the same, he stood dry and warm in the cold rain, taking in the sight of the carefully manicured lawn.  It was quiet, and he was beginning to enjoy it.
The silence was broken by a deep rumble of thunder from the south.  It hardly seemed to be coming from the air but from somewhere deep in the earth, like some aftershock.  A wind gusted overhead, making the old fabled hollow sound that was a theme of fall, fright, and cold.  Here in the courtyard, however, there was no wind, and the thunder seemed long off like a titan chained on another continent.
Against the mute light of this dreary day, gray ruled over the colors.  The flowers, vibrant as they were, seemed to be under a veil of drab.  His hand went up to his hairline, that white crown of his own making, and he wondered if it was screaming its brightness here in this court of hollow colors.  Or perhaps it was dulled just the same as everything else, an olde time photo filter, an artful still devoid of the loud drownings which usually flooded the eyes.  Weak pastels.
Then the captain emerged.  He stood beside the novian and looked out at the lush but lifeless courtyard.  He was drinking in the aura of its dismal state the way a sommelier judges a fine wine.  His head tilted toward Xavier, but his eyes never left the gray. "We'll go to the arena, if that's alright.  I'd not harm the plants." He unfolded an umbrella, and Xavier wondered why he hadn't done so closer to the door.
Xavier did not reply, for he could think of nothing but rehashed phrases.  Being clich and running with the grain would be fine if he were simply Xavier Pontius, janitor turned military official, a fine story to write in the history books, not too exciting, not too daring.  That would be the tale of an honest-to-goodness Jonny Appleseed, the plastic mold for children to admire.  But he was not the cardboard cutout for boys to emulate.  He was the alien mage, the phoenix wizard, the once-upon-a-time werewolf.  And when it all came down to quaint little conversations like this, how could he spew out the cookie cutter answers?  His stencils were crooked and twisted, and his goods would never sell by the bakery window.
Nine
As they came up to the arena, Xavier wondered if Partheus would consider where they'd gone off to.  He supposed Bor might have been explaining that on the way out.
The inside of the arena was large and circular, just like the one in Rome.  At each entrance were two paths, one forward to the fighting area, one to the left leading to the seating areas.  There were a series of speaker systems and an oversized screen running ads not unlike a baseball stadium.  Over the speakers a song was playing by radio.  A rock song well suited for working out.  It was a popular song, and he managed to catch one line. "But I don't need . . . friends like this." It broke off into an instrumental section, and he got the feeling that this was more of a gym than a gladiator's death cage.  There were people up in the stands eating from the concessions and people down below training.  It was all tame.
There was equipment in the field along the edges where the stands met the sands, a place that in a hockey arena would have a clear shield up.  Bor took off his jacket and set it on a rack, and Xavier did likewise.  Not for the first time and not for the last, he fully felt the unfitting quality of this business.  It was a sweater knitted of porcupine bristles, and he was wearing it inside-out.
Bor walked to one end, while Xavier stayed near the equipment.  The people training on the far end stopped and retreated to the wall, recognizing the captain.  Someone in the stands squealed and ran for an exit, shouting about Bor's match-up.  This was going to be a show, one way or the other.
Ten
"We'll warm up first!" Bor projected more than shouted.  He lobbed a sluggish fireball from the palm of his hand, the light of which made his whole body glow orange.
Xavier took note of the way the light reflected from Bor's left arm.  It was a fully functioning prosthesis, much smaller than the last he'd seen.  As the fireball descended toward him, he lashed it out of the air the way one might put out a candle by smashing it with a sledgehammer.  And going with the theme he'd been setting for himself along with the abundance of water coming down, Xavier conjured a boulder of ice which he sent rolling at Bor like a bowling ball headed for a lonely pin.
Bor split the boulder with some internal combustion, a flashy display which sent either sides of the sphere tumbling to the left and right.  
Now things heated up.  Bor drew a guitar from the aether and began playing it with savage fury.  The speaker system went silent as the captain's solo produced an unsettling rumble.  The notes began taking form as shockwaves.
Xavier wanted to come to terms with what he saw.  What he saw was a man playing a guitar with such power that it was a weapon.  It was a kind of magic so free and so pure that he thought he could feel a chamber of possibilities open up in his own mind.  What a wondrous display of wizardry this was.  But before he could curl up and meditate on it, the thunderous melodies closed the gap.  He erected a wall of ice and stone layered many times over.  He could swear that he'd once learned that sound lost strength every time it had to change its medium.  Water flung in dizzying arcs around the waves as they came crashing against Xavier's wall with a ferocity unrealistic.  He pressed on more layers and felt the opposing force driving against him, while Bor went on playing harder and faster.
Now the crowds were rushing in.  Partly because of the zealot who ran wide-eyed and dumbstruck from the stands earlier, but mostly because the symphonic destruction Bor was so known for was raging louder than it had in a long time.
Still the aemon played until the charge of his fingers drew in lightning bolts.  The combined thunder of his music and the ionizing air shook the arena and caused the stands to cheer and whistle with glee and fear.
Xavier pushed back with a piston of stone, ice, and static.  It was his first time really trying to harness electricity, but anything that could be added to his counter was welcomed.  The shield came loose and took flight, knocking the waves away like deer before a freight train.  As the tons of lethal matter came upon Bor, the aemon grabbed his guitar like a wild rocker meaning to use it as a club against some obnoxious vocalist.  He swung at the crushing construct like a pro batter.  The contact was deafening, and sent a pulse through the air which knocked the breath out of every viewer.  Eardrums popped, and a few people fainted.  Xavier kept driving the mass toward his opponent and could feel the physical strain behind the notion taking a toll.
At last Bor pushed the crackling silo into the air with his guitar.  The mass rose into the sky of its own accord.  The wizards' energies had given it a life all its own, short as it would be.  It ascended over the city where it suffered a meltdown not unlike that of a dying star.  It expanded in an ever brightening aura of blue and then collapsed into a blinding ball of white.  It screeched and once more likened itself to a train, but this time it was a contorting, derailing thing of certain death.  The clouds parted for a moment as its infernal supernova heat rocketed up into space.  The city glowed with its luminescence, and it was now impossible to not know of the battle, even if one lived up by port.
And the battle went on.

Chapter Twenty-Nine

One
Someone sitting on the outside might think there was a light show being held at the arena.  The clouds lit up with waves of color like a bruised aurora borealis.  Someone unable to see the discharges would feel the steady thrumming in the ground.  It would be no different than living near a mine during blasting hours.  Rhythmic pulses ran through the earth, each tremor more frightening than the last, each one coming closer to bending the frames of Achron's buildings.
For those on the inside of the stadium, this match was nothing short of astonishing.  It was the kind of sight that makes one's organs seem weightless and time irrelevant.  Ozone filled the air as streaks of electricity and waves of green fire rolled back and forth to either side of the sparring area.  The air pressure seemed to drop, and above where the clouds parted before, they now swirled.
Xavier thought he might be running a marathon for all the wear he was beginning to feel.  He thought this little contest had perhaps overstepped a boundary or two, and that the city might end up suffering the consequences of that recklessness.  At the same time, he was happy to keep pace with a veteran wizard.  Besides, if he pulled punches now, his own life might be in peril.  Let them see his strength.


Two
It took every fiber of his being to unleash his final assault.  He kept on sending that unnatural flame Bor's way, but he added a great golem of sand to bar the way between and stand as a kind of turret.  Bor would cut it down, Xavier knew, but it was only ever meant to be a distraction.  If he could only get the aemon to let down his guard for a moment, he was certain he could use his telepathic skills to win this fight.  As the golem made to charge the captain, Bor pierced it with a thunderbolt.  His eyes flicked away from Xavier, and the novian seized the opportunity.
Xavier leapt across the battlefield with his mind, rushing at Bor with a desperate kind of mental agility.  Bor saw too late what was happening, and thanks to that was unable to counter Xavier's physical form.  Meanwhile, the novian was rummaging through his opponent's mind.  The average mind was simple and labeled as such, but it seemed that the mind of a wizard was a maze laden with traps and dead ends.  He was looking for one simple thing, the nerve or fount of magical control.  In his own mind he always imagined it to be more of a spring, but he knew it might be different here.
Xavier Pontius stood hunched over on one end, while Bor stood likewise on the other.  Things had quieted, and the audience was on edge.  The song called "Dastardly Friends" was just now finishing. "But I don't need . . . friends like this.  Robbers and cheaters.  Takers and heartbreakers.  I'll just go it . . . alone." No one listened.

Three
It took him longer than he wanted to find what he was looking for.  It was a waterwheel tirelessly running at an old mill.  The place he was in might have been some cortex of the brain, but Xavier thought this was the mind within the mind.  If it was the soul, it certainly seemed barren.  Here he was not Xavier Pontius but a much larger creature which had once been called Cecil Fauden.
