The large creature ran into the mill and searched for what must be a lever to put a stop to the wheel.  He wanted to hurry before Bor found out where he was and came to stop him, and worse, saw his true self.
Bor was in the distance, where the inner world of the mill met the nexus of in-betweens that litter the consciousness, shouting.  The battle was over.  Even if Bor managed to catch up and see the human muddling, Xavier would've gotten to the switch by then.  His voice came to the human from that dark void, and it was not angry.  Why would it be?  This was not a fight between foes, after all. "I'm really impressed!" It called from everywhere.  The walls of this place were conduits for that owner's voice. "I misjudged you!" It sounded very close.
The lever was against a wall on the inside.  The waterwheel separated from Xavier by only a few feet of cerebral wood moaned and splashed as it always had.  Its steady power sent a numbing vibration through the wall, hardly perceptible but overwhelming in its longevity.  A real mill would've exhausted more than one of these in Bor's lifetime, but Xavier knew this was the one he was born with.  And if anything, it had only become smoother and more suited to spin with the soul's liquid energy as time went on.
He reached for the lever and began pulling.  A part of him feared that stopping the wheel would kill Bor, and he had to remind himself that he was not fighting Dahzir again.  It moved stiffly at first, never having been truly expected to turn from on to off.  As it inched downward, the wheel began grinding against some kind of braking system that sounded like a rusty deli slicer trying to cut through a sheet of aluminum foil.  It was halfway down when Bor truly began to scream.
"I yield!" And now it sounded like it was the mill itself begging for mercy.  In the outside world, which might have been a figment of the imagination as far as these two were concerned, Bor was yelling, "I yield!  I yiiiiiellld!" And unlike the feel-good song which had been playing, everyone heard the plea.
Xavier saw something in the distance, as he pushed the lever back up and released the brakes.  On the other side of the wheel across the pond was a scene playing.  In it the captain, but it was not Bor, was cuffing a rough looking fellow and walking him away.  And before the darkness could swallow up this little memory, the captain was falling and a friend of the rabble coming to remove the cuffs.  These last two vanished and a smaller aemon came trundling to the fallen man's side.  He was clutching his left arm, which bled over him.  Xavier saw no more, and the lever returned to its original place.


Four
Xavier opened his eyes to see Bor kneeling across the scorched field.  It did not look like they'd been sparring as much as it looked like nuclear warheads had been let off.  Tiny ones, maybe.  But the remaining smell of ozone and torn landscape suggested it had been nothing short of devastating.
The music, a faint static background, cut off and was replaced by a live voice. "I'm not even sure what I've seen!  Can you all believe it?" The man behind the voice, who was surely sitting down in a leisurely chair in a cozy room, was panting like he'd been on the field himself. "Let's give our combatants a hand!" The crowd burst with exclamation, and a great chatter began amongst those who came too late to see what had happened.
Xavier walked over and grabbed his and Bor's coats.  He was surprised neither had soaked up the crossfire, but it was just as well he supposed.  It was still raining, he realized, when he picked up the drenched jackets.  At some point he had stopped holding the rain off with that invisible umbrella, and though it was colder than just refreshing, he welcomed the icy droplets.
Bor stood up and shook the lesser man's hand.  He went to say something but instead bent over and coughed.  After some deep breaths he reattempted. "You've got my support."
Five
Partheus waited for them at the exit, his face still reflecting awe. "I hope you didn't wear yourself out too much.  We still have to award you that promotion officially." He said to the novian.
Xavier, who thought he would be quite able to take a nap on a bed of nails replied, "I'll soldier on." His cold jacket was a relief to his burning muscles. "Bor, that was good."
The aemon's breath had come back to him, but his booming voice was muffled. "It was.  I underestimated you.  Achron would be honored to stand beside such great warriors."
Partheus patted Xavier's shoulder.  The three were back on the street, and Bor was headed his own way. "Think you can do this at every city?" The elf asked.  It didn't sound rhetorical enough for the novian, as if this kind of proving may become normal.
"Let me rest up, Partheus.  Right now I couldn't fight off a squirrel." And he was tired.  Whatever forces he had summoned up in that battle, whatever energy he conducted had taken a toll on him as it passed through.  As of yet, magic had been as exhausting as breathing.  There was more to it. "So when is this ceremony?  I'm a little haggard at the moment."
Partheus looked at his phone. "About an hour and a half from now.  We can stop at the hotel, and you could shower if you like." The elf was checking his phone for messages and clumsily dodging other pedestrians in his distraction. "Just a small thing.  We'll go out to eat, have a few laughs, intake a few too many calories.  Maybe you can give a speech?"
Xavier didn't bother to look up at the grinning man. "If we learned anything today, it's that I am not the one that does the talking."
Six
They did stop at the hotel first, and by the time they headed back out, Xavier was catching his second wind.  Even so, he had fallen into a sort of relaxed state he thought would make a Buddhist turn green with envy.  After all the anxiety he went through earlier, his nerves were now submerged in a cool bath of tranquility.  It was rather serene, and despite his renewed strength it made him want to lie down and do some daydreaming.
They walked through the revolving door of a restaurant called Angelus, a posh establishment on the other side of the arena.  Without any real indication of the passage of time, they were entering a reserved room behind the main eatery hall.  It was at this point that Xavier realized he was enjoying himself, had to be.  Time only moves like that when you enjoy it, as if some sadistic part of the brain was blinding your internal chronometer so that your time of revelry could seem as short as possible.  He put a little effort into his consciousness, a little extra focus, if it would help.  It was all he could do to savor the evening.
The room was tiled and covered in ebony.  The lighting was strong but muted in a way that was only possible in a city like this.  There were murals on the walls of what he supposed were past figures come and gone, real or fabricated.  It reminded Xavier of the Sistine Chapel and the other Renaissance art he had the privilege of viewing in textbooks and documentaries.  And the way the light bounced off the murals gave them an ancient tone, like weathered stone.  There was only one table in the room, and it was large.  A long mahogany runway of candles and folded napkins.  It would've been akin to the cumbersome ones royalty seemed to have, where the guests sat too far from the host to actually enjoy dinner conversation.  It was trimmed with silver inlay, and a golden chandelier hung over its center, the room's focal point.
Underneath the chandelier was a leather bound case with two silver latches on the side.  Behind the case was Salina, nodding to welcome her compatriots.  To Xavier she was the best thing in the room, but thankfully that Zen sensation was able to keep him from being too overwhelmed by her appearance.  And that was good, because he didn't know how long his second wind would be able to hold him up if he started to get nervous again.  Salina took Partheus's hand and shook it.  When she took Xavier's, he looked up at her with a gleam.  On some deeper level, he knew he was being too easy to read, but he couldn't help himself.  Maybe if it had been his first wind, but not anymore.
Seven
She asked the two if they needed time to think about their orders, and the elf answered for both.  Salina told the hostess, who led the two newcomer's into the room and who Xavier had hardly taken any notice to, that they would need a few minutes.  That sword was hanging on her back, and Xavier wondered just how many times she'd used it since he gave it up.
"Would you like to do this now or wait until after we eat?" She asked the novian while tapping the leather case impatiently.
"Oh." It was a simple question, to which an answer wouldn't matter much one way or the other.  And that made it more binding.  Which direction should the whimsy go? "We could do that now, I guess.  I'll hardly be able to enjoy the food if I'm thinking about it."
She leaned over the case and unsnapped the buckles.  It popped open a bit, and she pulled the cover all the way off.  Inside was a crystalline sword that glowed the color of gold, and a belt scabbard rested beside.  The sword was etched with ancient Desturshan glyphs which read "Strength without discipline is weakness" to those who could translate.  It resembled a saber, one edge and a hand guard made of white crystal.  Along the length of the blade were white swirls like thorn vines which wrapped all the way from the tip to the handle.  Even in the soft light, it glimmered.  It was the kind of thing one would dream of using, but a real swordsman would dread tarnishing.
She took the sword and held it up sideways and leaned across the table to Xavier.  As he reached out his arms to receive, she said, "Xavier Pontius, in recognition of loyalty and service, we hereby award you the rank of colonel in the Free Army.  Wear the title proudly and let this sword be a symbol of your rank."
He took his reward in hand and inspected it.  The sword was exquisite, a bit long for his size, but he thought he could still use it.  She handed him the sheath, but he didn't even notice it in his free hand.  The way his hand wrapped around the handle was perfect, so natural. "It's too nice." He said, "A pin would've worked." He looked up at her, and she was rolling her eyes.
"If I told you it's just military surplus, would you be more accepting?" She asked.
He looked the blade up and down, saw his false initials engraved into the sheath, and shook his head. "It's too custom to be surplus."  He slid it into the sheath and laid the whole thing on the table. "I'll do what I can to uphold the position.  Thank you."
"Just keep doing what you're doing now.  You're a great asset." If the room had been a tad brighter, he might've seen the look in her eyes, the one that said he was more than just a resource to her.  But in the dim light, and without his lost inhuman senses, all he did was hear the words, the tones of which were perfectly platonic.
Partheus, who had lived long enough to hear such hidden expressions without magic, was standing off to the side of the table with his arms crossed.  He wasn't going to pry, even though this whole ceremony was technically a work function.  And for once, Partheus was the odd man out and not Xavier.
Eight
The hostess returned a few minutes later to see Salina had removed that gargantuan buster in order to sit, and a new sword laid on the table next to the leaning blade.  The three guests she came to serve were sitting on the right side of the table, while the left was being used to hold all their accoutrements.  The elf and the novian were agreeing not to order the same thing, and the human seemed to have made up her mind already.
She took a pen and opened up a small notepad.  She looked to the human, whose mind was set and whose appetite was irrelevant. "Are we ready to order yet?"
Salina glanced at the two. "I think so."
Orders were taken and received.  As usual, Xavier ate as much as a full-sized person, and while the other two wanted to take real note of it, they attributed it to his exertion earlier that day.  Still, Salina was beginning to think it was simply too much for a novian, worn or not, and she had to wonder (couldn't help but to) if it might have something to do with his secret.  And she didn't believe the secret would be as simple as a few tapeworms.  No, there had to be a reason why he needed so many calories.  He was a grown man, not a growing boy, and he was small.  But for today, she would call it a work hunger.
They spent a long while in that back room.  Things started out simple, talking about what city to go to next and wondering just what kind of obstacles waited, obvious things.  But after some time, they began talking like real people.  Partheus even indulged them with a few stories from his youth.
"I was working in Hurlinge at the time." He began and took a swig of scotch, "And there was this fellow who lived on the west side who was fed up with the mail carriers.  He was always complaining about getting junk mail, and he blamed us for it.  Said we ought to just stop bringing it.  I couldn't tell you how many times we tried to explain that what he got wasn't our responsibility.  We kept saying he needed to contact the companies behind the spam and let them know.  Of course, that never got through his thick skull.
So one day I'm delivering the mail, right?  His and a hundred others.  I get to his house and open the mailbox, and the flag is up, so I'm expecting there to be something in there.  Well, I reach in, and I guess I should've looked in first, because a little noose closes in on my wrist and tightens up.  I look, and here he had a weight set up to the rope, and it triggered when I put my hand in.  So twenty feet away is the iron plate that has my hand tethered in the box.  The guy cut a hole in the back of the box for it and everything.  I'm just standing there, yelling at his house that he's committing a federal offense, but no answer.  So I get my radio with my free hand and tell the office my situation.  I try to move the weight, but it's too heavy, and the mailbox won't budge.
Then as soon as I get done talking to the postmaster, the guy comes bumbling out of his house, cackling like a loon.  I tell him I will personally help stop the junk mail, if he'll cut the rope.  At first, I think he really thought about it.  He goes back into his house, and when he comes back out, he's got a big orange bottle with a brown nozzle and a white bag.  I was really hoping he wasn't going to torch me, and luckily he wasn't.
Well, when rescue finally came in the form of a Leviathan and two of my coworkers, they found me covered head to toe with feathers, sunburnt where I wasn't.  I couldn't tell if the guard was laughing under his helmet, though I bet he was, because Jan and Ostin were.
The guy got sent away, and his mail was canceled.  So I guess he got what he wanted."
After a few more stories from each, the three parted, Xavier and Partheus to their hotel and Salina to wherever she desired.  All of Xavier's remaining strength was falling off, and he supposed he could sleep anywhere if he only rested his head for a moment.  He was thinking about that hotel bed, the one that didn't belong to him, the one that said he was just a wanderer.  His bed on Earth was the only one that he could call his own.  He had never used the one in Redora, and the one at his apartment was the property of Xavier Pontius.  Then, somehow, he found himself standing in front of that bed, oblivious to the journey from the street to his room.
Fatigue took over, and he fell asleep atop the sheets.

Chapter Thirty

One
He was standing in the middle of a street on a hot day, and it was bitterly dry.  Immediately he was aware that he was human and lacked magic.  Around him were dilapidated buildings.  The street was riddled with holes, and most of the lampposts had fallen over in some long ago time.  He noticed there was no vegetation, not on this street or the next and barely any off in distant hills.  After that, he noticed that there were no people.
He looked down at his hands and a familiar strength wrapped them.  He focused and his nails turned to claws, his skin darkened, and thick fur of jet black and blue swirls carpeted his hands and forearms.  He undid the partial transformation and moaned with joy.
"Salina!" It was the first name that came to him, so he called to her.  He was running down the broken road, head swiveling back and forth, nose flaring.  He could smell people, but didn't recognize the scents. "Salina!  Partheus!  Hodge!" These things and the names of all his friends came roaring from his mouth, but no replies came.
The wind changed direction, and he could smell the odor of burning exhausts, oil, sweat, and more blood than he wanted.  After turning a corner around a pile of rubble that had once been a drugstore, he saw a caravan of men in rusted old vehicles.  There were dead bodies on the street around them, and corpses hung from the standing posts, tethered either by rope or intestines.
The one Cecil thought to be the leader, as he was the most gruesomely decorated, aimed a rifle at the werewolf and screamed.  Cecil heard a click and the followings shots.
Two
Gunfire turned into hard knocking, and he was back in the hotel bed again, as tired as any mortal should ever get. "I'll be right there." He said and yawned.  He sat up and looked at the alarm, seeing that it was still the wee hours of the morning.  He forced himself awake.  He threw on yesterday's clothes and went to the door.  He was too short to see through the peephole, so he just flung the door open.
Bor stood in the hall in his civilian attire, arms crossed and eyes wide.  Xavier saw and looked down either side of the hall. "No one is listening." Bor said, "I already checked.  May I come in?"
Xavier gestured the aemon in.  A thought, brief and thin, crossed his mind.  It would be a lot nicer if Salina came to visit him at one-thirty in the morning.
"If I had known," Bor started before the door had closed behind him, "I may have pledged my men sooner.  But I had no idea."
Suddenly three hours of sleep felt like a dozen. "Huh?"
"I saw you." Bor's face seemed to glow. "You made it to the waterwheel.  It's just a memory from my childhood, really.  It would be different for other mages.  You beat me there, but I still saw you at the lever.  Cecil Fauden."
Xavier felt the foundational cracking of his plans the way one feels a broken nose. "You haven't told anyone, have you?"
"No, not even my brothers.  I wanted to speak with you again first."
Something slick and malignant slithered through the pseudo-novian's mind, a little eel of malice, a serpent whispering murder.  He banished it. "Please," He said, feeling walls enclosing in on him, "you can't speak of this to anyone, not until I do first.  Can you promise to keep this a secret?  I need this to stay between us."
"I can." The aemon looked baffled beneath his strange leathery skin. "But could you tell me why?  If The Order already knows-"
"They don't.  You and I are the only two people in the world that know, and they can't know.  Not yet." The slimy black snake started giving up. "They think I'm dead, and if they knew I came back, they'd be protecting me.  But I have to contribute, don't you see?  I'm the reason they're here."
Bor nodded and cocked his head as if to double-check on eavesdroppers. "Do you think they'd lock you up or something, do you really?"
He did not.  On some deeper level, he knew this was all ridiculous. "I don't know, and that's the trouble.  I promise I will tell them." Salina first. "When the time is right.  After I help them sort this all out.  Bor, I'd like to go home with them.  I want to be with them when they search for Earth, believe me."
Bor crossed a hand over his heart and grinned. "The secret is safe with me, but you humans are odd.  I'd just be out with it and have it taken care of." He lifted the hand to his chin. "Hmmm . . ."
"Is there something else?"
"I have a favor to ask, since I'm keeping this for you."
Xavier felt eased.  A bargain was binding, and it would ensure the secret was kept, at least to some degree. "Oh?"
"Tell me about Earth.  I want to know everything about it."
Xavier felt his sleep slip away.  That three hours would have to be enough.  He sat on the edge of his bed and motioned Bor to do likewise on an adjacent couch.  Then he began.
Three
Bor left half an hour before Xavier's alarm was set to go off.  They were leaving for another city today, and he supposed he might get some rest on the train.  For now though, he stood by the window and looked at the still-dark sky.  He was holding the little stone disk he'd made for Salina and held it up to the glass.  The fatigue had settled into his chest, and he felt his breathing weigh heavily upon him while his heart beat arrhythmically.  He wondered where she would be right now, when most people were still asleep.  Once upon a time he had used the extra hours to practice swordsmanship, though clumsily and perhaps pointlessly.  Was it all business for her?  He didn't know, wasn't sure if he wanted to know, and had to ponder if she used this much of the clock to think of others.  He had to abide by the mortal laws of eating, sleeping, and dying but still found room in the day to reminisce and long.  He wondered if she ever thought of him.
That morning Xavier and Partheus packed up and headed off for the train station.  On their way, they passed up Academia Celestial, a university for wizards.  Xavier was a little disappointed he didn't get a tour of the place, as seeing it from the street was but a tease.  As he passed by he looked back and saw the glass buildings of the college come to life in shifting prismatic colors.
Four
They arrived early at the station, and their train had yet to arrive.  Xavier set his luggage down, bumping the sword on his hip.  It was odd to be carrying a sword again, especially through a city.  It was perfectly legal and not the least bit outstanding, but it was still strange.  He liked it, though.  It meant purpose.  As he thought and waited, the red sun peaked over the city wall, and the white concrete around them turned a rosy amber.
Partheus, a man who was not young by any standard, sat on a bench nearby.  His legs and joints ached, and he thought it just might be a lingering effect from his jog under Bishop the year before. At least his lungs and mind felt good.  He couldn't be too far over the hill, if he wanted to keep up with Patricia.
He called to the novian, "Did I tell you?"
Xavier approached. "Not sure," He replied, "you say a lot of things."
"Miss Aston will be accompanying us on our journey.  We're headed to Garmond next, and she'll be here soon to board with us." The elf stretched, popping his back and shoulder sockets. "The train ought to be here in a few minutes."
"Aston?" Xavier mirrored.
"Yes." His brows raised. "She gave you that sword last night."
"Salina?"
"Didn't you know her last name?"
"She never told me, and I never asked." Xavier felt a guilt seeping up into him.  How did I never catch that?  Or did I, but forgot it? "Well, thank you for telling me." He looked away from Partheus. "It'll be good to have her with us."
The elf nodded.
Five
People began flooding into the station area, and the bullet train followed suit.  Ticketholders started to board, somehow sooner than the elf and novian.  Partheus told Xavier that Salina would sniff them out and there was no need to wait for her to board, but the novian lingered by the steps for a few moments just the same and scanned the crowded deck.
It was easy to pick her out from the throngs, what with her height and the long blade.  It was enough to see her, and Xavier was about to step up into the locomotive, but he noticed that she was not alone.  Beside her stood an elven man with thick golden hair and a sharp face, utterly imperial.  The two leaned closer together and exchanged a lengthy kiss.  Then he held her hands up to his face, gave them both a quick peck, and spilled out some goodbyes.  But, Xavier saw, that wasn't enough, as the two came together again.
Xavier boarded, his stomach in knots and his head light.  She had every right, he knew, and he had no right to be upset.  He ran that thought on a loop as he made his way to where Partheus sat and took up a seat by the window.  The elf made no comment.
She came a few minutes later, or it might've been a few hours.  She sat next to Partheus who had oddly taken an aisle seat.  She was right across from Xavier.  She rested the sword in a narrow space between the booth seat and the window and opened a brown courier sack on her lap.  She drew out a pad of blank white papers and a box of pencils and pens.  The pad stayed on her lap, and the box was wedged between her thigh and the wall with the open end sticking up.  She smiled and nodded at Xavier, and he returned the gesture through a herculean effort of will.
It was going to be a long trip.

Chapter Thirty-One

One
On Earth, reading had always made Xavier sick during car rides.  He tested it on the train and was delighted to find that he suffered no ill effects.  The reading was a great distraction from the soreness, but after a while his eyes grew weary and the words began to blur.
Outside was an unending plain of green grass with pine forest trim and mountain caps far beyond.  His elven companion was fast asleep, but the werewolf was on her third page, deftly sliding a pencil across the surface.  Xavier glanced away from the window to see what she was doing but only for an instant.
But she noticed his curious eyes and stopped her current project. "Would you like to see?" She asked.  Her hands were on the side of the pad, and it was clear that he was going to see it one way or another.
"I didn't know you drew." He wanted to add something about her being too much of a tomboy, but he decided he better not push it.  It would start there, and before he knew it he'd be spewing out some goop.  Maybe he wouldn't be so transparent, but he thought now was not the time to risk it. "Yeah, I'll take a look."
She handed him her set of drawings.  He looked at the first, a sketch of the station.  The next two were of the countryside they'd passed.  Each drawing was detailed.  There were things about the station, trash cans and what the people wore, that were so accurate it was as if she had traced a photograph.  The story was the same for the country, and the last one was even in the process of being colored.  She captured every color, every shadow, and every wisp of cloud.
He handed them back. "You should have these framed." He didn't think that compliment was a stretch in the least.
"Oh please," She replied, "these are just doodles."
His eyes rolled.  It was impossible to keep them from doing so. "Yeah, and the sky is just blue, and the oceans are just water."
She smiled and was glad to see his face light.  He was so much like Cecil, and thinking of him that way made her feel a bit guilty, not enough to stop her but enough that she had to consider if it really was the novian she admired, or if it was just who he reminded her of.
The silence caused Xavier to turn and look toward the inspiration of her newest art, trying to catch some of that sleep he missed the night before.
Thankfully this level of fatigue didn't care about the dance and twirl going on below the surface, and Xavier nodded off.
Two
The train rolled along, past the long wavy fields and down into a narrow valley.  Sunlight flitted through trees and rose up over the locomotive and back over the other side.  At some point Partheus awoke and commenced to fiddling with his phone.  Xavier heard the sound of brakes, and his eyes opened to Salina working on a new page and Partheus flicking his thumb back and forth to the glow of a screen.
He sat up straight and stretched.  A yawn was in order, and then he rubbed his eyes.  Oddly, he felt more awake than before, but his body seemed sorer than ever.  It had been warm in the cab last he remembered, but he found himself breaking out in goose pimples.
"So, I guess we've reached our destination?" He asked neither in particular.
Salina replied, "No, not even halfway there.  We're at Igmont now, picking people up, mostly dropping people off."
"How long was I out?"
"A few hours.  You can go back to sleep if you'd like, but you should probably stay up.  It'll be late when we get there."
The novian nodded to her.  He stood up and was caught off guard by his legs, which were filled with pins and needles.  They wobbled, but he managed to wrest control of them. "Anyone want something to eat or drink?" He was looking at the elf.
"If you wouldn't mind getting me a water, Colonel, that'd be great."
He walked to the end of the cab.  Just before the door connecting to the next caboose was a coffee station with a brewer polished to a gleam.  On the adjacent countertop was a bare assortment of hunger fighters, crackers and stale biscuits, an empty box of donuts and a few bagels.  At the far end was a watercooler, something he'd heard oft about but never seen.  It really struck him that it was so Earth-like.  He took two cups from a receptacle and filled both.  He refilled the second twice after quenching a thirst he hadn't been aware of.
The elf had given up on whatever communication he'd been having on the phone and was instead shuffling his fingers, clearly regretful he had not brought anything else to occupy his time.  Salina was taking a rest from the drawing, her tools and papers resting on her lap.  Xavier sat down across from her and reached over with the cup of water he'd not drank from.  Partheus accepted it and took a few sips before letting it rest in a fold out arm by the aisle.
Three
"So what's Garmond like?" Xavier asked.
Salina shrugged. "Never been there myself."
Partheus popped his back.  It always seemed able to pop a bit more and never capable of finding a true position to stay in. "Well," He said, "it's a swamp.  Pretty dark, too.  There's a thick canopy over the area for miles, but it's nothing like the Dark Forest.  Expect mosquitoes and alligators." His hand went to the back of his neck as if expecting one of the little bloodsuckers to have been summoned merely by the mention. "And make sure not to fill up on junk before you get there." He looked at Salina and rolled his shoulders. "Best cooks in the world live there.  There's a culinary school, place called The Stove.  From what I hear, it's more of a boot camp than anything else, but the cadets that graduate from there go on to start their own restaurants.  Always five stars."
"And what exactly are we headed there for?  You make it sound like fine cuisine." Xavier inquired.
"Beyond the quality of the food is the quantity.  You can't fight a war on empty stomachs, and the rations we have up north aren't going to cut it.  We need regular supplies with nutrients and calories.  They can help with that."
"How are we going to bargain for that kind of aid?  I hope I don't have to get into a cook-off.  I can light fires with the flick of a wrist, but I don't think I can properly butter toast."
Everyone who had business in Igmont was off the train.  New passengers were filing in to take their places, and conductors were ushering travelers along.  Hustle and bustle, and a whole lot of chatter.
"Tariffs." The elf went on. "Food tariffs in Garmond are higher than in most places.  Even without a king to enjoy the treasury, collections are still taken.  Mostly on their exports.  Drives up costs, so less people order food shipments from them.  They have more than just gourmet meals.  We just need to promise relief after our victory in exchange for some rations."
"The Leviathans really won't budge?"
"Not one iota.  It's all or bust for them."
Xavier sighed.  This was all so roundabout. "Wouldn't it be easier to infiltrate the Leviathans, cause some internal subterfuge?"
Partheus shook his head. "They've got some airtight policies.  It would be easier, but it would take decades to get anywhere."
"And it's no secret that Malachi is getting restless." Salina added. "In twenty years he'll have an army amassed.  So it's either small wars now or a holocaust later.  Sometimes fighting can be avoided altogether."
There was a screech, releasing brakes, and the train lurched forward. "I hope we can convince them." The novian breathed.
"Don't worry yourself.  I'll be the one in the arena this time around.  I daresay this city will be a break for you, not much need to showcase our military might.  Just politics."
Four
The canopy thickened and the natural light dimmed.  Darkness was coming twofold, and the cab lights came to life to compensate.  There was a little boy a few rows up asking his mother how long it would be, and over the course of time she told him on a dozen or so occasions that it would be a while yet and that he ought to take a nap.  Farther up someone else began snoring, but it was muffled by the steady hum of the locomotive.  Xavier went back to his book.
It got to a point where both Xavier and Partheus struggled to stave off sleep.  The novian found himself nodding off here and there.  It had to be the hum of the train, something about motorized transport.  Back on Earth he had always found it next to impossible to stay awake on long trips, but those had always been in cars.  He had never ridden on a train until that vacation to Mirwa, and that had been when tiredness couldn't touch him.  As a mortal, however, he found the train rather lulling.  What with its comfortable and warm booth and steady vibration, it practically dragged one down into sleep.
Book in hand he slumped over, straightened, and slumped again a few moments later.  Salina was getting some small amusement out of watching the novian bob up and down in his valiant efforts to stay awake, and a grin creased her face.  At one point he closed his book about knights and quests of truth to put it into one of his bags.  The job was simple, but it kept his hands moving and that, it seemed, was enough to keep his head above water for a few more minutes.  He became aware of his heavy breathing and that his body was falling asleep in every regard, that soon it would take a real stimulation to keep him going.
But that's exactly what he got, for a few minutes later came the gentle tug of inertia pushing him toward the engine.  He stretched, as did quite a number of passengers, and readied to grab his things.  Salina had already put away her things and sat on the other side, legs crossed and waiting for her two companions to get into the aisle so she had room to as well.  Partheus let the other passengers, even ones farther from the doors, light off first.  He turned to Xavier and said there was no need to shove.

Chapter Thirty-Two

One
It was a swamp indeed.  A great and noisy bog, where crickets and frogs knew no such terms as silence and calm, which stretched into the darkness.  This side had some pavement and walkways leading into Garmond.  The stone platform they stood on was raised higher than at most stations, and looking ahead Xavier saw that the buildings were on stilts of either wood or stone.  There was a faint mossy odor in the air, much weaker than he expected, and overpowering it was the scent of spices and meats, most notably that of fried chicken.  On queue his stomach rumbled with a tempestuous anger, absolutely demanding.
The hotel they found had a restaurant, but to Xavier's relief the kitchen was closed and would not be agitating his stomach with its pleasant scents until morning.  He hadn't thought of it before, but Salina was to stay in the same room as the elf and he.  There were two beds, and he didn't need to wonder which was hers and which was theirs, because he knew she had no need.  But still, her things would be there, and she'd be around.  For some reason he hadn't pictured it this way, as if the werewolves might stay in caves.  He had no points for thinking this way, as he himself had lived in an apartment.  In that time he hadn't even had the slightest urge to pack up and live in a hole in the ground.
They put their things away, and Salina said she was headed out to see the city while they slept.  Xavier wanted to go with her, knew he couldn't because of fatigue, and knew he shouldn't anyway.  Partheus set an alarm, but by then Xavier was already lying on top of his own bed, no more rested than he was the night before, and falling asleep on the sheets.
Two
When morning came for Xavier, the room was empty.  He did not wake to the sound of the alarm.  Xavier sat up and looked at the clock; it was almost nine.  A note was taped to the alarm.  The novian took it, wiped his eyes, and read it.
No need to wake you.  Might need you two tomorrow.
Take the day off.  Look around.
-Partheus
He took a quick shower and threw on some new clothes.  Neither the elf nor the werewolf were anywhere around, and other than the note, there was no real indication where they'd gotten off to.  Sword on his hip, he went down to the lobby.  They were still serving breakfast, so he got himself some bacon and eggs.
With that taken care of, he headed outside and into the surprisingly mild air of the bog.  He went over a few bridges to a great circular structure not unlike a gazebo.  It was the size of several city blocks and was riddled with peddlers.  In the back of his mind, he started a little prayerful dialogue, one he was ashamed to say he did very seldom.
So what should I do?  I'm running out of ideas.
An absolute feeling hit him then, quiet but resolute.  The feeling suggested he ought to come clean about who he really was and do it sooner rather than later.
Isn't there another way?  I'd really like to follow through with this.
The feeling did not sway.  Instead it seemed to tighten.
I know, but I can't.  And now she's . . . it's none of my business now.  I should've done something sooner.  If I just had the strength to accept it.
The feeling, the voice, pushed back.  It struck down his argument.
So what do I do?
The voice had already answered this.
I really shouldn't interfere.  It's unprofessional, and it's selfish.  Not to mention absurd.
The voice seemed to sigh.  It implied to Xavier that he ought to listen, not just hear.
Three
He returned later that night after a few good meals and picking up oil for her sheath.  Salina stood over Partheus who sat overtop a computer on the room's table.  They were going over some sort of form and discussing how rations of beets and leeks ought to be distributed.  Enthralling stuff, truly.
The sword was leaned against Partheus's bed, sheath and all.  Xavier set his bag on the mattress and took out a little red bottle with a narrow nozzle.  Salina passed a glance at him but didn't seem too taken by his appearance.  Getting the sword out of the sheath proved most difficult, what with his stature and his false muscle memories.  Last time this sword had been like a long dagger, but now he could feel the true weight.  He used the edge of the bed to catch the sheath and drug the blade across the sheets.  Once free the sheath tipped over with a dull thud, and Salina did turn with full attention.
There were some conflicts inside her at the sight of the novian's tampering.  On the one hand, that sword and its components were her only reminders of Cecil, her lost charge.  But the fury she expected, the natural reaction, didn't come.  Instead she was taken by curiosity at the halfling's actions, and there was something else, too.  The familiar way he looked at the blade, as if he were inspecting it for changes.  Quietly she monitored the novian's strange nicety, while Partheus pored over statistics of harvest seasons.
When Xavier was finished treating the equipment, Salina walked over to help put the sword back, acting as if she had only just noticed him.
"Do you know the history behind this sword?" She asked, looking down at her waist high admirer.
He met her gaze. "A bit." Then he looked away.  She was close enough that he could feel her body heat, and some kind of bashfulness swept over him. "That's Baneblade.  It belonged to a general named Titus."
He knew more than that, she bet.  Far too much for a janitor.  She wondered again of his past which he'd carefully erased. "Partheus," Turning to the elf, "can you handle things for a little while?"
"Mmhmm." The elf replied, flicking a wrist.
"Xavier, would you like to take a little walk with me?"
"I'd like that." He could feel his insides melting.  It was a simple request, he knew.  All the same it made his heart leap.
Four
They were outside to the minimal light of the swamp's sunset.  The drone of Garmond was behind them, while the buzz of the marshes laid ahead.  The nearest building was a fan boat pier, mostly for gator enthusiasts.  It was quiet, save for the white noise, and they were alone.  Xavier was confident this was business, but even so he found himself hiding his thumping heart.  Did she know she held that kind of power over him?  He hoped not, because if she did, she was intentionally breaking him.
"Colonel," She said, the word cold and unfamiliar, "I'm tired of wondering about you.  I've done a little research, as I'm sure you've suspected.  But I dug a little deeper than I think you anticipated."
He looked up at her.  The frosty tone was stinging him.  If it had been someone else's scorn, it would roll right off.
"You eat a lot more than someone your size should, you knew about my sword, and it's awfully coincidental that you happen to be so adept with magic and have been concealing, at the very least, your scent since we've known you.  Care to explain?  And not later, not when you're ready.  I'm ready now, and I don't plan on waiting anymore."
Well, it was good while it lasted.  One voice of consciousness sighed.  Another went, Run as far and as fast as you can. "I can't."
She nodded. "I think you're a spy." She leaned over him, her back toward the city. "In which case, we'll be getting information from you one way or another."
He couldn't bear it.  It was simply too much.  He slipped a hand into his back pocket and pulled out the little stone disc. "I need you to make me a couple promises if I tell you."
"Which would be?"
"You can't tell anyone, not even The Order.  Especially The Order.  And I don't want you to do anything hasty."
Her brows were raised, and her eyes drifting to his occupied hand. "What are you holding?"
The city was just a few hundred yards away and behind more than one willow tree, but he thought he might be standing somewhere between the ground and Orion. "I made something for you.  If you had made it, it would look a lot better, but I tried." He held it out. "Partheus says I remind him a lot of Cecil, which makes sense.  If you don't remember, the depiction is of the night Cecil came to find you.  It was his first night as a werewolf, and he didn't understand the patterns of his coat."
"Who told you about that?" She poured over it.
"I have one other favor to ask." His internal hands were on the reigns of his ruse. "Please don't be mad at me."
He took a step back.  He had spent so much time pretending to be Xavier Pontius, that turning back into Cecil felt like a loss of identity.  The chains and barriers he set up began fading away, and his shape changed.  His bones stretched into their true length, and muscles and fibers expanded.  His hair dulled from white to a human brown, and the restraints on his scent were loosened.  His voice dropped, and the sword on his hip was suddenly short.  Now he looked down at her, nervously waiting for a response.

Chapter Thirty-Three

One
There he was, in the flesh.  She could see him, and she could smell him.  "Cecil?" She ran up to him, wrapped her powerful arms about his torso and buried her head into his shoulder.
She had him just about lifted off the ground, and it hurt.  But seeing her happy nulled that pain.  Instinctively his arms went around her, and he thought again about the elf at the station.  Guilt tinged him. "I missed you, Salina."
She pulled back from him and wiped her eyes.  A quick jab from her fist met the shoulder she'd burrowed into, and she watched him reel back and fall.
"Ahh!" He blurted, propping himself up on his hands, "I'm liable to bruise."
"You'll be fine." She extended a hand.
Two
"Come on." She said and brushed off some of the dirt that had clung to his shirt. "I've got a lot to fill you in on."
He nodded and began shifting back into novian form.
"Do you really have to do that?  I'd like it if you just stayed yourself."
"I don't want to cause a stir.  Besides, I'm still me no matter what I look like."
"But the others will want to know you're back, or do you expect me to keep it a secret that I take to the grave?"
"I'll tell you what, after this whole campaign is over, and you're set to try and find a way back to Earth, I'll spill the beans.  If they happen to trust me sooner, then that's when I'll come forward."
"They aren't going to trust you.  Not fully." She closed the distance between them, looking down at him again in his false form.  He could feel her heat, could almost smell her sweet aroma. "And I'm not sure I can keep good on my promise."
His heart raced and thumped wildly, but words came out smoothly and mechanically. "Just give me a little time.  Please."
She put on a worrisome smile and stroked his white hair. "You'd better not get hurt."
He rolled the shoulder she struck, "As of late, you're the only one doing me harm."
She pulled back. "You brought that one on yourself, hon."
A few well-placed words and body language was enough to make him weak.  Every single time.  Did she even know?
All the blood was rushing to his face, and he did take the care to hide the signs of it with some complexion altering.  He scrambled for something else to bring up, or else he thought he might babble.
"So," All his strength went into changing the subject, "I have to wonder.  Are we really just gathering up allies from different cities?  You make it sound like more is going on, and I've been left in the dark."
She had very much intended the conversation keep going the way it had.  She wanted to see how far he'd take it, and how haughty he'd be about it.  It didn't take a wizard to know he was blushing under his skin, but he strafed from her deliberate line of insinuations.  That partly annoyed her.  She couldn't recall a time when she had to put much effort into working a man like dough, but the challenge intrigued her.  And the way she looked at it, she would have it no other way. "How astute.  Yes, and I'll fill you in back at our room."
Three
Walking back into Garmond was fundamentally different, he thought, than any other walking experience.  He felt much lighter, for one thing.  Walking with an old friend.  There was the feeling too, the one that initially drew him to her, the familiarity.  So long as she was by his side, he felt at home.
"Partheus," Salina began, "Will you be needing us in the morning?"
"No." The elf replied, "Thought I might, but these people have no concern for military goings-on.  I was able to settle things myself, and I've just a few details to clean up.  Then we'll leave by train sometime in the afternoon, I take it?"
"Change of plans.  We'll have new military officials to accompany you, when you need them.  But I'll be taking Xavier with me on a little excursion, and I'll need his help with the magic elements."
"Oh?" His eyes darted to the novian and then back to the werewolf.  The quizzical look on his face made him appear gaunter than ever. "I thought-"
"Don't worry about it.  We've settled all that.  Haven't we, Xavier?"
He thought he heard an inflection on his pseudonym, as if she were trying to let the elf in on the secret. "I believe so." He said.
Partheus shrugged.  He was sitting perpendicular to the room's desk, and his left arm was still resting over the keyboard of the computer. "Good to hear."
"Xavier and I will be leaving on the A.M. train.  We'll be headed south."
"Best of luck to you both." He turned back to the work. "This certainly uncomplicates things.  Think you could give me a rundown?"
Her arms crossed and she looked down at her small companion. "I would, but not right now.  Perhaps after we get back."
Cecil spoke up, "What are we doing down south?"
"Looking for something very important.  I'll give more details once we get to Blackstone.  Can you trust me till then?"
Unfortunately, he would leap from a bridge if she asked. "Yes." He replied.
"C'mon," She gestured for the door, "I'd like to stop somewhere before we head out tomorrow."
He made to follow her.
Four
As the door closed shut behind them, she asked, "Know anything about wards?"
"You're probably talking about a magic barrier, not just referring to a section or area?"
"Mmhmm, you're sharp."
He shrugged, "Not much." His feet moved him along behind her.  The hallway was just a bit too narrow to walk side by side, and he found himself trying not to stare at her gait.  It must be awful, he thought, always being ogled like that.  Almost forgetting to answer, he went on, "Any mage can make one, and any mage can break one.  The stronger one is, the easier it is to make a tough one, but it's really just a matter of time.  Even a novice could tear down a ward made by Orion, given enough time."
"Anything else?"
"I think there are types of wards.  Some just block intrusion.  Others I believe push back, maybe lethally.  I guess there might be some that are actively offensive."
Outside he took a position at her side.  They walked for a while and passed the more congested part of the market and its stalls to a quieter area with a few little shops that were self-contained.  While they went, he explained to her how he hadn't really been granted death at the Sentinel and how he had managed to return to the mortal realm.  She took note to the fact that Siegfried was finally throwing in the towel, and she said Hodge hadn't brought the fact up yet.  He wasn't too concerned about others hearing, since they were moving, and no one person would hear anything but a snippet.  He had been near bursting to explain.
She stopped in front of a tailor and motioned him to stop as well. "Wait here a few minutes." She said and walked in.
He waited, perplexed, curious.  This couldn't be what she had wanted to see, but he was patient.  Why she wanted him to wait outside was beyond him.  As he stood there, people passed by, chefs in training and food connoisseurs mostly.  They chatted about spices and exotic uses for this and that, ways to substitute one ingredient for another, and laughing about overheating something that could make a mess.  By the time Salina stepped out, Cecil's stomach was thoroughly convinced that it needed filling.
"Here." She said and handed him a plastic bag.  There was a set of clothes inside. "They're not the right size, but you seem to be able to work around that."
"Oh?" He took a look inside and pulled out a black and white gilded robe with a hood.  There were black leather shoulders on it, which looked very good for the rain.  A pair of loose looking pants came with and matched the color scheme.
"You're a wizard, in case you didn't know.  And this is pretty standard attire for your kind, though you seem completely oblivious to the fact.  Consider this a new uniform."
"Thank you." He said and closed the bag.
"Oh, try it on." She pressed, hands on her hips, "I know very well that you can keep it in repair and clean without a seamstress or wash bucket and that you don't need to strip to put it on now."
"Okay," He shrugged, "it is nice." He began melding the material into his own clothes.  There was excess, which ended up going into the bag.  He also added a wolf crest to the left breast of the robe and split the back where his legs started to add some flexibility, same in the front.  The material itself, he found out, felt mostly like silk.  To him it seemed awfully gaudy, but he supposed after visiting Achron that she was right about it being common.  He pulled the hood over his head as a test and saw the crown rest over the tip of his nose, so it was a bit long.  The sword on his hip did not seem hindered by the loose nature of the robe.
After a few adjustments, he was actually rather pleased with the way it felt and fit. "Is this what you wanted to see?  I guess it does seem more appropriate."
"Not quite, but I did want to stop here.  We've a little ways to go yet." She hummed a consideration. "By the way, you look very dashing."
"Thank you." He all but mumbled.

Five
She led him along further, and it seemed again as if they were going to skirt the edge of town.  The buildings fell away, and they walked a straight path that cropped out over a piece of the swamp beyond.  It ended a little ways away from the last structure, an overlook that gazed over the bog.  Now the sun was very low, and the crickets were starting their leg violin symphonies.  There were some pests in the air, but Cecil kept them at bay with a little warding of his own.  There was a railing on the rounded overlook.  She leaned against it, and he did likewise.
"You wouldn't mind watching the sunset with me, would you?"
"I'd love to." He replied, "So this is what you do while everyone else sleeps?"
"Take in the scenery?  Yes, most of the time."
"I'm sure you've seen thousands of sunsets."
"I have.  Not so many with a red sun, though."
He took a glance at her. "It's beautiful.  It looks so much larger than the sun of Earth." He looked behind. "I'm surprised no one else came to see it."
"Nothing special for them."
"Well, that's fine.  The company is good."
She didn't look at him, but she smiled. "Say, Cecil," She asked, "would you stick around a while before you turn in?  It isn't the sunset I brought us here for."
Very sincerely, "I'll stay as long you want."
Time passed.  The brilliant red sun did its own turning in for the evening but not until after showcasing its brilliant auras against the tilted atmosphere.  Fireflies came out, and bullfrogs added a bass to the cricket choir.  What noise rose from the city behind had died down to nil.  The platform seemed to be a singular probe on an alien marsh.  Fatigue was slowly working into Cecil, but he flicked it away with a mental wrist stroke.  If they only just stood here the rest of the evening, not saying a word, he'd gladly trade that for a day of exhaustion come the morrow.
"There!" She pointed into the dark bog dotted by fireflies. "You see it?"
He did.  There was a light far back into the swamp.  To him it looked like a lantern which might hang at the nose of a ferryman's raft. "That's no firefly."
"I'm sure you've heard of it.  On Earth we called it Saint Elmo's fire.  I think that's actually for something else, but I remember everyone saying that.  They call it 'white gas' here."
"I've never seen it in person before." He had an urge to slide a little closer to her, maybe go for a hand.  He resisted the urge, not fully sure why. "I bet this reminds you of home.  I heard you were always the tomboy.  Sure you visited swamps after dark more than once."
"I'd always catch frogs and bring them home." Memories covered up her sights. "Dad always hated that.  Told me to act more like a lady, a fine southern belle."
"I think you turned out better than he ever could've expected." He was surprised the words weren't catching in his throat.
It sounded sweet to her, but she wanted just a bit more. "You don't think I should be a little more dignified?  A little more refined?"
Still not catching. "I think you're great just the way you are."
"Thank you, Cecil." What a turn, she thought. "Tired yet?"
"Yes." He said, "But I'd like to stay even so."
She knew he was holding back.  Knowing he was interested, she had staged the farewell at the station to throw him off.
They watched the fires together until the night was short.

Chapter Thirty-Four

One
At some point in the night, he told her he would get his rest on the train, and she didn't force him to sleep.
After the sun began rising on their backs, they returned to the room and grabbed their things.  The walk gave Cecil a second wind which he knew wouldn't last.  They boarded another train with fresh tickets and took their seats.  He sat by the window and expected her to sit across from him, but she decided to take the seat beside him, almost up against him.
He was drowsy, but he didn't think he could fall asleep just yet.  The closeness, he realized, was very nice now, as he could whisper right up into her ear if he wanted.
The train wasn't crowded, and it was quite quiet, peaceful really.  As he drifted away, Salina took her sketch pad and began drawing.
Two
In his dream he was in a pine forest.  The air was cool and smelled of autumn.  He was low to the ground and after lowering his head he realized he was standing on four legs.  He twitched his muscles.  They were all there but it seemed in different spots.  A tail was back there, and it amused him to wag it back and forth.  All was in order, for wasn't this how it always was?
A silver wolf appeared before him.  He knew it was a she-wolf just by the smell.  His tail wagged without his knowing.  She ran up to him and playfully rubbed the top of her head against his neck.  She nipped at one of his ears, backed away, and made to pounce.  They tussled for a few minutes.  It was wordless, but they understood one another.
Then more wolves came.  The pack was whole again, happily reunited.  They ran into the woods, following the scent of their quarry.  It was so simple and right.  That he had ever dreamed of being human with such arduous burdens and complex concerns.  It was ludicrous.  This was the truth.
Three
The train had begun its journey in his sleep and was still going when he woke.  Judging the sun, he supposed it must've been an hour or so after noon.  Cecil found himself leaning on the werewolf, and he had the smallest tinge of shame touch him.  Oh, give it a rest.  He thought, Be glad she didn't shake you off.  He straightened and took the weight of his head from her side.  He yawned and popped his back, the silken robe making a whooshing noise against the imitation leather seat.
"Feel better?" She asked, eyes never leaving her pad.  She was drawing the two of them from the night he'd chased after her.  It was far more detailed than the rendition he'd carved.
"Yes, just a little groggy."
She smiled, "You really should let me do something about that.  Sleep is such a waste, necessary or not."
"Well, I don't mind you keeping me up." He yawned again. "But before long, I doubt I'll be able to resist any of your offers."
She continued to draw.
"Salina?"
"Hmm?"
"What did you want to be before all of this?  I mean, before you met Hodge and Nelrene."
"You mean before my neighbors drove me out?"
He nodded.  The train hummed along.
"Not a lot of options.  I could've been a secretary, a phone line operator, or a homemaker.  Oh, well I suppose I could've been a nurse." She took herself from the drawing a moment and looked at her wizard companion. "You forget what era I hail from."
He shook his head. "As if that could've kept you down.  But that's not what I meant.  What did you dream of doing?"
"Ah, now that's a bit different.  Ever read the Tom Sawyer books?"
"The first one, I believe.  Why?"
"They were the first books I read without being told that I had to.  I wanted to go to all the places Tom had and paint them."
"Did you?"
"It was a long time before I did, but yes."
He looked at his sword.  It rested against the metal wall below the window, a reminder of where he was. "I'd love to see how you depicted them."
"I'd like to show them to you.  But I have a feeling it'll be gone by the time we get back to Earth.  Who knows how far away it is.  We have the time, but my paintings won't hold up forever."
"Sorry.  I'm the reason you all ended up here."
"Hmm, no, that's my fault.  Besides, there are things on this world that no one on Earth has ever gotten to draw.  It's refreshing."
"So where'd you go first when you left home?"
"Not to Mississippi or any of the other places I wanted to see firsthand.  I also wanted to see Europe and Asia.  People said those places were a great deal different than what I was used to seeing.  But I ended up doing exactly what you did after the turn.  I went looking for Hodge and Nelrene."
"I'd like to hear all about it."
"And then you'll tell me your story?"
"Before this, what was I?  Just a kid with a weak immune system, going to school and worrying about my future.  Textbook example, me.  You've seen the coming and going of generations.  And I'd love to hear your journey through them."
"Okay.  But it isn't a grand tale." She put the pad down and saw he was still intent on hearing her story. "So . . ."
Four
"They were well gone by the time I'd become a werewolf and gotten myself branded a witch.  As far as I could tell, they hadn't left a scent behind, but really I was just a pup.  I couldn't be too sure.  All I could say is that it looked like they were headed to New Orleans, so that's the way I headed.  I thought they could give me a little more direction, maybe take me with them.  It was too bad David didn't want to come along.  He was my best friend, and I pleaded with him to come with.  But I think he was afraid to be seen with me.
It was odd being on the road by myself.  I had, as you guessed, visited the local mud holes and bayous without escort, but that was all home turf.  As a woman, it was very much abnormal traveling a main road day and night alone.  But it was also exhilarating.  I never felt so free and independent as I did walking those first few days.  And my, it was great using my legs again.
I got down to New Orleans after about a week of walking, stopping here and there to see the sights.  No sign of the werewolves I'd met, so I was starting to wonder if they'd actually headed that way or not.
I hit the local attractions, asked a few questions, got a few answers.  Everyone I talked to seemed to think I was being rhetorical. 'Seen a pair of foreigners pass through, a man and wife?'
'Oh, yeah,' They'd say, 'damn pond skippers are all over.'
I think I turned over every rock in the whole city, looking for those two.  While I was at it, I received a myriad of offers from the local fare.  People asked me if I was lost and might need help home, or maybe I simply needed company to protect me.  I was actually asked a few times if I wanted to be 'one of the girls.'
I wasn't interested in the least, so I moved on.
I had better luck after leaving New Orleans.  Next place I ended up was a little village, and I can't remember the name.  What I do remember is someone there actually had taken note to the werewolves passing through.  It seemed like they didn't hold up in the city at all, just walked right on by.  So I was farther behind than when I set off, but I finally knew for sure I was on the right track.  They were headed west, so I followed.
A couple days later I found them, and despite how it seemed like I was chasing them, they hadn't been trying to escape me at all.  Just moseying along, they said.
I told them about what happened back home, how I'd come after them, and how I wanted them to explain to everyone that I was not some kind of Satanist.  I don't remember crying, but I must have, because I remember Nelrene wrapping her arms around me like she was my mother and Hodge standing by with reaffirming words.  I don't think I ever felt so confused since.
I traveled with them for a while, and they were both very sorry about how I'd been treated.  I don't think I was really mad at them, though.  I was just frustrated with the whole situation.
After some time, I started to feel better.
We rode in a car, and we saw an airplane.  I couldn't believe either of those things were possible.
The Great War had ended, the Great Depression came, and I volunteered with the Red Cross during the next one.  I got to see a lot of those places I wanted to paint, but I wish I could've seen them another way.  
In the early 80s, I went home and tried to find David.  I knew he'd be old, probably a grandfather.  I can't say I wanted him romantically, maybe at one time I did, but I wanted to see a remnant of my old life, if that makes sense.  I knew my father was dead, so I wasn't expecting to see him.  But I wanted to see David, wanted to see if he'd recognize me after all those years.
He died in the second World War, so his daughter said.  She asked me how a young doll such as myself knew him, and I lied.  I told her he was a friend of my grandmother's.
Maybe I had wanted him to be in love with me after all those years.  I knew he was smitten with me when we were children, and it surprised me when he wouldn't come with me.  I had a crush on him, too.  So maybe I should've been the one to say something.  Maybe he'd be on this train with us, if I had.  Or maybe we wouldn't be here at all.
I got into the technical age, and I focused my time on mastering digital art.  I traveled the world and took up photography.  It was nice, and it wasn't hard keeping up with the other werewolves.  The Internet was invented, and phones started getting convenient.
I came back to the States one summer and decided to walk up the Appalachian Mountains.  I'd never been to Maine before, and I wanted to see the fall and winter go by there.  While I was on my way, a clumsy teenager dropped his flashlight, and I ended up getting involved."
"So," She said, "now you know."
"I feel like you skipped a lot of details, even though you gave me plenty."
"You'll hear it piece by piece, in time." She picked up her sketch pad again. "You should get more sleep.  We still have a couple more hours to go."

Chapter Thirty-Five

One
He awoke with a chill and found himself leaning against the wall.  Salina was off somewhere, it seemed, and the booth felt vacant.  He peered out the window.  The train was moving much more slowly than before and on an upward slant.  Cecil saw a valley far below and some smoky clouds hovering between.  In the distance he could see the vast expanse of the mountain range they'd entered.  It curved to the right, which must've been north, all the way to the edge of the horizon.
He warmed himself up and yawned.  He ached a bit, but he felt rested.  It occurred to him that he had never asked about the region, and he was a little caught off guard to see the mountains.  He had been subconsciously expecting another desert.  It was a nice change, though.
He headed to the restroom to relieve himself.  Still no sign of the werewolf, he started to wonder where she'd gotten off to.  On the way he took note of the other passengers.  They were all terribly drab looking, and he wouldn't be surprised if each and every one of them was a geologist or a mountain climber.  Cargo pants, pocketed vests, and the color beige seemed to be their only known form of attire.
The lavatory was cold and smelled of oranges, but not in the fruity variety.  It was decidedly pungent and biting like cleaner fluid.  He did his business and washed his hands, partially amazed the faucet wasn't squeezing out ice chips.  He studied his false face in the mirror over the sink, definitely tired.  He splashed himself with the somehow unfrozen water and ran a hand under the motion detecting towel dispenser three times to get an appropriate amount.  It occurred to him after that he could've done without, an early morning mistake.
By the time he was back in the hall and turning toward his seat, Salina had returned and had the window.  He took her old spot and popped his back.
"We're almost there." She said, eyes studying the landscape.
"These people." He said, "They look normal to me."
"What do you mean?"
"Well, didn't Siegfried say this whole world was filled with the 'legions of hell'?"
"Oh, Siegfried." She tilted her head in a sort of half-shrug, "I think he's one of those glass-half-empty types.  Things are a little different here, but it doesn't seem much worse than Earth."
"That's what I mean."
"Or maybe he saw something we haven't."
"Oh?"
"I've noticed it for a while, but his timing is always strange.  He always seemed to tell Hodge news too late or way too early." She waved at the sky. "And where is he exactly?  You were there with him, weren't you?"
"Somewhere in between.  Purgatory of sorts, but I don't think it had much to do with our sins."
"And how did time work there?"
"I wasn't . . . well, I never took much notice.  I just sort of readied my mind to wield magic."
"I don't think he was seeing things just how they are, but it's hard to say."
Two
A few minutes went by with no noise, save the yammer of a few other occupants, but they seemed far off.   His body was adjusting to being awake, and he found himself no longer needing his magical aid to stay warm.  Then curiosity struck him again.
"'The Order' is that the original name?"
"Hmm?"
"I mean, it doesn't seem too clever.  I feel like Arthur would make a fuss about that."
"That isn't really the name.  It's just what we've taken to calling it, for sake of convenience."
"Then what is it really?"
She scratched her head.  It had been a while. "I don't remember exactly, but it was a Latin name or maybe Italian.  Or both.  Foreign language was never my strong suit.  It means 'The Order of Magic Wolves.' A kid could come up with that."

Three
The train stopped in Blackstone a little while later.  It was a small town on the way to the southern shore, and most of the occupants remained seated while new passengers boarded.  The ones that left were almost entirely the type Cecil had dubbed "wannabe archeologists."
The streets were cobblestone and chipped up from winter.  The whole town stood on a sort of slant with the mountainside, and the place was riddled with steps leading up to everything.  A statue of a pair of dwarves was at the center of the commons, founders from when the town had ore to mine.  The roofs were thatched with pitch and pine, and the largest part of each building was built into the earth.  There were people on phones and a few street lamps, but all in all, Blackstone was a town too small to fit the modern age into.
They stayed at a little inn.  Cecil slept and as far as he could tell, Salina either never left the room or she returned before he awoke.  It made him wake up easy, but it also made him feel a little selfish.  If he would just take the offer again, he wouldn't be burdening her with his need for rest.  What was he really waiting for?  There was something, or maybe there was nothing, so he thought he could wait at least as long as it took to figure out which.
Four
They picked up a local map and headed higher up to where the cobble gave way to small grassy pastures and trails that had once been cart tracks.  The thin mountain air was cool and steadily combed over the grasses and their hair.  It prickled the back of Cecil's neck with gooseflesh and ruffled his robe back and forth.
"Can you sense any wards?" Salina asked.
"No."
"What we're looking for will be behind one.  The caves around here were dug into and made into mineshafts.  Mostly.  But I think it'll be in one of the 'dry' caves.  There are still plenty of those."
"If it's anywhere nearby, it's being concealed by a strong spell."
"Well, I've got my walking shoes on.  How about you?"
"A nice hike should do me fine."
They were passing up the last stretch of grassy pasture before the mountain gave way to its exposed rocky core.  Cecil felt removed from it all, as if he were missing something.  It was like having his mind floating atop a puffy cloud that never knew the turmoil of being a thunderhead.
Shortly after walking up an old tram road, a path branched off to one side and up a short way to a little wooden gate.  Curious, Cecil stepped away from Salina and made to examine the passage.  He leaned over the gate and saw a quaint little cemetery, where a number of violet and white mountain flowers had been planted.  The headstones were old and worn, but he could read most of them.  There were only a little over a dozen total, and half belonged to one family
Five
After returning and walking up the tram a bit more, Salina bumped him with a hip and sent him stumbling to one side.  She didn't laugh, but he could see it on her face.  He tried doing the same to her, but his size prevented him from budging her stride.  Trying again, he added a gust of wind to the hip check.  With that, he managed to slide her to the edge of the road, where a steep grade waited.  He clasped his hands behind his back and made himself appear to be innocently studying the slope on his side.  He wanted to whistle, really ham it up, but he knew it would send him into a chuckle.
He enjoyed it the search.  Though, they hadn't actually accomplished anything.  They checked a plethora of hollows, but there were no signs of wards or anything magical.  She said it could take a few weeks to find it, a month even.  He wasn't opposed to having an extended stretch with Salina, but he also knew finding their quarry sooner would be better than later.
They set up camp in one of the vacant caves.  He started a fire and took a few rations from a sack he'd brought along.  If it took more than a couple days, he'd be cooking hares over these fires and eating berries.  As he sat on a stone he'd fashioned into the shape of a chair, he expected her to get restless and wander out, but she stayed.  It didn't even appear that she had much intention to leave him, and Cecil was fine with that.  Her company was reassuring.  He ate, and she looked over the map, scratching off locations they'd already been.
"So," He asked, "what are we looking to find behind this ward?"
"Hmm?" She looked up from the map.  Her emerald eyes were sharp in the flickering light of the fire.  Wolfish, one might say. "An artifact Dahzir made and couldn't destroy."
"Black crystal then?"
"Yep." Back to the map, but she kept talking. "With an interesting focal point.  The thing is a type of reverse horn, a funnel.  He never got it to work properly, so he sealed it away in the mountain here.  It took too much effort to uncover the files on it to let it slip away."
"What's it supposed to do?  Strike fear into the hearts?"
"It will, but as a side effect.  No, it's supposed to drain magical energies from wizards, rendering them powerless.  The notes we found on it had the thing referred to as the Soul Siphon.  Since he couldn't get it to work and he didn't want anyone else to have it, he had it locked away.  But if we could find it and use it, we could take away Malachi's sorcery.  He'd be nothing then."
"And why do I have this strange feeling that I'm going to be the one getting it to work?"
"Oh Cecil, for someone so clever, I'd think you would just let me make you immortal as soon as possible.  You're right, though.  It'll take a wizard to use it, and your potential has been . . . explosive.  To say the least.  You do realize that most of those cadets you went and trained with are just now learning how to project fireballs without hurting themselves?"
There was a piece of beef jerky stuck between his molars, and it made him reconsider that bit about immortality. "Well," He took a glance at her, but she was buried in the map, "I had and still do have some great motivation."
Six
He was nodding off in his chair, but at some point, maybe five minutes later, maybe an hour, he noticed Salina standing next to him.  That was enough to keep his eyes open.  He smiled up at her and sighed.  Again, he wondered what exactly it was that he was waiting for.
She put a hand on the back of the stone chair. "C'mere a second."
He complied, and she led him to a flat spot in the cave, still in the light of the fire.  The walk there was short, but it seemed to activate all the neurons and synapses in Cecil's brain.  His body still ached with worn muscles, but he knew he could stave off sleep a while longer.
"Lay down." She said, and of course, he couldn't refuse.  The stone was surprisingly warm, and she laid next to him.
She rolled onto her side and looked at his face.  Down here they were the same size. "How long do you think you can stay awake?" She asked.
He gave it some thought. "An hour.  Maybe more.  I'm pretty tired, but I think I can manage a while."
But sleep was closing in, and his eyes grew too heavy.  She leaned up over him, whispering in his ear. "I wish you could stay up all night with me."
Cecil fell asleep.

Chapter Thirty-Six

One
The next morning he awoke alone, the floor still oddly warm below.  The fire had died, and without its crackle the mountain sounded dead.  He left the shelter and looked down at the expanse of valley.  There was a fog rolling over the lowlands that seemed thick enough to swim in.  He thought it would be very fitting if the whole of the world had been swallowed up by some puffy sea overnight, and all that had been spared was this mountain.
As he stepped into the mouth of the cave with his pack, Salina strode up from the outside.  He nodded to her and gave a good morning.  She did likewise. "So, you check more caves while I was out?"
"Yeah," She replied, "looks like we'll have to try the other side of the mountain.  It figures, because they really didn't mine that side."
"No reason for kids to wander from the village there."
"That's what I'm thinking.  No reason for anyone to wander there, ever."
"And, I imagine, it won't be easy to get to?"
"No known paths lead there, not according to this map.  We'll probably have to climb over the peak.  Or we could go back to town and see if we can't hire a guide that would know."
Cecil shifted his bag and looked at the sword on his hip.  His eyes ran over the complex network of patterns on his robe.  It could be easy to forget he was a wizard. "I might be able to levitate us to the other side.  I'm not good at it yet, per se.  But, I might still be able to do it."
"Really?  It would save us time."
"Yeah, but it won't be a flight on first class."
"As if I care, but . . . will it wear you out?"
"Uhm, yeah it'll use up a good bit of energy.  I can pace myself, though."
"What happens if you run dry?" She knew the answer.
He shrugged. "We'll both fall and tumble down the mountain.  Your clothes with get all scuffed up, but you'll be okay.  Me?" He shrugged. "I just need to practice."
"That's not very reassuring, Cecil.  I think I'd rather try the guide."
He sighed. "It's not like my batteries are just going to quit all of a sudden." As if to reinforce the point, a chilly gust swept over the mountainside, snapping him into full wakefulness. "If I start getting tired, I'll let you know.  You could grab onto a rock or dig in with your claws or the sword and hold us there till I catch my breath."
She rolled her eyes. "Fine." She conceded. "But you had bettered know what you're doing."
"I've never needed to know that before."

Two
They found a spot where the slope to the other side was shortest.  She wrapped her arms around him, told him again not to overdo it, and he began ascending with her and their gear in tote.
The wall was almost at a ninety degree, so he had to stop hovering right over the ground and begin a direct rise.  He wondered what a spectator would think of this.  It must look like she's clinging onto an oversized luck troll.  The thought amused him and took his mind away from the strain he was beginning to feel.  The wind howled around them, and he did his best to stay steady, but it was slow going and the mountain seemed to stretch above him forever.
About halfway up he began breathing heavily.  Salina loosened her grip a bit and asked if he was going to make it.  He reassured her, but he was starting to wonder.  It certainly hadn't sounded like such a hard thing to do in the planning phase, but the execution part was taking its toll.
