When they crested, he set them down on the peak.  The ground here was noticeably colder than it was below, and there was a distinct lack of soil and, by extension, vegetation.  He took a moment to catch his breath and take in the view.  It wasn't until the panting slowed down that he realized she was rubbing his shoulders.  And as if knowing this made it more affective, he felt a great deal of tension and ache leave him, although the strain he was under was very minimally physical in nature.
She stopped and patted his back, "You going to be alright?"
"Yeah, it's easier to descend anyway." He rolled his shoulders in a subconscious attempt to let the massage sink in further. "But I just thought, what's the plan to get back after we get down to that side?"
She got up and walked over to where the peak began its sharp drop.  She leaned over the edge, her sword raising like a crystal tail. "It doesn't look like either of us thought of that." She examined that side a few moments, poised as rigidly as a statue. "There are paths down there, old ones, but still.  People made them, and I doubt they floated over the mountain."
"I bet if there's no direct trail, there's probably a tunnel.  Could be caved in, though."
"That's possible.  Do you think we could manage with a cave-in?"
"You mean clearing one?  My telekinesis isn't so bad."
"Maybe we should just worry about it later, Cecil.  You're going to end up overthinking, and I don't think we're going to run into too much trouble getting back.  Finding the relic and breaking the ward are going to be challenges to think about."
He horsed his lips, stood up and stretched. "I remember when all I had to think about was whether or not I wanted to pack a lunch or eat the school slop.  I also remember worrying about shipments of chips and strung-out teens that puked on the floors."
"It sounds like you miss all that.  Guess you'll be going back to that after this is all said and done, right?" She was grinning at him.  It was mischievous.
"Oh yeah," He replied, "that was the life.  Not like this is a hundred times more interesting and enjoyable.  Nothing like that.  I really look forward to being on my hands and knees again with the smell of ammonia burning my nose."
"To each his own.  Maybe I can hook you up with a crystal mop set or a military grade floor buffer."
"Would you?  Those floors are a real battle.  Oh, and you should get me an enchanted black crystal trash can.  A few, even.  They have to be set to have no bottom.  And a security guard for the basement."
"Sure, I'll write that all off."
He walked over to the ledge and leaned over. "Eh, I think I might stick with this.  It looks and smells a lot better." He extended an arm to her. "Ready to get down there?"
"If you are."
He nodded.
Three
The descent was indeed easier, though he hadn't been too sure that saying so would make it true.  Even so, he took a few minutes to catch his breath.  The air filling his lungs seemed warmer than the air of the other side, and he wasn't sure if that was a symptom of pressing himself, or if it was actually from the temperature.
"You know," Salina said, already peering around for signs, "I used to be terrified of heights."
"Really?  I didn't take you for the fearful kind."
"I'm pretty sure everyone has at least one phobia."
"Do you still have any?  You don't seem too flighty around all these ledges."
They began on up the path. "A few." She said. "They aren't conventional fears like spiders and tight spaces."
"So then, you're afraid of clowns?"
"Oh yes, horrified."
"Really?"
"No." She shot him a grin, and he rolled his eyes.
"But seriously, what are you afraid of?  Outliving the world and being cursed to roam it forever?  That was something I'd been thinking of, when I was a werewolf."
They came to the mouth of a cave.  It steadily released a whooshing draft and appeared as if man may never have gotten his grubby hands on it. "Not really." She stepped in first and gave the air a whiff, shaking her head that nothing and no one had been in here for a very long time. "It doesn't go well, worrying about things so far off.  Right now I'm afraid you're going to get yourself hurt.  Looking down the line, I'm a little concerned we won't ever find a way home."
The cave narrowed, and he used his size to shimmy through a passage. "Dead end." He shimmied out. "It's like you said, though.  By the time we even could get back, you'll have lived here longer.  Think it might become home by then?"
"Do you really think this world could ever be home?"
He shrugged.
Four
There was no luck that day in finding anything but topics of conversation.  That night he sat and went over the map, and once again, Salina didn't seem willing to let him doze off alone.  She sat across from him, sword laying on the ground.  It was easy for him to forget how small she was in his false form and without the lengthy blade hanging on her back.  Here and there she poked the fire with her hands.  It was odd, but he supposed he would too, if burns were no issue.  When he was satisfied with the map and where they ought to head come dawn, he set it aside and smiled at her.  But she was looking solely at the fire, eyes downcast and a sour expression set upon her face.
"Salina?"
She looked up.
He walked around the fire and sat next to her. "I can't read your mind, but I still know something's bugging you."
"Perceptive, aren't you?  Can you figure out what it is?"
He scratched his chin.  It didn't itch, but it was a nervous tick, he supposed. "I can take a good guess.  Since, you did tell me your two present fears."
"I don't understand what you're waiting for.  You can keep it a secret from The Order all you want, but I saw how exhausting going over the mountain was for you.  If you'd have run out of steam, sure I could've caught you.  But not both of us.  We'd have both tumbled down the mountainside.  And I'd be holding your mangled body by the time we stopped."
There was a dam in his mind, and one of the levees was crumbling. "You're right, Salina.  You are.  I don't know what I'm waiting for, really.  I just got so caught up trying to help from the background." He sighed.  His cheeks felt like they'd been rubbed with red coals and his hands dipped in liquid nitrogen. "I'm just an idiot is all."
She wiped her eyes. "Yeah, you are."
He wanted to let her know how he really felt but knew he couldn't.  His whole head must've been dipped in magma.  His heart fluttered, and his breath felt light and airless. "Well, no more waiting.  Go on, do it."
Her eyes widened, and her lips parted in a smile. "Serious?"
"Yes.  I can't bear having you so worried." He tilted his head.  There were tears streaming down his face, from relief and happiness he guessed. "My throat's yours, Salina.  I offer it freely."
The smiled widened, and her face changed.  He had forgotten how strangely smooth the transformations were, and how fast.  Another blink, and her human head with its blonde hair and fair skin was replaced by a wolf's with brilliant silver fur.  She gleamed almost a gold in the light of the fire, and the light danced resplendently as she lowered her head to his level.  She pushed him over.  Her jaws open, revealing the razor white, and she descended on his neck.

Chapter Thirty-Seven

One
The next morning he awoke with a stiff neck, which didn't surprise him.  His plans were foiled last night, and he supposed Siegfried would be jubilant.  Cecil sat up, rubbed his neck, and felt the result of his perforated skin.  There was scarring on his throat, which wouldn't normally make sense.  The bite had been shallow, but it wasn't just a bite; it was a covenant.
He grabbed his things and concealed the scar with an illusion.  Doubtful anyone would see it before it cleared up on its own, but it couldn't hurt to be safe.  He walked outside, knowing she would be there to greet him.
"Morning!" Salina said, as Cecil stepped into the day.  Her voice was noticeably lighter, and it was obvious to him that a burden had been taken from her.
"Good morning!" And it echoed. "I hope you know you played me like a fiddle last night."
"I'm aware." She gave him a coy grin.
He shrugged. "Actually, I'm glad you did.  Just thought I'd let you know that I'm onto you."
She closed the gap between them. "Oh no!  What if you catch me?"
Two
As the day went on, he felt his vigor drain more swiftly than usual.  At first he assumed it was nothing but the exertions from the day before, but soon his body ached all over and he took to coughing.  It was nonsense, since he'd immunized himself to illness, and he had made sure to adjust himself to the thinness of the air.
They called it a day a few hours early, and he didn't resist the idea of it, couldn't.  He made a fire in a shallow cave, as per usual, and found himself soaking in its warmth.  He was shivering, and his lungs ached.  All his muscles felt tight and knotted, and it was all he could do to eat some rations.
Salina felt his forehead and bit her lip, "Cecil, you're burning up."
He took a swig of water.  He knew without it, talking would feel like chewing sand. "The thing is . . . I can't get sick.  I'm well aware of all the bacteria and viruses that come near my body, and I eradicate them.  All of them.  I don't know what this is, but I'm not sick.  There's nothing in my system."
"You're coughing, you're pale, you're running a fever, and you're fatigued."
He didn't want to go back.  By the time they could get to a hospital, he'd either be dead or immortal. "This isn't a side effect of the bite?  That's all I can think of." And it was hard to think.  His brain seemed to be in a pan, sauting itself in feverish juices.
"Possibly."  She lowered her head.  All that worry that seemed to have lifted from her from before reappeared.  It made her older. "There's another reason why we don't give out lycanthropy to those who seem to need or deserve it.  It's why they were mad at me at the start for not consulting everyone first.  I thought since you already had it, you wouldn't have the issue.  Cecil, it's usually lethal.  Instead of becoming a werewolf, most people die."
He leaned against the cold wall, hoping it would warm up soon.  There was something especially awful about being sick this time around.  Maybe it was the fact that he hadn't been ill in years, but he thought it was more likely the fact that he wasn't home.  He was on another world, sniffling and hacking.  No box of tissues, no glass of OJ.  Still, he had someone that cared about him, and he supposed that was most important.  It looked like he was going to be a martyr after all.
She sat and leaned with him. "I lied to you about David.  I never went back looking for him, because I'd already tried to turn him.  He died from it." Her eyes fixed blankly on the fire. "How bad do you feel?"
"Bad." He replied, "But I've felt worse.  A lot worse.  I think I'll be alright."
"Two more nights."
"And then no more worrying.  Do you think The Order will be happy to see me again?  I think the first thing I'll do is clobber Arthur over the head.  Just for laughs."
"They all miss you, even Arthur.  They'll be glad to know it's been you all along."
Three
There was a long silence as the two listened to the crackle of the campfire.  He was wheezing here and there, and she wasn't breathing at all.  Oddly enough, he was enjoying it, just sitting with her near.  He guessed when his body wasn't getting used, he could almost forget his aches and pains for her comfort.
"Salina?" He finally hummed, on the verge of sleep.
"Hmm?"
"Do you think there are other worlds out there?  We all wondered if there were any, and now we know there's at least one.  Since there can be two, don't you think it stands to reason there might be a third, a fourth, maybe hundreds?"
"Yeah, I guess there probably are more.  But I have to wonder if they're reachable.  Maybe there's only one per dimension, if those are even separate things."
"Siegfried told me something was coming."
"Oh, and what's that?"
"I don't know.  I saw on the news a while back that there's a comet coming by.  Wouldn't it be funny if it turned out to be something else?  Like a ship?"
She sighed.  It was something to consider soberly, but it was probably just his fever. "Yeah, that'd be something.  How about you get some sleep, and we'll talk more in the morning."
He hummed an affirmative and dozed on her shoulder.


Four
The next morning he slept in.  He wasn't happy about that, but he needed it, and she must've thought so, too.
"How are you doing this morning?"
"As good as I think I can be." He shrugged. "I've got a little get-up-and-go in me now.  Don't think it'll last all day.  In fact, I'll probably have to turn in early again."
"We should get you some help."
"How would we get back over?  Besides, if this is from the bite, I don't suppose anyone on Destursha is qualified to treat it." He coughed up some phlegm.  It was thick, dark, and tasted sickly of iron. "Not tonight but tomorrow night, we won't have to worry about it, right?  I can easily go that long without clerics."
The pacing was slow that day.  He ran out of breath after only a few minutes of walking, and she had to carry the bulk of the gear.  The coughing became persistent, and his lungs started to ache grievously.  He knew there'd be blood coming up with the phlegm.
In a strange way, though, it was interesting being this miserable.  It had been some years since he had a cold, and the most recent bout of physical pain had been smashing his toe in his apartment.  Not that he could say he'd miss this when it was over.  But it was an experience all the same, something to write about later.

Five
Later on, shortly before calling it quits, a concern struck him. "Salina," he asked, "you don't seem affected by magic, but can you wield it to any extent?"
She hardly seemed to be a person as much as a moving pile of packs arranged around a sword, but lo the mound speaketh, "I can light candles, maybe turn pages without touching them.  I just don't have much use for magic, and I'm told I don't have much 'aptitude.' Why do you ask?  Don't think I'll be much of a sparring partner for you."
"Oh, it's not that.  I was just wondering, because I'd never touched the stuff before.  I'm wondering if that was because I could or not.  You know it would be a little discouraging if I become a werewolf only to lose my wizardry.  I mean, I'd prefer the lycanthropy, but if this magic hasn't been useful."
"Making mountains out of molehills.  Nelrene and Marianna have a small penchant for the art, but the rest of us, excluding you, are pretty much hopeless in that department.  And that's a pretty normal margin to fall in.  When we started our training courses for mages, I did a little research into it.  One in five people can't use magic whatsoever.  Only one in a hundred can develop the skill.  About one in ten thousand can get it to a point of self-sustenance, like Dahzir had with his couple thousand year life.  It's one of the reasons clerics get paid so well and why militaries have always valued them.  They provide a unique skill.  I'm not sure what percentile you're in, but there are no records of anyone having such explosive growth with their skills.  You're where most skilled wizards are after a good forty years of specialized training."
"That is . . . very reassuring."
"That was the intent."
Six
That night, while he was having a bout of cold sweats, there was a buzzing sound coming from Salina.  It was a phone, he realized, almost forgetting the things were around.  She answered and walked to the entrance of that night's cave.  He was in a strange state of wakefulness right before slumber, where everything sounded louder.  It was obvious she was going for signal, not secrecy, and it seemed he could hear all the same.
"Yes, Hodge?" She said into the night.  A pause as she listened, and Cecil could not hear the ear speaker. "Yes, he is with me." A few more seconds. "Oh, don't worry.  I'm keeping a good eye on him, and he's behaving."  A long pause. "Really?  There isn't much mountain left to wander.  We'll probably have it within a few days." She started pacing back and forth, her free hand rubbing at her temples. "Can you get by without us?  You'll have to, but we'll be there as soon as possible." She stopped and sighed. "I promise you he isn't going to betray us.  No need to slap on a leash, okay?  Keep me posted.  We'll bring the siphon up, and this'll all be cleared up in a couple months." A lengthy pause. "Yeah, I wasn't expecting it yet, either.  Which, makes sense, doesn't it?  Anyway, I'll let you go.  Thanks for warning us.  I'll report in a few days." She came back in.
"You awake?" She asked the curled up ball.
"Yep."
"Did you hear?"
"Your half, yeah.  Being called back in early?"
"Malachi laid siege to Fraushein.  Our allies haven't been able to provide reinforcements, and we're losing ground."
"Never a dull moment."
"This is serious, Cecil.  People are dying."
"And there's not a thing we can do about it here.  We'll get this thing, we'll disable his army, we'll all eat cake."
"We're hardly cut out for this.  Hodge had some war experience on Earth, and so did the others.  It's different here.  People kill with their minds.  There are less tangible forms of warfare."
"If only the Leviathans would let us borrow the Phoenix.  They've been cooped up in that for a while.  And seeing that float towards Daskin would send a message, wouldn't it?"
"I think the generals are going to try and clean up the scraps from the war, let us fight each other, and then take over themselves.  I don't buy their statements about adherence to the king's postmortem fail-safes.  Telling us they'd concede after we overthrow the government, and I don't know what Malachi thinks about it, but I'm sure he knows more than we do."
"So, we're really fighting a couple wars?"
"Just get some sleep.  I need time to think.  Tomorrow night we'll have all the time in the world to bounce around ideas, but you have to get that far.  God, you look ill."
"Can I ask you something first?" The words were garbled, and he cleared his throat.
"Yeah, go ahead."
"Now that there's no turning back, I want you to be honest with me.  Ever since Garmond you've been showing me a lot of affection." He was already out of breath.  He wheezed. "Why?"
"What do you mean?  Because I want to."
"Yeah, and I'm grateful, but . . ." His throat was starting to hurt from the dry air, and he could feel the rise of blisters. "I don't know for sure why you want to.  I feel like I'm just an asset.  You don't have to lie to me if that's the case.  I can understand why you'd manipulate me, if that's all it was.  Bring me back into the fold."
"You're my responsibility.  Have been since we got here." She was sitting by him now, but he didn't seem to notice right off.
"Never mind.  I'll settle for that." He wanted to ask about the elf at the station and a dozen other things, but he refrained.  There was a sudden and immeasurable feeling of wrongness to his line of questions.
"You don't seem satisfied.  Why don't you get whatever it is off your chest?"
He smiled. "I would like to very much.  Maybe one of these days I will."

Chapter Thirty-Eight

One
Cecil awoke gasping and coughing in some wee hour of the night.  Salina was nowhere to be seen, and he had the horrible sensation that he was about to suffocate.  Blood came with the phlegm, to his dismay.  He rolled over toward the wall and began gagging.  His throat seemed to shorten, there was a small acidic sensation, and then he was emptying his gut on the ground.  The gagging was so overwhelming that he could feel his face fill with that blood of his, and the choking feel came into full force.  Some moments went by, and he wondered what would happen if he was not able to draw breath in again.  It would go out and not return.  Just a few more hours.  He thought, though another day looked a lot like an eternity.
The choking stopped.  He continued to cough, but in the moments between, he took in shallow gasps of air.  That too slowed, and he regained some stability.  He felt his forehead, it was hot and drenched in sweat.  The hand shook of its own accord, and it dawned on him that he was freezing.  He crawled closer to the fire, wondering if another day would be possible.  He reached over into a knapsack, a feat far more grueling than it should've been, and withdrew a canteen of water.  He drank, and it hurt.  There were blisters in his throat now.
He faded in and out of consciousness, mostly in, for a few hours.  He didn't feel like he was getting worse, and experience told him early morning and late evening were the worst times for sickness.  He'd pull around come noon and be flying high until later that night.  Of course, come night this would go away.
Unless it doesn't.  Unless it kills you.  Maybe here, you'll just die, no turning.
He pushed the thoughts away.  If that was the case, it was too late to do anything about it.
Two
Salina walked in, face like a stone. "Oh good, you're up." She frowned. "How are you feeling?"
He shook his head.
She noticed the vomit in the corner, sighed, and went to him. "Just a little while longer."
He opened his mouth, and the cool air stung.  He couldn't clear it this time.
"Let's not worry about it right now.  How about you stay here today?  Take it easy, and I'll go searching.  It'll be harder, because I can't detect it like you, but I'd rather you not complicate your condition.  If I don't find it, we can look together tonight.  If I do find it, I'll let you know."
"You think I'll die, don't you?" He swallowed, and did it ever hurt.
"No." She lied. "Not if you stay here and keep warm."  But she thought he might anyway.  All she could do was hope he'd stick it out a little while more.
He nodded, keeping the moisture in his mouth.
"Unless you want me to stay here.  Keep an eye on you like Hodge wanted." She smiled, but it was a front.  If he wanted her to stay, then he was in worse shape than she thought.  If he told her to go, and she did, she wouldn't feel right about leaving him.  No winning here.
He motioned toward the entrance with a tilt of the head.
She made for the exit but decided to only pretend to leave.  To hell with the urgency of the siphon.  She rounded the outside and waited for him to fall asleep.  It didn't take long.
Three
The fever dreams started mild.  Wispy scenarios like getting a license picture, seeing that the new president of the U.S. was an aemon, and celebrating his cousin's thirtieth birthday, even though they were the same age.  It went downhill from there.  There were dreams about being lost in caves, dreams about spiders crawling into his mouth, and half lucid ones where he was compelled to hurt someone he didn't want harmed.  Most of it was gobbledygook, but it descended down to a grounded place.  And the nightmare began.
He woke up in a posh Victorian room.  He crawled out of a beautifully gilded mahogany bed, and it seemed like it was his.  He looked down at his bare legs, his bare human legs, and the striped boxers.  They were Fruit of the Loom, not a brand he thought he'd be seeing again.  Outside the wind was howling, and though the room was bright with curtains drawn, he felt the night.
He got out of bed and walked over to a dresser.  The top drawer had shirts, middle pants, bottom socks and underwear.  Everything was neat and orderly, dusted and arranged to perfection and not without a citrus scent.  He heard voices coming from outside, and it seemed a long ways off.
At the door was a pair of sneakers, not surprisingly bearing a check mark on the sides.  He put them on and stepped into a candlelit hallway.  The architecture screamed of Earth, and the product placement reinforced the feeling.
One side of the hall was a wall of windows, displaying a courtyard with assorted flowers and a fountain.  There were statues and hedge trimmings, and he did not care.  He felt rather good, healthy even.  Whatever sickness he'd gone to sleep with was long gone.  That begged the question, just where had he gone to sleep?  There was an odd knotting sensation in his gut and a tide of dj vu.  It had to have been here in this . . . manor.  Somehow, though, that seemed wrong.
The place was eerily deserted, as he checked each bedroom, bath, ball, and kitchen.  Hours melted away as he looked for life, or at the very least a way out.  He could find neither of these, and all he got was a growing sense of unease.  He knew someone was here.  There was no corporeal feeling to indicate that, but he knew.  The way a mother knows when her children are lying, or how certain clerks know when a robbery is about to happen.
The upstairs and first floor were vacant, and he had an urge to go into one of the empty rooms, hide in the closet, and wait for something.  He had to go into the basement, though.  Had to.  Because . . . he just had to.  But it also seemed wrong like it was a trap.  Yes, don't let it come to you.  You go to it.  What "it" was his subconscious would not elaborate.  He no longer felt comfortable walking around unarmed and was a little disappointed he hadn't found a sword hanging on a wall.  But, why would he carry a sword?  That was dated tech, and he had no idea how to wield one properly.  Certainly a gun would be better to defend him from . . . and again, the mind went blank.
The basement entrance was in what he supposed was a parlor, or was it a bureau?  This was not his element, but whatever it was called, it seemed odd that no doors led outside.  He descended a wide set of steps with blood red carpeting into the belly of the mansion.  The carpets in the rest of the house had been a somehow distinguishing looking brown, and the change of color ran a chill up his spine.  It made change.  It meant that if he found no one above and no way out, he would find something down here.  And some sage and primal part told him that he may not want to find what he was going to.
The walls were lit by candles, dimly flickering flames casting stark shadows upon what had become stone walls.  There were electric lights embedded in the ceiling and switches to match, but these did not seem related to one another.  There was no tangible power, but Cecil knew there was some kind of energy crackling about.
He tried using his magic, but he couldn't.  It reinforced the idea that he might be back on Earth, but where else would he have ever been?  He could swear there had been another place, felt it with that primal gut, but the feeling seemed to be weakening, dying.  And for that matter, wasn't magic just some sleight of hand, smoke, a rabbit or two and sleeves?  All at once, he was quite positive he was insane.
Though, he had been somewhere else, and there had been magic.  It was like remembering a dream that had been forgotten for years.  It was all bleeding up to the surface.  The werewolves, the people, the dying and coming back.  It was foggy and not altogether there, but it left him wondering how he ended up here.  Somewhere here there had to be answers.  He kept going, and as he did, his skin began to feel quite alien.
He observed some paintings on the walls, nothing he'd ever seen before.  Part of him thought the landscapes were in Europe and Asia, since he'd never been.  That wasn't right, though.  The places depicted were not on Earth.  There were gorgeous cities and emerald oceans, and people as fantastical as fairytale characters.  If any of it was real, whether he'd ever seen it firsthand or not, he would've read about it.
He had a sinking feeling, as though he were falling into himself, into madness.  He hastened his pace.
Five
At the end of a hall was a double set of ebony doors with golden trim and silver handles.  The wood had been carved in the fashion of exquisite swirls and branching arcs.  It looked very much like a sculpted mural of tree roots, and the curved surfaces gleamed with polish.  It drew in attention like a black hole draws in light, and he felt compelled to see what was on the other side.
The doors were surprisingly heavy and at first refused his efforts.  But after applying a little more pressure, a little more intention, they pushed open.  Light flooded out from between before settling, and he saw what was inside.
There was a man standing at a table.  Iron sconces lit the room, and the firelight made him appear as a statue.  On the table was a map with little red and blue pins scattered about at different points.  The man wore an embroidered green robe.  There was a monogram on the chest with the letter "M" in red surrounded by white fire.
The man looked up from the map.  His face was sharp and angular, his ears long and pointed, his long hair as silver as polished steel.  He was an elf, Cecil knew.  But not like the others he'd seen, or perhaps dreamed of.  Yes, this man was narrow, but he seemed tightly packed, spring-loaded as it were.  There was no trace of weakness in any form about his posture, and his eyes matched the robes, a twin set of green jades.
"Oh." He said.  His voice was deep and graveled, paralleling his refined looks. "I wasn't expecting you.  I've been trying to summon you, but you've resisted.  I see you're even farther away now, so I suppose something has you ailed.  It's very bad form for a mage to take ill, you know."
The words seemed heavy, but Cecil forced them out. "Who are you?"
"Malachi Hierophante.  I once went by the name Ashtoreth, but that's archaic.  No one will recognize me by that these days." He made a sweeping motion, and all the flames died.  Electric ones switched on, and darkness was banished. "And you go by Xavier Pontius.  Don't look surprised, please.  It's only natural for enemies to keep tabs on one another.  Your real name, and this is a surprise to me, is Cecil Fauden.  I had no idea.  Truly, you are a wonder.  Saying it's rare to die and come back is, of course, an understatement.  You'll have to tell me what it's like sometime."
"Why am I here?"
"Because I want you here.  I've wanted to talk with The Order's new colonel for some time.  I thought maybe I could persuade you to take my side.  I hope you don't see this so black and white, that I am the bad guy, and The Order represents good.  I just want my side to be heard."
Cecil's eyes furrowed.  This whole situation seemed off.  If it was real, it was real.  If it was a dream, then it would just need to run its course. "I couldn't turn on them, if that's what you want.  You'd be wasting your breath to try."
"I know." Malachi replied.  His face drooped, and it looked very much like genuine disappointment. "I wouldn't ask you to.  When I thought you were freelance, I had hopes to have you see things my way, but I couldn't ask you to betray your friends. 'Victory won by evil is banished in the swift dawning light.' It's a quote from Faust, one of the first and greatest crystal architects."
Cecil shrugged. "If you can't convince me, I'd like to leave."
"I'm not evil, Cecil.  I want you to understand that.  I've been gathering forces for a very long time, because I was disgusted by the way the late king treated his dominion.  I planned on overthrowing him and championing a new era of freedom."
"If that's true, why did you attack Fraushein?  We have little claim over this world, so I don't see why this can't be resolved peaceably.  What's your angle?
"I need the full support of the Leviathans.  They've promised it, if I take care of you and yours."
"They've made a similar deal with us."
"And I have no doubt that someone greedy in their ranks will try to clean up what's left of the winner.  I am prepared for that.  Are you?"
"We adapt."
"You've lost your powers, haven't you?  You're a superb mage, but you've lost what made you like the others.  It was taken from you when you died, was it not?  Is it true that your friends can bestow it on others?"
"Yes, but it doesn't usually take."
"You aren't convinced of my merits, because we are enemies.  I understand that, but please, I mean no ill-will toward you and yours.  This is simply the way of things." He drew a sword from under a fold of the robe.  Green crystal. "I don't suppose you'd forgive me for this, but, we are enemies after all.  And the less loss I have after this war, the better a chance I'll have of annexing the Leviathans and uniting Destursha.  You'll get in the way, because it is your duty." He took a step around the table. "I don't do this out of greed, or anger, or insanity.  This is necessity, and I am sorry." He advanced.
Six
Unarmed, Cecil instinctively turned and fled from the room.  The elf was fast at his heels and casting magic.  All the memories which had seemed faint before were bright and to the forefront.  His strength was still meager in the arcane elements, an effect from whatever sickness was claiming him.  However, he managed to keep his back from being accosted.  He wasn't sure where he would go, wasn't even sure if this was real.  A part of him wondered if he could even be hurt here.  Sure, he was in his human form, but he thought it was some conscious projection.  That his actual body and tether to the mortal realm was likely safe occurred to him, but the risk was great, and testing it sounded unwise.
In this form he had a good stride, and after a little jaunt he seemed to lose the governor.  He stopped to catch his breath around a corner, listening for footsteps.  He heard some running down another corridor and knew he had a few moments to rest.  Whatever spell had him here needed to be broken, but he wasn't so sure he had the strength for it.  And if this was all real, he could be a few moments from death.  He listened, and he heard echoing steps a ways off.  Good.  He said a short prayer, since it seemed like the best time for it.  As usual, he felt a tinge of guilt for waiting until he was in crisis for it, but it was better done now than never.
In an instant there was a green blade coming into his field of vision from behind.  Instinctively, Cecil raised his arms, and he did so just in time.
The blade pulled back quickly and sliced open his forearms.  Had they not been there, it would've been his throat.  He yelled in agony as hot blood, his blood, poured from veins that were only there in essence.  The pain of it was all very real, and he slammed back with all his weight into Malachi.  The governor tried to grab on, but Cecil thrashed and sent the elf sprawling.
At this point Cecil realized he was going to bleed out.  Not as fast as he would've, but fast enough that this battle was essentially over.  He turned around, determining to take the governor with him, and tackled the elf while he still had strength in him.
Cecil somehow managed to knock the sword away, and it fell to the red carpet.  His blood was trickling out, and his fingers were starting to numb, his head getting lighter.  If he could just wrap his hands around the throat for a few minutes, it would do.  Then he could die.  He could die again.  A passing consideration went by, while he was lowering two bloody hands to the elf's neck.  It always had to be gory when he went, didn't it?
Unsurprisingly, the elf threw Cecil off with a gust of magical force.  And with that, all his hopes were dashed.  He fumbled backwards, distancing himself enough that the next swing couldn't hit him.  All he could do now was buy a few more seconds.  Cecil wasn't sure just how much fluid he'd lost, but he supposed he would probably be dead already or at least passed out if it weren't for the adrenaline pumping through him.
Malachi grabbed his sword and quickly got to his feet.  Cecil felt an overwhelming rush of stupidity and shame wash over.  He'd forgotten in the heat of it that the sword had been up for grabs.  He could've gone for that instead of scrambling, and then maybe . . . but it wouldn't do to regret it now.  Too late to mope.  If he could just break this spell.
Cecil began hobbling away as quickly as his oxygen starved muscles would let him.  Numbly, he felt a hand grab his left shoulder.  He knew that in a moment that sword would go right through his back.  He wasn't going to have another chance to tell Salina how important she was.  He wouldn't get to tell Hodge he'd come back from the dead, wouldn't even get to clobber Arthur over the head.  He'd never see Earth again.  In a fraction of an instant, he considered all the ways he failed and how if he'd only listened sooner.
Time was at a crawl, and he could feel the blade move forward, but it was off course.  Instead of piercing his back, it went by his side.  The flat of the blade was cold, even outside his shirt, and Cecil turned to see a shocked Malachi stumble toward the wall, sword flying off.  He was being attacked by a great black wolf.
"The hell?" Malachi blurted and furiously began casting any detrimental spell he could imagine at the beast.  It didn't seem affected and tore one of the sleeves. "Hellhound, I banish thee!" And again, it did not care.
This beast's back was higher than Malachi's head.  It was larger than a draft horse, somewhere between that and a van.  Its eyes were blazing blue and possessed a sharp intelligence.
It didn't seem to hurt Malachi, but it drove him away.  The wolf was growling, and it sounded muffled.  Cecil knew he was going to faint soon, but he tried concentrating on the wolf.  It was, he realized, a part of him.  And it had come when he needed it, whether or not that would matter, he was unsure.  It turned to him and charged.  Cecil didn't flinch or throw up his arms.  This wasn't an attack; it was his strength coming to him.
It grabbed Cecil firmly in its jaws and ran with him, the way a mother wolf might carry her pups by the scruff of their necks.  Its breath was warm against his burning arms, adding to the heat.  And though its teeth were like razors, they didn't cut his skin.  Cecil watched the hallway fade to black behind his wolf.  He wasn't sure if he would live or not, but it seemed he wasn't going to die here.

Chapter Thirty-Nine

One
Salina was leaning over him.  Her hands were holding both of his, and she was biting her lip.  He sat up like a shovel that had its head stepped on, throwing her off and making her let go.  A horrific pain radiated from his core to his outermost being, seemingly ripping through his soul itself.  He wasn't aware of location anymore, and it didn't matter.  The first change was coming.
He grew in size and changed to his human form, screaming with blinding agony driving him.  He hadn't taken his clothes into account as usual and didn't alter them with his body, so his robe tore around his expanding torso and arms.  His pants did likewise, and his shoes popped off.  His hands curled up and released, tendons like coiled steel.  His nails changed from clear to black, got longer, and became sharp.  His jaw popped and elongated, as fur began sprouting up from his skin.  His teeth changed, getting longer and pointed.  A tail fell out the back of his pants, and his ears took canine form.
The pain receded, and he was left panting.  Then the panting stopped as did his need for the oxygen.  He smiled with razor canines hanging from his gums and glowed for a moment in his new energy.
Two
In the next moment Salina had her arms wrapped around him, lifting him off the ground and not holding back.  She put him down, and he did the same to her, whirling her around in the firelight.
"I didn't know if you would pull through." She said and wiped a tear from her cheek.
He held her close. "Thank you, Salina.  For everything."
A few minutes passed, and he got a feel for his new body.  She took the sword he'd left her, walked up to him, steadied herself, pulled back and let loose a mighty swing.  The sword hit him with a thunder, and he was launched into the air.  He sailed from the cave before landing and tumbling down the incline a few hundred yards to a flat spot.  He stood, dusted himself, and there she was in front of him.
"That's what I wanted to do in Garmond." She said with a wry grin.  It was dark, but his vision was impossibly sharp.
He looked further down the slope.  There were no more flats for quite a distance. "I think I'll fall right off the mountain if you do that again, and besides-" He tackled her.
He mended his clothes and reverted to his human self.  He was going to change again to novian, but Salina convinced him to stay as he was for a little while longer.  When their gear was in hand, they started searching again.  He felt utterly explosive.  It seemed like a lifetime ago when he'd been like this, if ever.

Three
On the northern continent, inside his manor, Malachi picked himself off the ground.  The creature that had attacked him disappeared along with his summoned guest.  It hadn't broken his skin because of a ward he'd placed over himself, but it had both startled him and had an upper hand in the fight.  He looked at his hall.  There were vases shattered on the floor, paintings knocked off their mounts, and some impressive claw marks in the carpet.
If the corridor had just been a bit narrower, the wolf wouldn't have fit.  He sighed.  Nothing to do about it, and he sensed that wherever Cecil was now, he was more than recovered.  So, there went that stratagem.  He repaired the torn sleeve the same way his guest just had done and went down the passage.
Unlike what Cecil had seen, there were plenty of exit routes.  He had made sure to veil these from the human, in case the passive spell did succeed in summoning him.  Malachi banished the spell.  Cecil would wreak havoc, were he to return.  His composure returned, he laid his hands on the doors leading out to the rear veranda and pushed them open.
To his right and left were stationed guards.  The left one, not prepared to see the master, chirped nervously, "Sir!" A hastened salute, and his tone flattened. "Are you done reflecting?" The other remained silent.
"Yes." But this was not entirely true. "I had a chat with a guest I summoned.  Things became heated, and there is some cleanup down the hall.  Have someone take care of that.  I'm going to think about the conversation for a bit."
"Are you hurt, sir?  And also, were you able to dispatch the assailant?"
Malachi thought it was dully ignorant of the guard to be so quick to take his side, but loyalty was valuable. "I am fine.  But no, he was able to break my summons before I could overwhelm him.  For all I know, that may be for the best.  Now go.  I need some time."
They obeyed, and he stepped up to the railing.  There were monorail trains rumbling on a network of tracks above the streets.  On the streets themselves were a hustle and bustle hive of pedestrians and vehicles.  Daskin was one of the few places citizens were permitted to drive in the North.  The licenses were hard to obtain, but even so, many had them here.  Most of the buildings were crystal, many of which in the style of stained glass.  The "cheaper" structures were obsidian or marble.  The city was sturdy, enormous, and beautiful.  It was an hour after dusk, but the city was aglow with brilliant green light.  It bounced off the cobble streets and crystal walls like a warm mint sun.  He looked down on it from his vantage point, the highest point of the mesa upon which the city had been built.  From here, he could see beyond the city's walls and to the flatlands beyond.  Out there, there were copper mines and one of the world's six race tracks.  It would be a shame, he knew, if the war came here.  This city had withstood the harshest hells of each era.
He sighed and collected his thoughts.


Four
Cecil and Salina headed up an old tram road, one of seemingly a thousand.  He was buoyant and jogging, but she was setting the pace.
"Do you feel anything that might be a ward?" She asked.
"No.  I think it's been obscured." They were making great time compared to the days before.
Over the course of a few hours, they covered the ground of a week.  Shortly after dawn, they found their quarry.
A collapsed cave entrance with an old tin sign placed in front wasn't a shining beacon, but it had a pulse.
They started clearing the rubble from the cave mouth and unceremoniously tossed the debris down the mountainside.  There was a slight odor in the air he thought he recognized, but he couldn't place it.  A little ways in he found another sign, the text upon which was still legible, though seemingly misplaced.  He stopped his clearing and read it aloud.
"And when all the men gathered together in that place, they left the stone there, Dahzir's Brace.  Then Borthir and Enthir butted their heads, for they were dwarven newlyweds.  And came then many to see this joyous ceremony.  But it was not their intention to give away their master's secret, so they sealed the chamber without regret.  And the stones tumbled down upon their heads."
"Think that means anything?" Salina asked.
He shrugged.  He was about to toss the sign with the rocks, but then he grabbed up one of the bags and placed the tin inside.  Maybe it did mean something.
They went back to work.
Five
It took some time to clear what had fallen, and even after being able to see into the depths of the cave, it was impossible to say if this was the right place.
She asked again, "Feel anything?"
"Not magically, but yeah in my gut I do."
"Well, lead the way."
He did and conjured up a bright white orb of light to go out before him.  The cave definitely held a familiar scent, but its origin was lost to him.  It began descending sharply into the mountain, so they left their things behind by the entrance.  The walls and air seemed particularly cold, and there was a presence about the place as if it had been visited recently.
Down one particular slope he lost traction and slipped on ice.  Not yet readjusted to his strength, he overcompensated and kicked out with the rogue foot.  It struck a rock and sent his prone body lunging up into Salina's shins.  She tripped over him, and he missed the rock on the way back.  They both tumbled down the dark incline, his orb of light autonomously illuminating the path ahead of the Jack and Jill duo.
Before he fully registered what had happened, she was on her feet and moving into the recesses of the light.  She stopped and drew her sword, holding it before her.
"I thought I smelled something." She said.
"I did too." And then he saw it.  His light took on a greater brilliance, and the chamber they were in glistened a pallid violet.  Toward the far side was a sleeping dragon twice the size of a train cab.  Its scales were green upon its back, golden down its belly and shone finer than polished brass.  It didn't seem to be breathing, but it also didn't seem dead.  He guessed it could be frozen or in some stasis, but there was an odd darkness to it.  Normally he could probe a living thing, but it was as if it did not even exist.
She walked along one side and he the other, meeting up at the inanimate occupant.
"This is it." He whispered, uncertain if that mattered. "This isn't the ward I was expecting, but this dragon has the Siphon."
"Your gut again?"
"No, I'm positive now.  I'm trying to get a read on it.  Just the smallest bit of info would do, like how old it is or what gender, but it's not 'here.'" He turned to her. "You've always been under my radar, but if I concentrate, I can identify your unique presence.  Same with the rest of The Order and their magical resilience.  This," He looked at the dragon's head, a massive construct of scale and flesh, "well, it might as well be an illusion.  Actually I could 'feel' an illusion.  It's like trying to sense without my powers."
"Is it alive?" She reached for its nose, but as she did there was a great static discharge.  After a deafening crackle and a light brighter than Cecil's, she was thrown back by a shockwave.  The pulse knocked him back as well and broke his balance.
The dragon awoke.

Chapter Forty

One
It rose and shook like a dog repelling water from its coat.  A dust fell into the air, and it whisked it away with a gale-force flapping of its wings.  Its jaw opened, displaying a set of fine whites, and popped in a yawn.  It arched its back and stretched.  Once it had fully adjusted itself to the waking world, the dragon opened its cavernous maw again and let out a low and steady roar.
Great lights filled the chamber, and the violet aura became stronger, drowning out Cecil's own source of luminescence.
"Why have you woken me?" The dragon asked.  Its voice was smooth and melodic, sounding the most delicate bit male. "No, do not give me your reasons.  I've been asleep long enough that anything you answer with will be irrelevant." The dragon's head swayed from Cecil to Salina and back.  Its flecked orange eyes were sharp, wise, and tired. "Please, leave me to rest again."
Salina had her hands on her hips, unimpressed. "We're looking for an object called the Dark Siphon.  We believe it's here, and we have need of it."
The dragon sighed, and light tendrils of smoke drifted from its nostrils. "That is the only reason you could've given that would cause me alarm . . . and it's the one I expected.  What you want is here, but I cannot give it to you, for my master tasked me with its protection."
"Your master has passed on." Cecil replied. "Dahzir is dead.  Are you bound by the unliving?"
"I know he is dead.  You are the one that killed him, and you also killed my brood sister along with her hatchlings." The dragon watched Cecil's expressions. "But, I also know why you did these things.  Defense for you and your friends.  I cannot be upset with you for that.  As for the item you seek, my master's absence does not negate my duty, for he planted it in my body before I retired to this den.  To take it would require you to kill me."
"Well . . ." Salina held up her blade.
"Hold on." Cecil said. "Dragon, what is your name?"
"Oh?" The dragon tilted its head up. "Shall we become friends?  I am Pytmos, Son of Aria and Malthios.  Master Dahzir saved me from poachers when I was but a scaling, and I served as his hunting scout many years thereafter.  In return for my loyalty, he blessed me with clairvoyance and his artifact.  There, I have given you my name, and I've told you who I am.  I know your names and your deeds, and I fear for my task, as I know I cannot merely silence your spells and scorch you as I could the mortals."
"You're far nicer than your master." Salina remarked.
Pytmos inhaled deeply, drafting the air of the cave. "Dahzir was once a kind and gentle man.  He never hunted animals that had 'more life to live.' I saw him on many occasions release creatures he deemed too young and vibrant to be cut down.  When he did take life, he always made sure there was no pain involved whatsoever, and he would send soothing magicks to beasts as they passed.  His men loved him, and he was a loyal and devout family man, though he never had children of his own.  Dahzir always put others before himself, and he adored his wife."
Cecil and Salina shared a glance at one another.
"I slept through these last few hundred years, but I saw in my dreams what became of my master.  It was known that Trothos was draining the life of his nephew to secure his place as king.  What people don't know, because it happened too slowly for them to see, is that what Trothos really did was poison Dahzir's soul.  After Yena died, a suspicious death if ever there was one, my master became especially susceptible to his uncle's influence.  Oh, he held onto his free will but at the cost of his morals.  I wanted to leave this place and lend him aid, had reached out to his soul with mine, but he always reminded me to stay.  I believe he wanted me to avoid him because of what he was becoming.  He saw where he was going.  The Dahzir you knew is not the one I knew, and I would honor his last request to me."
Salina was a statue.  It seemed she didn't even breathe until she needed to speak, which, Cecil reminded himself, was very possible.  She commented, "You're awfully talkative.  The few dragons I've seen just growl like animals."
"We were once an intelligent race, yes.  But we were scattered to the winds and made primitive so that we could be hunted as beasts without remorse.  That happened after man discovered magic and grew strong enough to fight against us, but that is an old fairytale I don't want to tell again.  Not now.  What will you do?  Will you go on without my master's relic, or will you slay me in order to rip it from my lifeless carcass?  I will fight, but I know what I'd be matched against.  What is it then, can you leave without the treasure you seek?"
Cecil dispelled his light.  No more need for it. "You said you could silence the spells of those who'd come here to take the Siphon.  Does that mean you can use it?"
Pytmos nodded, and it was an odd gesture.  His long reptilian neck did not bob up and down but rather undulated like the coil of a snake.
"Would you come to our aid then?  It doesn't matter who has it, so long as it can be used against Malachi."
"Malachi is a good man even to this day.  He was one of my master's closest friends."
Cecil and Salina shared another glance.
"If I agree to help you, will you promise to do no harm to him?  I will continue guarding this relic within me, and I would like very much to preserve my master's friend.  He was my friend as well."
Salina sheathed her sword and threw her hands out in a "whatever" manner. "This isn't what I expected to find here," She said, "but the results are the same, I guess.  I don't see why we couldn't spare him, especially if he's as fine as you claim.  Then you'll help us?"
Cecil guardedly thought about his encounter with the governor and added nothing to the conversation.
"Yes, I will help you come to peaceable terms.  Thank you for being reasonable."
Salina walked over to Cecil and gave him a clap on the shoulder. "Bet this wasn't what you expected either, but to be honest," She looked at Pytmos, "I was expecting a lot more hassle any which way things went.  This was nice."
Pytmos's tone was tired, relaxed by the mood. "I only leave this cave a few times a year, and the next time was approaching soon.  If you want my help, I'll need to fly up to Atticus.  Right now, I don't have the energy to do that.  I need a few days to eat and gather my strength."
A sense of dread ran over Cecil like a bucket of ice water.  He tried shifting back to novian but could not. "Did you . . . ?" He looked up at the dragon.
The reply, "I did not drain your powers."
Salina concealed a smile.  She knew this would happen.
Two
Mara and Gabe, having been sent to Ceres (a city occupying a tropical island) to investigate Leviathan interest in the war, were on the cusp of finding the truth.  It was hard enough to share information, when utter secrecy and discretion was needed, and their orders had them staying at separate locations with as little communication between.  Even so, Scylla's headquarters were in the business district.  It was believed that the executives of the company would be chummy with military commanders and know sensitive subjects more intimately.  Their mission was, so far, quite successful.  The company was profiting from deals with the new Grand General, a dwarf named Banix Frendt.  In exchange for cushy amounts of lucre, Scylla was ensuring a swift victory for the Leviathans in their "cleanup" of what remained of The Order and Malachi's army.  That was the belief, and they were here to prove it one way or the other, and they were close.
Mara was meeting with a contact from within Scylla who promised to get them IDs and upper level access cards.  Gabe was standing guard outside the coffee shop where the exchange would take place.  For insurance measures, they hadn't informed the contact about both of them being present.  Though, it was a public place, neither of them had felt at ease anywhere in the city.  It was as if they were being watched, a normal sensation when doing covert operations.  That feeling was stronger here than anywhere else.
The square in front of the shop was bustling with people, and Gabe was just one of the tourists sitting on the outside patio and enjoying a cup of joe.  It was midafternoon, a terrible time to make a scene and an impossible one to get away with a crime.  The shop itself was only half full, enough to be busy but not so much that it was hectic.  The walls, floors, and counters were varying hues of brown, giving the idea that they were inside a giant coffee bean, and the aroma would've only added to that illusion.
The meeting went on behind him.  He had wanted to be sitting somewhere where he'd be facing the two, but it wouldn't be prudent.  He was no actor, and keeping his expressions aloof would've been a challenging task.  So he sat with his back turned, taking only cursory glances over his shoulder to check on Mara.  Everything seemed to be going smoothly, and it was strange to think of what work he was actually doing.  If no one tried to pull anything shady, Gabe's job was to sit here and drink.  It was hard to believe you could get paid for something like that, until you took into account that you and/or your soon-to-be spouse might be dead in a few minutes, maybe five . . . maybe right now.  When you thought of it that way, you felt very much slighted on payday.
On one of his random glances, he noticed things had gone quite awry.  And unfortunately for Gabe, his stunned look gave him away.
Three
Hodge was returning to the Sentinel after repelling Malachi's invasion.  He sighed, slowly walking from ruin into unscathed territory.  Nelrene was gathering the wounded with Arthur and Marianna, and Hodge found himself in need of his wife.  To an extent Arthur and Marianna had understood what he was, but it always seemed to him that only Nelrene truly had a grasp on it.  He was the oldest of the group.  The people looked at him for guidance, but his military experience was rudimentary at best.  After all, at his core he was but a shepherd.
Time went on without his notice, and he stood in the war room with his advisors, the real leaders of this coupe.  It had been easier when Siegfried had been his eye-in-the-sky, but these days his old companion had gone silent.  It was the final breaking string which had tied him to Earth, and getting things turned upright again were proving difficult.
The war room was simple, a round, soundproof, bug proof amphitheater with a twelve seated ebony table at the center and two exits, one on the north side, the other on the east.  He was being told that Malachi's forces retreated under unusual circumstances, that his forces could've pushed farther and harder than they had.  A trap was almost guaranteed.
That was when a figure took shape by the north door and approached the council.  A lithe elven man with fine green robes and silver hair.  He bowed as the group silenced to the uninvited guest. "I am sorry." He said.
"Malachi," Surprised but less so than Hodge thought he ought to be, which was the real surprise, "you bastard." He drew a sidearm.
"You know I'm not really here." The elf replied.
Hodge shrugged and pulled the trigger.  One perfectly directed bullet left the end of the pistol's barrel and passed through Malachi's chest where his heart would be.  As expected, there was no resistance, because this was only a projection. "I always choose the side of caution.  What are you here for?"
The council remained mute, and it was as if the room was only inhabited by the two leaders. "Well," Malachi began, "I've been giving this whole campaign a great deal of thought.  There's so much for both our sides to lose, and what is there to gain?  You want to go home, or at least have the hope of someday being able to, and I want to clean up the oppressive mess left behind by Dahzir."
Hodge nodded.
"At first I thought I could convince your forces to side with me, but I see you and yours are noble, and the people respect you.  I respect you.  But this causes a problem.  We're pitted against each other while most of the Leviathans sit back and watch.  Surely you're suspect as well."
"It seems everyone on this planet has been waiting for their chance to rise.  Yes, I am aware of . . . certain schemes."
"I'd rather we not walk headlong into them.  I'm asking for a peace council to be arranged.  I hope my personal appearance will convince you of my sincerity."
An advisor whispered something into Hodge's ear.  The werewolf replied, "I should hope this isn't a plot."
"If you agree to the council, I would like it if The Order chose the meeting place.  So, you see I will be 'in your hands' as it were.  There will be complete transparency."
"It sounds reasonable.  I'll relay the message to the other werewolves.  You won't be fickle and back out, will you?"
"I lean not on my own understanding.  Were it up to me, I would be bullheaded and rush us all into destruction."
Hodge cocked his head.  Malachi had quoted something he could not possibly know of.
"I do have one request."
"And that is?"
"I want Cecil Fauden in attendance."
"You son of a bitch!" Hodge was very close to growling, and his hairs were standing on end.  In his youth such a rage would've sent him into a transformation, but he held it back. "That's sick."
"Oh, I didn't mean to set you off, honestly.  Don't be hasty now to call off our gathering, but I think there's someone you need to speak with first.  The colonel you've had under scrutiny, a mage of exceptional potential."
Hodge's hair settled, and curiosity replaced his anger. "Must you be so cryptic about it?  What about Xavier Pontius?"
"I know his secret, but I'm not a gossip.  He should be the one to tell you."
Hodge sighed.  There was a burning joy welling up in his guts.  He would say it was something akin to finding out a long missed friend was safe.  He composed himself, but his beaming smile fought against his overriding expression of indifference. "I'll tie things up on my end, and we'll get back to you."

Chapter Forty-One

One
Cecil and Salina were riding the train back to port.  Pytmos said he would meet them when he was needed.  They wanted to wait and make sure he'd be fit for travel, but with the recent attack their return necessitated urgency.  Urgent or not, Cecil was struggling to keep his nerves in check.  He was stuck in his human form, and no matter how hard he tried, he couldn't even change the color of his hair.
He knew Salina was happy about his plight, but he wasn't mad at her.  He'd been getting advice for a long time to just reveal himself, and now he was, unwilling or not.  She was glad to have him back, but he had his suspicions that the others wouldn't be so welcoming.
The train rolled along, unimpeded by the war, the werewolves' problems, or the starving children making paper headlines.  It simply continued on.
On the ship back to Atticus, Salina got a call from Hodge.  The situation had calmed down, but he needed to see her and Xavier as soon as possible.  Unlike his last trip, the two had clearance to stay on deck all night.  Their military paperwork was hardly even necessary, as their appearance gave them away.
He was waking up to the world it seemed.  Thinking about where he was going and whom he was going with sparked a quaint little idea.  So, he asked Salina if he could borrow one of her plain pencils and a sheet of paper.  If he was going to be awake, he was going to be alive and take advantage of it.
"Going to sketch me something?" She asked.
He smiled timidly and looked out over the deck to the vast sea. "I couldn't sketch one drop of water if I tried."
Two
He began writing, not shocked to see his handwriting was still close to illegible.  He could fix that, but first he needed the words put down.  Cecil spent longer working on it than he'd thought he would, not for lack of creativity, but simply because making it was such a joy.  When he finished and magicked the letters to be readable, he wasn't sure it had any value.
Oh, to my werewolf queen
My companion, my best friend
On me might you forever lean
Your loyal king till all things end

Your golden locks, like a shining sun
Your emerald eyes, piercing my soul
Without you I would be undone
But with I am made whole

This cold thing inside you melt
Into this dark world you bring light
Shining radiance from your silver pelt
My beacon in blackest night

I lose myself in your warm embrace
All these woes by you are shed
Whenever I see your lovely face
Without you I'd be dead

I'd stay with you ten thousand years
But that would only be a fleeting start
When with you who calms all my fears
This meager thing I give to you, my heart

My werewolf queen, my best friend
Stay with me till all things end
Your teeth and claws could've torn me apart
But willing I gave you my throat and now my heart
He looked it over.  Salina was pawing at him to see, but he shook his head, crumpled the paper, and tossed it to the waves.
"What was it?" She asked.
"Nothing." He couldn't look at her. "Needs work.  I'll try again another time."
Three
They got off in Ares and boarded a train bound for Fraushein.  Cecil was beginning, only just, to become accustomed to railway travel.  On Earth, he had never ridden a subway and only encountered trains when his bus stopped at crossings.  There was something novel about going long distances like this and getting to really view the surroundings in a way you couldn't when dealing with traffic.  Of course, the company was nice as well.
He looked out the window at the flowing waves of green and mountains set so far away.  He wasn't even thinking about this upcoming reunion, where everything would be laid bare.  It didn't seem important anymore.  What was more pressing was the idea that somewhere in that great blue above him, some unfathomable distance away was his home. Cecil looked up at the sky, even his new eyes not capable of piercing the atmosphere, and wondered.
Cecil made a conscious effort to remain conscious.  Staying awake was no problem, but he didn't want to fall into his old trappings.  Things were finally moving forward in his life, and he intended to keep the momentum.  That meant he would need to be alert.  He looked over at Salina sitting by the window with her sketchpad.  He would have to thank her.
The train made a few stops, but Fraushein came as fast as ever.  He was a bit surprised transport hadn't been suspended because of the battle and took it as a good sign.  They got off in the station, a locale which had been unaffected by the war.  Its silver gilded clock still stood, and its patrons still shuffled about their busy lives.
The werewolves made their way through the crowds and onto the packed street.  The face of the crowd, that familiar pattern of pedestrians, saw them and seemed to bubble about them.  It was as if the two were surrounded by an invisible force, and Cecil wondered if he wasn't putting up a ward unintentionally.
Four
Foot traffic slowed as they approached the Sentinel and left the congestion stacked in the streets leading to the hospital.  They passed through the gate and entered at the main door.  The inside had hardly changed since Cecil had come here a few years ago.  The only difference was the staff and furnishings, and he supposed that might only be because the originals were destroyed.  A secretary sat at the same desk he'd seen those years ago, and she stood to salute Salina.
They walked by and took a door on the left.  If he remembered correctly, this had once lead to the study and master dining room.  He was right, though both rooms had been repurposed.  The study was now a waiting room, but it was empty now.  The dining room was lined with squat tables covered in monitors, an armed technician sitting at each and two guards at every door.  The guards and techs rose in a salute, and Salina waved them at ease.
At the far end, where the dining room had once gone into a library, a soldier in full crystal armor watched the door, a blast model with a keycard reader.  As Salina approached, he tightened his grip on his rifle and cast a warding about himself.  "The inner sanctum requires-"
"Yeah, I've got it." Salina cut in.
Before she could produce her proper identification, the door unlocked from the opposite side and a tall man with short wheat hair appeared in the threshold.  "Stand down, Captain.  They have clearance."
A clean and obedient "Yes, sir." Sounded from behind the man's helmet.
Five
It was still a library with shelves of books reaching up to the high ceiling and stretching out in all directions.  A little ways ahead was a high standing table with three more humans around it.  Cecil became fully aware of his garb, his wizard robes and his blade, and he felt very much out of place.  The books suited him, but his peers, more familiar than the alien life of this world, felt anything but.
He and Salina stood opposite the other three humans, while the man who led them in joined them.  Not standing on ceremony, these humans were wearing what looked like nothing more than street clothes.
"Well," the man said, "Xavier Pontius, everyone."
No one said a word.  It was as if the span of a year was passing in this moment.
A bit shamefully Cecil responded, "Hodge," he nodded to each as he called their names, "Nelrene, Arthur, Marianna."
Hodge's shoulders pushed forward, his head rolled about, and he turned.  His black fur rose over his wheat hair, his snout became long and ebony, his arms coated, and razor claws replaced his nails.  He took a whiff of the air and raised a brow to Cecil, a bizarre gesture with his wolfish features.
Cecil took the queue and did his own turning.  His fur was darker than Hodge's but also possessed fiery blue patterns about his arms and down the middle of his nose and head.  Embarrassingly, he forgot about the tail and had to magically alter the back of his garment to let it through.
Like dominoes the other werewolves transformed, starting with Salina.  Then there they were, a pack once again.
Six
A long silence haunted the room, while Hodge stared Cecil down.  Everyone and everything was as a stone.  They didn't even breathe.  It was at this point Cecil was certain he'd be in for an unforgettable chastising, but he supposed the worst of it was just waiting for it to happen.  Almost humorously he marveled at the fact that Arthur had kept his silence.  It was amusing but also unsettling.
He could hear their heartbeats, Cecil realized.  But they weren't discordant like random piston firings.  No, they were in unison.  The werewolves shared one heartbeat, one pulse.  Suddenly his worries were unimportant as he listened to the rhythmic hum of their blood pumping.  A warm sensation filled him, one of welcoming and place.  The final piece was in place for him.  He knew he belonged.
As if seeing this, Hodge broke the silence and stepped up to the youngest lycanthrope.  He made a wolfish smile and placed a clawed hand on Cecil's shoulder.  Salina joined in.  Then Nelrene, Marianna, and Arthur.  They began howling.  At some point, though he'd never be certain exactly when, Cecil began to howl with them.
After welcoming the return of their lost member, the werewolves shifted back to human.  Hodge clapped Cecil on the back and made a very normal looking smile. "We've got time, and we're all very happy that you're alive.  We're also curious.  How are you alive?"
Cecil agreed, and over the next few hours he explained how he managed to come back.  Hodge and the others went over with him what had happened since they'd formed the volunteer army and where things were headed.
"We don't trust the Leviathans, and neither does Malachi." Hodge explained.
Nelrene, "It's been obvious for a while now that they mean to clean up the leftovers of this war and seize power for themselves."
Arthur, "Then we're back at square one again, and no one's game for that."
Hodge again, "Malachi said he's spoken with you.  He also said he was given some advice, specifically concerning you, Cecil.  That after conversing with you he felt it.  Apparently he is now filled with conviction to do business with us in an amicable fashion."
Arthur rolled his eyes and whispered something under his breath.  Marianna jabbed him sharply with an elbow.
Hodge went on, "We're going to set up a peace council.  Hopefully we can keep this a secret from the Leviathans, though they probably already know.  I suspect things will go well for both sides, so if we could at least trick them into believing they do not, we could have a leg up.  Cecil, I have a job for you."
Cecil raised his brows.
"I want you to set up the council.  The date and location.  If you talk to Malachi through telepathy, I doubt it could be bugged.  If we could just stay one step ahead of our enemies."
Cecil nodded. "I think I know a good place.  I'll make contact as soon as I can."
"Thank you." Hodge bent over the table with one hand under it. "You can come in now."
One of the library doors swung open, and a small cadre of intellectuals walked in.  Among them was an elven man and gray wolf woman.  Cecil recognized them immediately, and they recognized him.
Partheus broke into a brisk jog, his sprinting days behind him.  He came up to Cecil, gaunt but in the elf style.  His tight skinned face made a myriad of confused and delighted expressions as he attempted to spit out words.  He looked Cecil up and down, seeing the robes and the sword at his hip. He shook his head. "Well," he began after finally finding a foothold of words, "you fooled me.  I never would've figured it out." Perplexed again. "Is that really you?"
"Hello, Partheus."
The elf shook his head again. "You kids are giving this old man too much excitement."
Arthur stepped in, "Don't get old just yet.  There's a lot left to be done."
Seven
After catching up with his old friends, Cecil went off by himself to an unoccupied corner of the library and began to meditate.  He had never tried to send his presence such a distance before and without knowing just where Malachi was, he found himself in a drifting state of consciousness, sifting through vast stretches of land in search of strong magical outputs.  Some time went by, a long while by his reckoning.  In reality it was quite short.
Then he felt the familiar presence and sent out a thought, just the name, Malachi.
A few moments passed, and there was a response, Cecil.
I've been asked to choose the council's meeting place.
And where would you choose?
Cecil sent out the thought.
Oh, that IS fitting.  When?
Cecil sent the image of a calendar with a marked date.
I will be there with my most trusted men.
We want to keep this hidden from the Leviathans.
Yes.  My lips are sealed.  I look forward to seeing you there, brother.
Malachi broke the transmission, and Cecil returned to himself.

Chapter Forty-Two

One
The weeks between then and the council passed in haste, despite Cecil's extra eight hours of day he'd gained.  Just minutes ago he had boarded the train in Fraushein, yet somehow Hurlinge was just a couple moments down the track.
He was wearing his gilded mage attire and his sword.  The other werewolves wore simple but austere military uniforms, and Salina had his old sword resting on its own booth.  The cab was theirs and theirs alone, and measure had been taken to make sure that no one was listening in.  Even so, the group remained hush.  Things had been quiet since the withdrawal of Malachi's army, and without a doubt the Leviathans were becoming suspicious.
Cecil casted a spell to surround himself and the werewolves as they readied to disembark.  He couldn't change either his form or theirs, but he could place a powerful illusion over them.  To the citizens and all but the most powerful of wizards, they would be nothing more than a novian family, apparently returning from a trip.  He would keep the spell up until the city lights were behind them.  Then it wouldn't matter what form they appeared as.
They pushed through the teeming station and set their course for the dark beyond, which was the forest.  Taking familiar roads, ones still lined with silver lamps, they would return to where Destursha's trials had begun.  The Dark Forest had changed since last he'd been there, and it didn't surprise him.  The woods, a veritable sponge of impulse and magic, would be scarred by the deeds of the old king.  Despite the good intentions of the previous inhabitants, it would be seeking vengeance upon anything it could lash out at.  Perhaps that was why it attracted the mutant hordes, those dregs of experiments better left abandoned, and the primordial creatures that spurred superstition.  Such monsters would be at home in the festering gash of the forest's heart, leeching it and feeding from its anger.  All the while hardening whatever sentience the place had and turning it into malice.
Two
He and Salina lagged behind as the werewolves left the highway and entered into the forest proper.  He summoned a sprite of light, but the forest's darkness was material and choked the great beacon to naught more than a strong lantern.  They could all feel the presence of monsters, but the foul things were nowhere within the reach of the five senses.
He turned to Salina, admiring the way she carried his old sword.  Every day he became more aware of how she treasured him.  He didn't understand it, and he didn't acknowledge it.  Cecil struggled often with his expressions for her.  What he couldn't articulate with his mouth, he seemed quite capable of doing with a pen, but he refused to share.  It was out of line.
They continued the trek.
The old walls were unharmed by the couple years which had passed.  Other than a bit of overgrowth, things seemed unchanged.  The great stone walls rose up into the dark canopy from which there was no light of moon, only thick black.  The gate was open, likely rusted that way permanently.  It was impossible to see into the ruins of Chrissenia, but scents wafted through the air like flitting calling cards.  Malachi was here, but there was something else here also.
"Pytmos." Salina said.
They entered Chrissenia.
Three
Malachi stood in what was the old town center, talking to the dragon like to a friend.  He turned to the werewolves and sent them a half bow. "Sorry we're early." He said. "The host should be the first, but we made better time than we had thought." He turned again to Pytmos. "I'm glad this is how we get to meet."
"Yes." Hodge replied, "Come, we'll make ourselves comfortable."
They went as a group, Malachi, his men, and the werewolves.  The coffee shop seemed completely unaffected by time, despite its obvious lack of patronage.  Pytmos, too large to fit through the door, waited outside while the rest pulled chairs together around a banquet table.  To all parties' delight, the creatures which lived there did not make a den out of the shop.
When everyone was situated, Cecil conjured up a pitcher made of ice at the center of the table and filled with purified water from the air.  He also made several cups with which to drink and left them on Malachi's side of the table.
Malachi nodded his appreciation and spoke first, "I'd like to do this with as little political jargon and rigmarole as possible.  I've never been a bureaucratic type, and I don't imagine any of you are either.  If you were, you would've wanted to meet somewhere palatial with no sentimental value and with servants and press gawking about.  So, you speak first.  What do you want from all this?"
Hodge replied. "We want two things.  When we first learned of this world, we were under the impression that it was host to demons that threatened our world.  We thought legions of hell would spill onto Earth from Destursha.  When the rift between opened, we were the ones taken here, and we have no way back.  I still believe there are demons afoot, Malachi.  We would like very much to quell them.  The second thing we desire is to return home.  It might take a hundred mortal lifetimes, but we will work for it."
