Arthur, more dignified than ever, took his turn, "And what is it you desire?"
"I want to atone for what I've done and what my compatriots have done." The elf replied, "A long while ago, I considered myself a god.  I was an adept mage, and I had a unique gift for sending out my presence.  I preyed upon the weak minded and convinced them I was a deity.  It fed my ego, and I can't even say I knew the faces of my worshippers.  They were far away, if they existed at all.  I think most of it was born from my own arrogance.  My eyes were finally opened to myself and what my allies were doing.  But, we had all become complacent under the old king, and I could not stand against him alone.  So I bided my time and waited for help to come.  You, whom I called enemies, are the help.  Again I was caught by my own trappings and did not see this.  I want a stretch of peace and happiness for the people.  I know well it can't last.  But Destursha has been unhappy for a long time, and I would see light return if only for just an hour.  To do this, the greed must be excised from authority.  Not even I can fight the Leviathans by myself, and I fear you all lack the know-how."
A few moments passed between the werewolves. "Well," Hodge led, "should we draw up papers, make a formal alliance?  We help you become the acting monarch, you don't try anything shady, and we work on the space program?"
Malachi shook his head. "Later there can be papers.  If we write anything, the Leviathans will be a step ahead.  Frendt is a resourceful man, but I don't think he knows about this meeting.  I'd like it to stay that way."
"Do we seal this with a handshake, a little spit?" Arthur asked.
Malachi almost chuckled but held it in. "More or less, yes."
The dragon poked his head in through the door. "No," he said, "there are bigger problems now."
The werewolves and Malachi turned to the dragon. "Pyt?" Malachi almost hummed it.
He looked to the werewolves, focusing on Hodge. "The Phoenix is plummeting, and soon we'll have to greet your people."
Arthur shrugged. "Good lord, do you have to be so cryptic?"
Marianna gave him an elbow.
"Let's get back to the city." Pytmos said, "So that you can all see the sky."
"How urgent is it?" Malachi seemed at ease, implacable.
"It's too late already." Again he looked at the werewolves, "They crept up so well, like shadows inside darkness." Pytmos pulled his head from the door, and the sound of his quadruped footsteps followed.
Malachi was already on his feet. "You see, a bureaucrat would throw a hissy fit about something like this." His men followed fast behind him and the werewolves followed suit.
Four
When they came to the first clearing, they found Pytmos with his neck craned upward, gazing at a great light in the night sky.  It was the comet that had been coming, but it was close to the planet's surface, suspended on the tip of space like a moon.  Now it was clear to see its shape, or the shape it was taking.  It had indeed been a comet to the astronomers, but bright lights of fire flashed from it, and its rocky skin fled away.  It was enormous, shaped like a perfect ring, and its silver color shone from sunlight on the planet's other side.
A few minutes went by, and a blinding light came from its center.  When the night sun receded, a multitude of small silver points of light were in front of it.
Pytmos lowered his head and looked at Cecil. "Can you reach up there and feel them?"
Cecil didn't reply, but he acted.  He sent his mind's eye up into the farthest reaches of the atmosphere, where Destursha's magical influence was weakest.  He couldn't interact with what was here because of the distance, but he could see them inside and out.  These were ships.
"They're human." He said at last.
"What?" Arthur asked.
"You heard me.  Those are human ships up there, and there are people in them."
"Do you know why they're here?" Malachi asked, trying to raise his own mind.
"I don't." Hodge was stunned. "They aren't here for us."
Malachi gave a signal to his men. "I think this would be a good time to part ways for now.  The Leviathans, everyone, will be distracted by this.  We can pretend it never happened.  I'll need to return to Daskin and reassure my people.  I suggest you all do the same.  Pytmos, will you come with me?"
The dragon nodded.
"We'll sort this out later." Malachi and his men rushed into the forest.  The werewolves made for the road.  There wouldn't be time to take a train.  They would need to run as a pack.
Five
Those were the longest miles of Cecil's life.  The running was easy, but the seeing was hard.  Every chance he had, he looked up and saw those spacecrafts descend lower to the surface.  They were headed for Fraushein, and he supposed that only made sense.  They'd be headed for the flagship.  Whatever they wanted, it wouldn't do to talk to anyone but the top, if talking was even what they intended to do.
As they approached the capitol, there was a bright flare over the Sentinel.  The Phoenix, crystal pride of the Leviathan Corps, was sending out warning shots to the approaching ships.  A comparably sized ship sent a shot back, but it was no warning.  A beam of blinding brilliance lit the sky brighter than the day, striking the Phoenix with such force that a thunder rolled across the earth.  Cecil could hear the windows in the capitol shattering to this noise.  The Phoenix, unable to break apart, simply lost altitude like a newspaper coming down on a fly.  It sunk over the horizon and was followed by another tremor, and a great cloud of dust rose up.  There was no doubt that even though the ship remained intact, its crew had been jarred to death.
The Sentinel itself was unharmed, but power to the city was out.  The werewolves waited outside by the gate where some time ago, protestors had been struck down.  A smaller ship, sleek and silvered as a mirror, came down into the clearing before them.  There were only a few people standing in the streets to watch, and it was as if the whole of the city might've been the werewolves' own private residence.
It landed before them without ceremony.  It was shaped almost like a bullet with a hatch on the side.  It gave off the faint scent of ozone, and whatever windows it may have had were not visible.  The hatch opened not unlike that of an airplane, and two figures stepped out clad in armor that seemed a part of their very structures.
The second looked at the first, and after a moment the first took off its helmet.  There was a man behind that helm.  He inhaled for a second, nodded, and placed his helmet on his hip, where it stayed without any noticeable reason.  He had a satchel on his left thigh and reached into it, pulling out what looked like a black headband.  He placed it carefully around his head and over his mouth.
"Do you understand me?" He asked the werewolves.
"Yes." Hodge replied.
"Note." He said to the other figure. "Their language has not changed since previous investigations and still seems similar to Terran.  Also note: they seem to have evolved, at least one grouping, in height and weight."
The first figure held up a small metal rod and waved it through the air before saying something to the first.
The first replied, "Oh, really?  Well, that is interesting.  Tell me!" To the werewolves, "What does the name 'Earth' mean to you?"
"It is the world we are from." Hodge said, "We six lived there."
"Is he pulling an illusion?" The man asked the other figure and seemed jubilant to see a shake of the head for a reply. "Ancient history, that.  Do you suppose you know what year it is?"
"Two thousand twelve or thirteen."
"This place is a real phenomenon.  I underestimated the value here." To the first figure, "You have the data you need?" There was a nod, and they both made back for the ship.
"Wait!" Hodge shouted.
They did not.
Shortly after returning to their ship, another disembarked and waved the vessel away.  The figure approached them and spoke. "Is this the head of this region's government?"
Hodge nodded and added. "We're in a bit of a transition at the moment, but we are the heads.  Speak with us."  The rest remained behind him, solemn and erect.
"Turmoil?  Good.  That should make things easier for us."

Over the Stars

There was a dead calm in the street, the ships above silent and motionless.  The man stood across the way from the werewolves, black cloth on his face and arms crossed smugly.  It was hard to be sure in the dark, but Cecil thought he could see the creasing of a smile under that mask.
Arthur stepped up.  In his uniform he seemed austere, resolute, implacable.  His footsteps sounded emphatically, and his expression was grim.  His nostrils flared, and his accent, usually tinged with sarcastic overtones, breached the silence in serious sternness. "Barnabas." The shadows on his face grew long with his displeased expression. "You came from Earth?"
The man bridged the gap between himself and the rest.  His armor was immaculate, a shining shell of mirrored alloys, and it bent to his will and motions without any noticeable restriction.  He stood before them like a mentor standing before wayward pupils, hands on his hips and head cocked.
He adjusted the mask. "Sorry to keep this on.  Haven't spoken your tongue in a good while.  To answer your question, yes." He raised a hand to the sky and made a short sweeping motion. "But, I haven't been there in a very long time."
Hodge opened his mouth to speak, but Barnabas cut him off.
"No," he said, "not here.  Let's talk more in my ship."
A gleaming object the length of a train cab descended.  It had the shape of an oblong egg with sharp points at the poles.  When it landed Barnabas entered, and the others followed.
Inside was a sterile white environment.  Nothing about it seemed comforting, the way one might personalize a car or a room.  There were few things within, none of which appeared to be furnishing, only a few consoles and a monitor.  The interfaces of which did not appear to exist.
The only recognizable object was a round white table, although it was not accompanied by chairs.
"Come." Barnabas said, "Take a place."
They did.
"I'm aware of what's going on here." He said to them. "It's cute, really.  I've seen it before.  Can't say it was ever this amusing, but still." He grinned under the veil. "I'm the head of Colony, a Kingdom company that finds suitable planets to inhabit and gather resources from."
Cecil fixed his eyes on Barnabas, one of the few things not blindingly white. "How long have you . . . what year is it?"
"I've been colonizing planets for the better part of the last thousand years.  Keeps me busy.  Gives me time to pursue."
Cecil turned his eyes down.  He had a plethora of questions lining up, but it had not been just a few years.  A great stretch of time had somehow passed.  It meant that if he did get back home, even at this very moment, his family would be gone.  They had already been gone.
"And what are you after?" Hodge asked.  The way he spoke, the way he stood.  It was as if he was perfectly comfortable in the floating bullet.
"Siegfried, of course.  I never did believe that story of yours.  I can hardly believe that I remember it.  I'm quite sure he was never human and that your explanation was a cover-up.  Wherever he is, I intend to find him."
Hodge did not respond at first, only bit his lip.  He was cornered. "You're right about him."
The others turned to him, all except Nelrene.  She knew.
"I don't know why you hid it." Barnabas went on as if he'd paused for breath and nothing else. "And I don't care.  I'm going to find him." He removed the black face cover.  He was an ordinary looking man.  Brown eyes, lightly olive skin, and complexion that was neither clean nor ruddy.
He transformed, all those features receding for new ones.  His fur was brown like mud.  His eyes remained the same.  He was, even as a werewolf, extremely plain in appearance.  He changed back and reequipped his mask.
"You're all going to help me find our progenitor." Barnabas looked up, and the ship raised.  They all felt it.
The white room shifted, became transparent.  Now they were suspended above Destursha.  There were ships below, the moons were hidden behind the world, and they were moving away.  Cecil gaped, unable to protest, as he felt his magic drain away.
Whether or not they chose to help, they were going with him.
